viii
  A ttitudes to artiﬁ  cial intelligence (AI) remain remarkably polarized. 
For some it is a tool with extraordinary potential to make life 
easier in the future. As Alan Turing wrote of computation in general, 
‘This is only a foretaste of what is to come, and only a shadow of 
what is going to be. ’  1   Meanwhile for others, AI itself poses an 
existential threat to human civilisation. As the late Stephen Hawking 
warned us, ‘The development of full artiﬁ  cial intelligence could spell 
the end of the human race. ’  2   
 In this sense, AI is a two-edged sword. It has potential for both 
good and evil. AI poses a dilemma, and no one sums up this dilemma 
better than Elon Musk. On the one hand, Musk is himself developing 
AI-based tools for his commercial ventures, from the now well-
established Tesla car company to the still speculative Neuralink 
project. On the other hand, Musk is only too aware of the potential 
dangers it poses: ‘I think we should be very careful about artiﬁ  cial 
intelligence. If I were to guess like what our biggest existential threat 
is, it’s probably that. So we need to be very careful with the artiﬁ  cial 
intelligence . . . With artiﬁ  cial intelligence we are summoning the 
demon. ’  3   Stuart Russell faces a similar dilemma. Although he is one 
of the pioneers of AI, and co-author of the inﬂ  uential book,  Artiﬁ  cial 
Intelligence: A Modern Approach  , he has also commissioned an arms 
control video warning us of the dangers of ‘slaughterbots’, 
autonomous AI-controlled drones that can kill.  4   
 This series reﬂ  ects this paradox. This ﬁ  rst volume,  Architecture in 
the Age of Artiﬁ  cial Intelligence  , is a celebration of the positive 
contributions of AI. It outlines the extraordinary achievements of AI 
in the past, and speculates about the potential of AI to design 
buildings for us in the future. By contrast, the second volume,  The 
Death of the Architect  , exposes the potential dark side of AI, and    Preface  
PREFACE x
outlines the risks that it poses in terms of unemployment, surveillance 
and the loss of personal freedom. 
 These ﬁ  rst two volumes are intended as a dialectical foil to each 
other. There is no presumption that one should be more persuasive 
than the other. It is left to readers to form their own opinions, based 
on the arguments in both volumes. In some senses, however, the 
arguments are actually the same. They are just two sides of the same 
coin. Once AI is able to design buildings for us completely 
autonomously, will this not lead to the ‘Death of the Architect’? 
 This is the second edition of the ﬁ  rst volume, updated to reﬂ  ect 
the ever-evolving landscape of ideas and technological advances that 
are fuelling the AI design revolution, and helping us to generate even 
better designs. 
 Neil Leach, Venice Beach, California

               I n t r o d u c t i o n             
   A I is now very much part of our lives, even though some of us 
might not realise it. 
 AI identiﬁ  es our friends on Faceook, and categorises our images 
on Instagram. It feeds us news and advertisements based on our 
online histories. It ﬁ  lters our email spam. It suggests how we should 
end our sentences. It translates foreign texts. It opens our phones. It 
gives us advice on ChatGPT. It generates images for us on MidJourney. 
It feeds us music on Spotify, and movies on Netﬂ  ix. It even introduces 
us to our potential partners. 
 AI has colonised our homes in the form of Siri, Alexa, Cortana and 
Google Assistant. It monitors our heating, and checks the quality of 
our water.  1   It controls robots that clean our ﬂ  oors. It pays our bills, 
helps us with our taxes, reminds us of our appointments, and 
schedules our meetings. In some cases it allows us to pay for our 
goods through facial recognition, and to even board our planes. It is 
embedded throughout the fabric of our cities, monitoring them and 
making them more efﬁ  cient and sustainable. 
 AI also warns us, when driving, if we stray out of lane. It controls 
self-driving cars, buses and trucks. It pays our tolls with transponders 
and vehicle identiﬁ  cation systems. It shows us the fastest route and 
tells us where to park.  2   It is transforming how we shop, order rides 
and organise our lives. 
 All of a sudden, AI is everywhere. 
 But it is not as though we even notice it. AI does not look like a 
robot. In fact it does not look like anything at all. With AI, think 
algorithms, not robots. AI is completely invisible. AI controls 
the environment around us. Even though we might not even realise 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 2
it, AI has already inﬁ  ltrated our lives. And its inﬂ  uence is growing 
every day. 
 It is as though the Earth has been invaded by an invisible, super 
super-intelligent, alien species.  3    
   AI and architecture  
 What impact will AI have on architecture? 
 There are several areas where AI is already having a major impact 
on architectural culture. Its most noticeable impact has been in 
academic circles, where AI has burst onto the scene in design studios 
in most progressive schools of architecture. Although the most 
 FIGURE  0.1 David Alf, Coastal Compositions, (2024). House nestled in 
the dunes, generated using MidJourney V6.0. 
INTRODUCTION 3
popular AI techniques were discovered only recently, interest in them 
has begun to spread like wildﬁ  re. Inspirational images of buildings 
and urban design proposals are now being generated automatically 
based on prompts, and architects are becoming increasingly 
competent ‘prompt engineers’. AI-generated work is starting to 
attract considerable interest, while workshops and tutorials 
for generating AI-based designs have also become immensely 
popular.  4   
 ‘It is as though the Earth has 
been invaded by an invisible, 
super intelligent, alien species.’ 
 AI has also become a hot topic in academic research conferences. 
It has become a popular ﬁ  eld for doctoral research, especially in 
technologically advanced institutions. Several academic courses and 
even programmes now focus speciﬁ  cally on AI.  5   Within a few years 
we can expect AI-based courses in every single discipline. 
 AI is also having a major impact in the world of galleries 
and exhibitions. The ﬁ  rst exhibition of AI-generated artworks 
was held in 2016, and the ﬁ  rst auction of an AI-generated artwork in 
2018. And architecture is not far behind. The ﬁ  rst exhibition of 
AI-generated architectural designs was held in 2019. There has 
been considerable interest in AI-generated work online, with 
regular postings on Instagram, Facebook, WeChat and other social 
media platforms. 
 AI is also starting to have an impact on architectural practices. An 
increasing number of high-proﬁ  le architects are starting to incorporate 
new AI-based techniques into their design strategies. Wolf Prix of 
Coop Himmelb(l)au has been exploring the potential of AI to improve 
the design process. Meanwhile Patrik Schumacher of Zaha Hadid 
Architects (ZHA) has used it primarily – but not exclusively – to 
generate ideas for competitions. Meanwhile, other high-proﬁ  le 
architectural practices, such as Foster + Partners, are using it in a 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 4
very practical way to enhance and speed up their design procedures. 
AI is spreading rapidly through architectural practice. 
 AI is already embedded in standard architectural software, 
although most architects are probably not aware of it. Established 
commercial companies, such as Autodesk, are now beginning 
to integrate advanced AI into their software and have acquired 
software developers, such as SpaceMaker, now renamed Forma, 
while XKool has been developing even more advanced AI-based 
software tools for architects and developers. AI is proving capable 
of processing complex site information, and offering a greater range 
of design options, maximising the potential of any given site. 
In addition, AI is now being used to control robotic fabrication 
technologies and to improve the performance of buildings once 
constructed. 
 FIGURE  0.2 Hera Kim, Blue and White Ceramics Pavilion, generated 
using MidJourney (2023). This pavilion is a blend of Oriental and 
European infl  uences, showcasing a rich cross-cultural history. 
INTRODUCTION 5
 And yet we are still in the early stages of the AI revolution. Ben 
Bratton has described how we are still in the ‘silent movies’ period of 
computation, and AI is but a recent development within the longer 
history of computation. There is a lot more to come. AI is certain to 
become an integral part of our future, and will undoubtedly prove to 
be a powerful aid in augmenting human design abilities and speeding 
up the design process. The writing is on the wall. 
 By the end of this decade, we could predict that there will not be 
a single profession or discipline left untouched by AI. And architecture 
will be no exception. Thanks to AI, the very practice of architectural 
design will be completely overhauled, and so will architectural 
education.  
   Beyond technophobia  
 Not everyone is so positive about the introduction of AI into 
architecture. Some dismiss it, just as some dismissed computers 
when ﬁ  rst introduced into mainstream architectural culture some 
thirty years ago. Why use computers, some would ask, when human 
beings can draw so much better? Why use artiﬁ  cial intelligence, 
others would also no doubt ask, when we have human intelligence? 
Equally, others would be even more critical, dismissing AI as part of 
a ‘New Dark Age’, or a vital cog in ‘surveillance capitalism’.  6   
 This is perhaps understandable. After all, how often do we see 
robots and computers portrayed in a negative light in popular culture? 
From  Metropolis   onwards, many movies cast robots as evil artiﬁ  cial 
life forms that pose a threat to humankind. Take  2001: A Space 
Odyssey  , where Hal, the sentient supercomputer controlling a space 
ﬂ  ight to Jupiter, eventually turns against the astronauts on board. Or 
take the  Terminator   movies, where Skynet, the often invisible, super-
intelligent AI system, plays the main antagonist. Or take  Blade 
Runner  , where bio-engineered robots inﬁ  ltrate their way seamlessly 
into human society, causing mayhem. 
 This distrust of robots and computers in the movies can be 
understood within the broader context of technophobia in general 
that can also be traced back to some of the more conservative 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 6
approaches in philosophy that still inform architectural theory. One 
problem with philosophy, as Stephen Hawking famously observes, is 
that too often it fails to keep pace with technology.  7   But another 
problem with philosophy is that it is often antagonistic towards 
technology. 
 Martin Heidegger, for example, saw technology as a potential 
source of alienation. He failed to appreciate, however, the gradual 
way in which we appropriate new technologies through a form of 
proprioception.  8   Think of the outrage caused by Le Corbusier’s 
famous description of the house as ‘a machine for living in’. But do 
we not now live in houses bristling with technological devices of 
every kind? The same goes for engineering. Take the Eiffel Tower. 
When ﬁ  rst constructed, many Parisians regarded it as an unspeakably 
ugly ediﬁ  ce. Indeed, Guy de Maupassant would frequently take his 
lunch at a restaurant in the Eiffel Tower, even though he was not so 
fond of the food there, on the basis that it was the only place in Paris 
from which he could not see the tower.  9   And yet the Eiffel Tower has 
now become a much-loved symbol of Paris. Heidegger’s anti-
technological stance now seems radically out of place in a world 
where technology has colonised our horizon of consciousness, where 
we not only accept technology, but even start connecting with it from 
an emotional perspective. How long is it before we become attached 
to our laptops, and give our cars names?  10   Isn’t time to  forget   
Heidegger?  11    
   The prosthetic imagination  
 For a more nuanced philosophical understanding of how we adapt to 
technology, and absorb it so that it becomes a prosthetic extension to 
our own bodies, we should perhaps turn to Maurice Merleau-Ponty, 
who recognises that any tool can eventually be appropriated as part 
of our extended body schema, so that we come to experience the 
world  through   our tools.  12   Think of a blind person who has to navigate 
the world using a white cane. Eventually the cane becomes ‘invisible’ 
as it is absorbed into the extended sense of the embodied self.  13   T h e  
same happens with cars. With time we become so familiar with the 
operations of driving – accelerating, braking, steering and so on – that 
INTRODUCTION 7
they fade into the background to become part of our subconscious 
such that we come to drive  through   our cars. And something similar 
happens with the spaces we inhabit. The initial sense of displacement 
or alienation that we might feel when ﬁ  rst moving into a new 
apartment or house gradually fades away, and over time we become 
attached to it and start to feel at home there.  14   
 Andy Clark and David Chalmers take this idea even further and 
argue that a tool can even become part of our ‘extended mind’.  15   T a k e  
the mobile phone. Instead of memorising phone numbers, we now 
keep those numbers on our mobile phone. Our social contacts are 
stored there. Our whole lives are contained there. Our ‘cellphones’ 
have become our ‘self-phones’. We have absorbed our mobile phones 
and other external devices at a symbolic level, so that they have 
become part of who we are. They have become prosthetic extensions 
of our minds. 
 Katherine Hayles then goes one step further still. Why limit 
ourselves to the human mind, when the whole body has been 
augmented by the increasing introduction of these new technologies? 
For Hayles, we cannot separate the mind from the body, as though 
the mind is simply housed in the body.  16   The mind is precisely part of 
the body. Moreover, as Hayles points out, the body has indeed been 
extended by these new technologies, such that the traditional 
concept of ‘humanism’ – the notion that the human body can be 
perceived as a discrete unit in and of itself – has now become 
untenable. For Hayles, then, we need to go so far as to challenge the 
traditional liberal concept of humanism and accept that we now 
operate within a ‘posthuman condition’.  17    
   Cyborg culture  
 The term ‘cyborg’ was ﬁ  rst coined to refer to the potential of 
enhancing the human body in order for it to survive in extraterrestrial 
environments.  18   More recently, however, it has been deployed by 
Donna Haraway to refer to a cybernetic organism, ‘a hybrid of machine 
and organism, a creature of social reality as well as a creature of 
ﬁ  ction’.  19   Moreover, this hybrid condition should be understood not as 
static, but as one that is continually adapting and mutating: ‘ Already 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 8
in the few decades that they have existed, they have mutated, in fact 
and ﬁ  ction, into second-order entities like genomic and electronic 
databases and the other denizens of the zone called cyberspaces. ’  20   
As such, we have now evolved, so that the cyborg has become our 
predominant disposition: ‘By the late twentieth century, our time, a 
mythic time, we are all chimeras, theorized and fabricated hybrids of 
machine and organism; in short, we are cyborgs. ’  21   
 Although this notion of the cyborg might conjure up images of 
technologically enhanced human bodies, such as Terminator or Eve 8, 
a cyborg does not actually depend on advanced technology. Forget 
the romanticised notion of the cyborg – half human, half robot – that 
we see in the movies. A cyborg is simply a creature that uses any 
form of prosthesis. An ordinary walking stick is a prosthesis.  22   A s  
Clark comments: 
  The cyborg is a potent cultural icon of the late twentieth century. 
It conjures images of human–machine hybrids and the physical 
merging of ﬂ  esh and electronic circuitry. My goal is to hijack that 
image and to reshape it, revealing it as a disguised vision of (oddly) 
our own biological nature. For what is special about human brains, 
and what best explains the distinctive features of human 
intelligence, is precisely their ability to enter into deep and complex 
relationships with nonbiological constructs, props, and aids.  
 The usefulness of these ‘constructs, props and aids’, however, is 
determined by their affordances – their potential uses – which remain 
constrained by our own capabilities. A tool, after all, is no use, if we 
cannot use it. Likewise a tool might ‘ lend itself  ’ to certain operations, 
but not to others.  23   A tool, however, has no agency.  24   It cannot force 
us to use it in a certain way. Nonetheless, with an appropriate tool, 
the capacity of human beings can be enhanced considerably. 
 As such, as Elon Musk has commented, ‘ All of us already are 
cyborgs. ’  25   It is, moreover, our increasing reliance on sophisticated 
digital prostheses that is making us ever more cyborg-like.  26   These 
digital prostheses are enhancing our abilities, such that they have 
begun to make us ‘superhuman’. Musk again: ‘You have a machine 
extension of yourself in the form of your phone, and your computer 
and all your applications. You are already superhuman. ’  27   
INTRODUCTION 9
 It is precisely our capacity to adapt so effectively to new tools that, 
for Clark, makes human beings ‘natural born cyborgs’.  28   We are 
naturally adaptive creatures, because our brains are themselves so 
adaptive. Neuroscientists tell us that the human brain is ‘plastic’.  29   
The brain has the capacity to adapt constantly to everchanging 
circumstances, and it is its very ‘plasticity’ that gives human beings 
the capacity to appropriate new technologies so that they become 
part of our extended sense of self: ‘It is the presence of this unusual 
plasticity that makes humans (but not dogs, cats, or elephants) 
natural born cyborgs: beings primed by Mother Nature to annex wave 
upon wave of external elements and structures as part and parcel of 
their own extended minds. ’  30   
 Moreover, for Clark, this is why cyborg culture does not make us 
post-human, but rather afﬁ  rms us as being quintessentially human, in 
that the innate capacity to adapt plays such a signiﬁ  cant role in what 
it is to be human. This, then, allows Clark to challenge Hayles’s claim 
that we have become post-human: ‘Such extensions should not be 
thought of as rendering us in any way post-human; not because they 
are not deeply transformative but because we humans are naturally 
designed to be the subjects of just such repeated transformations!’  31    
   Extended intelligence  
 It is in the context of this burgeoning ‘cyborg culture’ – where humans 
have been augmented to become superhuman – that we can explore 
the full potential of AI as being not an end in itself, but a prosthetic 
device that can enhance the natural intelligence of the human being. 
For if the mind – or indeed the embodied self – can be extended 
through the phone and other devices, could we not also argue that 
intelligence itself can be enhanced through artiﬁ  cial intelligence? 
 Increasingly nowadays we are hearing references not to 
straightforward ‘artiﬁ  cial intelligence’ but to ‘extended intelligence’ 
(EI). Ultimately, the most productive strategy is to see the relationship 
between AI and human intelligence not as one of competition, but 
rather as a potential synergy between the two, whereby AI operates 
in tandem with human intelligence and becomes an  extension   to 
human intelligence. As Joi Ito puts it: 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 10
  Instead of thinking about machine intelligence in terms of humans 
vs. machines, we should consider the system that integrates 
humans and machines – not artiﬁ  cial intelligence, but extended 
intelligence. Instead of trying to control or design or even 
understand systems, it is more important to design systems that 
participate as responsible, aware and robust elements of even 
more complex systems. And we must question and adapt our own 
purpose and sensibilities as designers and components of the 
system for a much more humble approach: Humility over Control. 
We could call it ‘participant design’ – design of systems as and by 
participants – that is more akin to the increase of a ﬂ  ourishing 
function, where ﬂ  ourishing is a measure of vigor and health rather 
than scale or power. We can measure the ability for systems to 
adapt creatively, as well as their resilience and their ability to use 
resources in an interesting way.  32    
 An alternative way of understanding this coupling of human and AI is 
as a form of ‘intelligence augmentation (IA)’. As Anant Jhingran 
comments, ‘ AI makes machines autonomous and detached from 
humans; IA, on the other hand, puts humans in control and leverages 
computing power to amplify our capabilities. ’  33   Whether we call it 
‘extended intelligence’ or ‘intelligence augmentation’, the basic 
concept of coupling human intelligence with AI remains the same. 
As such, we should not be referring to AI in isolation, so much as a 
potential extension or augmentation of human intelligence. This 
extended or augmented intelligence – this synergy between our 
bodies and machines – will undoubtedly prove enormously productive. 
As Yann Lecun observes, ‘Our intelligence is what makes us smarter, 
and AI is an extension of that quality. ’  34    
   An introduction to AI  
 This is an introductory book outlining what AI can contribute to the 
discipline of architecture. As such, it does not go into any great detail. 
Rather it offers an overview of what is a fascinating and increasingly 
relevant topic. It tries to describe in a few broad brushstrokes how AI 
operates, how it has developed and what potential it holds for the 
INTRODUCTION 11
future of architecture. It should be noted, however, that this book is 
no technical manual. It does not offer any technical instructions as to 
how to use AI.  35   Nor does it attempt to provide a comprehensive 
account of AI. Indeed, although it does touch on questions of 
performance, it is preoccupied largely with a relatively narrow range 
of popular AI techniques being used by some architects to generate 
some extraordinary design work at the moment. It therefore privileges 
the aesthetic potential of AI to produce visually stimulating images 
over performance-based considerations. This is where AI is arguably 
having its most signiﬁ  cant impact at the moment. In the future, 
however, the success of AI is likely to be judged in a very different 
way: by its capacity to calculate the most efﬁ  cient solution to a design 
challenge from the perspective of performance. 
 FIGURE  0.3 Ross Lovegrove and Ila Colombo, The artistry of parametric 
design, generated using MidJourney (2023). Inspired by the harmonious 
complexity of natural structures, these hand gloves not only fi  t the 
contours of the hand but also delineate the lines of advanced biomorphism. 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 12
 Chapter 1, ‘What is AI?’, is the most technical chapter. It introduces 
the basic principles of AI. It makes a series important distinctions 
between the different forms of AI and offers deﬁ  nitions of a number of 
key terms. It addresses the development of deep learning and the use of 
neural networks. It goes on to also look in more detail at Large Language 
Models (LLMs) and Chatbots, such as ChatGPT, which are among the 
most signiﬁ  cant developments in the ﬁ  eld of AI in recent times. 
 Chapter 2, ‘ A brief history of AI’, is more historical in its orientation. 
It offers a brief background history of AI, focusing in particular on the 
visibility and invisibility of AI. It introduces some of the most important 
ﬁ  gures in the history of AI, such as Alan Turing, whose once secret 
and little-known contributions to computation have now been 
recognised and celebrated in popular culture. It highlights both the 
low points in its history – the so-called AI ‘winters’ – but also the 
high-proﬁ  le media events, such as matches of Chess and Go, that 
helped to raise awareness of AI. It then looks at how the launch of 
ChatGPT brought AI to worldwide attention. 
 Chapter 3, ‘Image generation’, traces the development of 
techniques for image generation. Tracing their origin back to image 
recognition, it focuses on DeepDream and generative adversarial 
networks (GANs) in particular. It then goes on to look at diffusion 
models, such as DALL -E, MidJourney and LookX, which have now 
revolutionised the realm of image generation. The chapter goes on to 
speculate whether these tools might have reconﬁ  gured the entire 
design process, and asks whether some of these diffusion models 
have learnt to design. It then goes on to text to video models, and 
tools for generating 3D forms from 2D images. Finally it looks at 
performance-based AI design tools. 
 Chapter 4, ‘ AI, art and creativity’, focuses on the realm of art, and 
examines the capacity for AI to be ‘creative’. It looks at the impact of 
generative AI techniques on the ﬁ  eld of art, especially at a moment 
when AI artworks have not only been displayed at art exhibitions and 
sold at auctions, but have also won international art prizes. But this 
chapter also asks some searching questions. Is AI-generated art 
really art? Who owns the copyright of AI-generated art? Does AI 
contravene copyright regulations? And what does AI tell us about 
creativity? Is it even time to rethink our understanding of human 
creativity in the light of AI? 
INTRODUCTION 13
 Chapter 5, ‘ AI, media art and neuroscience’, asks whether 
machines are able to ‘dream’ or ‘hallucinate’. It highlights the 
importance of the moment when Reﬁ  k Anadol projected his machine 
hallucinations onto Frank Gehry’s Walt Disney Concert Hall in Los 
Angeles, and compares these machine hallucinations with the theory 
of ‘controlled hallucinations’ developed recently in the ﬁ  eld of 
neuroscience. The chapter goes on to consider what insights AI and 
neuroscience can offer us into the way that architects think and see 
the world. It concludes by asking whether AI could become a mirror 
in which to understand what it is to be human. 
 Chapter 6, ‘ AI and architecture’, eventually addresses architectural 
design itself. It looks at the recent explosion of interest in AI within 
architectural design circles, and considers how AI is beginning to 
bring fresh approaches to architectural design. In particular, the 
chapter focuses on the different approaches that progressive 
architects, such as Wolf Prix (Coop Himmelb(l)au) and Patrik 
Schumacher (ZHA), are taking towards AI in their design practices. It 
also looks at how Foster + Partners have used AI in a very different 
way to enhance and speed up design production. It concludes that, 
whatever the approach of the ofﬁ  ce, the architect of the future will be 
augmented signiﬁ  cantly by AI. 
 Chapter 7 , ‘The future of the architectural ofﬁ  ce’, considers how 
the architectural ofﬁ  ce of the future will engage with AI. Here the 
focus is less on experimentation and more on practical applications, 
less on aesthetics and more on questions of performance. The 
chapter engages with two of the leading forms of AI-based 
architectural software, XKool and Forma (formerly Spacemaker), and 
compares their different approaches. It argues that in terms of the 
introduction of AI into the architectural ofﬁ  ce, the crucial turning point 
will come when clients start to insist that their architects use AI. The 
chapter concludes by considering the role of AI in the fabrication of 
buildings. 
 Chapter 8, ‘Brain city’, speculates on the potential impact of AI on 
the future of our cities. It argues that the key driver of change is less 
likely to be the quest for novel architectural forms, than the introduction 
of AI-based informational technologies. AI will begin to inform not 
only how cities are designed but also how they operate. Cities will 
become informational cities, and AI will operate as a form of ‘brain’ to 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 14
allow them to operate more efﬁ  ciently. The city of the future will 
become an intelligent, efﬁ  cient city – a ‘Brain city’, an AI-enhanced 
version of the city of today. 
 The ﬁ  nal chapter, ‘The future of AI’, speculates about the future of 
AI, and offers an overview of predictions made about the potential of 
AI. Will AI ever be able to match or even surpass human intelligence? 
Could we speculate it might have done so already? And could we 
even claim that it has learnt how to think? The book concludes with a 
list of predictions about the impact of AI on the profession of 
architecture. It argues that over time AI will become an indispensable 
tool in the architectural ofﬁ  ce, and that it will grow in its capabilities, 
until eventually it will be capable of designing buildings all on its own, 
and the full potential of AI will have been realised.  
   Intended audience  
 This book is aimed primarily, but not exclusively, at a younger 
generation – especially students of architecture. After all, this is the 
generation that can relate most to AI, because AI is already so much 
part of their world. This is the generation whose social lives depend 
upon AI, a generation that uses ChatGPT, MidJourney, TikTok and 
Spotify, and a generation that has absorbed AI into almost every 
aspect of their existence. Moreover, this is a generation for whom the 
real contributions of AI will kick in as they approach the peak of their 
careers. However, this book is also aimed at architects already in 
practice, offering them some insights into the kinds of tools that are 
now being developed, and into the changes that are likely to occur in 
architectural practice in the future. Finally, it is also aimed at the general 
public and anyone else interested in the creative potential of AI. 
 This is a timely book. AI is a hot topic. And new apps, new design 
techniques, are being developed every day. This is the second edition 
of this book, but it will need to be constantly re-edited – updated – 
just as our technical devices are themselves constantly being 
updated. But at least it is a start. And perhaps its greatest contribution 
will be to help to initiate a debate about the potential impact of AI on 
the world of architecture. And, for sure, it will produce a counter-
reaction. Many are already deeply sceptical of AI. 
INTRODUCTION 15
 This series, however, anticipates this counter-reaction. This ﬁ  rst 
volume is largely positive about AI, and argues that AI will eventually 
be able to design buildings. The second volume, by contrast, looks at 
the dark side of AI. Among other concerns, it argues that inevitably 
AI might lead to the death of architecture, a profession that is already 
struggling. 
 Future generations, no doubt, will look back at these two editions 
of this publication and see them as offering time capsules of important 
ideas from precise moments in time in the third decade of the twenty-
ﬁ  rst century, when architectural culture was beginning to embrace 
the possibilities afforded by AI.       
 David Alf, Equestrian Retreat (2024). Courtyard with horse, generated using

               1  
 W h a t  i s  A I ?             
   A  common deﬁ  nition of AI is that it seeks to mimic or simulate 
the intelligence of the human mind. As Margaret Boden puts it, 
‘[Research in] AI seeks to make computers do the sorts of things that 
minds can do. ’  1   Meanwhile John Kelleher describes AI as ‘that ﬁ  eld of 
research that is focused on developing computational systems that 
can perform tasks and activities normally considered to require 
human intelligence’.  2   The implication here is that AI might soon be 
able to take over these tasks and activities.  3   
 It could be claimed, however, that in certain speciﬁ  c domains AI 
already surpasses human intelligence. For example, the time has 
long gone since human beings might expect to beat AI at a game of 
chess or even Go. Meanwhile ChatGPT, we are told, already knows 
ten thousand times more than any one human being.  4    
   Narrow AI  
 When deﬁ  ning AI, it is necessary to draw up a series of distinctions. 
First and foremost, although the term ‘intelligence’ is used for both 
human beings and machines, we must be careful to distinguish AI 
from human intelligence. For the moment, AI does not possess 
consciousness.  5   It is neither sentient nor self-aware – as far as we 
know. This is important to recognise. For example, AI might be 
capable of beating humans at a game of chess or Go, but this does 
not mean that AI is  aware   that it is playing a game of chess or Go. 
 At present, then, we are still limited to a relatively modest realm of 
AI that is known as ‘narrow AI’, which – as its name implies – is 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 20
narrow and circumscribed in its potential. ‘Narrow AI’ – also known 
as ‘weak AI’ – needs to be distinguished from ‘strong AI’, which is AI 
with consciousness. At the moment, ‘strong AI’ still remains some 
way off. The only ‘examples’ of ‘strong AI’ at the moment are pure 
ﬁ  ction, such as characters from the world of the cinema, like Agent 
Smith in  The Matrix   or Ava in  Ex Machina  . 
 Classic examples of ‘narrow AI’ would include chatbots, such as 
ChatGPT, and AI assistants, such as Siri, Alexa and Cortona. However 
sophisticated these might appear, it is generally assumed that they 
cannot think for themselves any more than a pocket calculator can 
think, and are incapable of undertaking any activity that requires 
consciousness.  6    
   The diff  erent forms of AI  
 The term ‘ AI’ is often used as though it is a singular, homogeneous 
category. Indeed, this is how the general public understands 
the term. However, there are in fact many different forms of AI, and 
even these can be further divided into a series of sub-categories. In 
 FIGURE  1.1 Diagram of the relationship between deep learning, machine 
learning and AI. This Venn diagram illustrates how deep learning is 
nested inside machine learning that is itself nested within the broader 
category of AI. 
WHAT IS AI? 21
order to understand AI, then, we need to differentiate the various 
forms of AI. 
 Within ‘narrow AI’ we should make a further distinction between 
the broader category of AI itself, ‘machine learning’ and ‘deep 
learning’. These three can be seen to be nested within each other – 
somewhat like a series of Russian dolls, or layers in an onion – in that 
‘deep learning’ is part of ‘machine learning’ that is itself part of AI. 
Early versions of AI referred to machines that had to be  programmed   
to process a set of data. This is also known as ‘Classical AI’, or even, 
somewhat disparagingly, ‘Good Old Fashioned AI (GOFAI)’. The 
important point here is that with early AI, the machine could only do 
what it was programmed to do. The machine itself could not learn. 
 By contrast, machine learning goes one step further, and is able to 
train itself using vast quantities of data. Importantly, machine learning 
challenges the popular myth that computers cannot do anything that 
they have not been programmed to do. As the term implies, machine 
learning is able to  learn  , and even programme itself, although we 
should be careful not to conﬂ  ate the way that machines ‘learn’ with 
human learning. Like other terms used for both AI and human 
intelligence, ‘learning’ does not necessarily have the same meaning 
in both contexts. 
 Stuart Russell comments: ‘Learning is a key ability for modern 
artiﬁ  cial intelligence. Machine learning has always been a subﬁ  eld of 
AI, and it simply means improving your ability to do the right thing, as 
a result of experience. ’  7   Furthermore, learning is essential if AI is ever 
going to match human intelligence. As Pedro Domingos observes, 
‘The goal of AI is to teach computers to do what humans currently do 
better, and learning is arguably the most important of those things: 
without it, no computer can keep up with a human for long; with it, 
the rest follows. ’  8   
 Deep learning is a relatively recent development within machine 
learning and has led to many signiﬁ  cant advances in the ﬁ  eld of AI. It 
is deep learning that is, for now at least, the most promising form of 
AI. In fact deep learning has become the dominant paradigm to the 
point that – somewhat misleadingly – it has become almost 
synonymous with AI, at least within the popular media.  9   Indeed, 
whenever AI is mentioned in this book, it is invariably deep learning 
that is being referred to, and certainly not GOFAI. Importantly, 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 22
however, deep learning depends on a vast amount of data, so much 
so that data, as is often said, has now become ‘the new oil’. 
 Kelleher deﬁ  nes deep learning as ‘the subﬁ  eld of machine learning 
that designs and evaluates training algorithms and architectures for 
modern neural network models’.  10   Here we come to an important 
point regarding the term ‘architecture’. Confusingly, ‘architecture’ 
is also used within computer science to refer to the internal 
organisation of a computer.  11   This has nothing to do, however, 
with the term ‘architecture’ as used in the context of buildings. 
Readers should therefore be careful not to confuse the ‘architecture’ 
of computer science with the ‘architecture’ of the construction 
industry. 
 In short, the differences between early neural networks and more 
recent neural networks used in deep learning should not be 
underestimated. There is an enormous gulf between these two in 
terms of their performance and capabilities. The difference is even 
more extreme, perhaps, than that between early cars – once known 
as ‘horseless carriages’ – and the sophisticated, potentially self-
driving cars of today.  
   Training techniques  
 Further, we need to distinguish the three primary training techniques 
used in machine learning: supervised, unsupervised and reinforcement 
learning. 
 With supervised learning, the system is literally trained to perform 
certain tasks according to a desired outcome by being fed a vast 
quantity of clearly identiﬁ  ed examples. Boden offers a helpful 
deﬁ  nition: ‘In supervised learning, the programmer “ trains  ” the 
system by deﬁ  ning a set of desired outcomes for a range of inputs 
(labeled examples and non-examples), and providing continual 
feedback about whether it has achieved them. ’  12   
 Thus, for example, many images – over 1 million in some cases – 
need to be fed in, and labelled or ‘classiﬁ  ed’ as the process is 
sometimes called. In the case of images of cats, these would need 
to be labelled ‘cat’. Likewise, a vast number of images that are not of 
cats would also need to be labelled ‘no cat’. 
WHAT IS AI? 23
 This is not so dissimilar to how human beings learn. Obvious 
comparisons can be made with the process by which a parent teaches 
a young child to identify various objects, such as animals: ‘This is a 
cow’, ‘This is a horse’, and so on. Similarly, comparisons could be 
made with architectural education, where students are taught to 
identify various features and attributes of architectural design. 
 With unsupervised learning, there are no desired outcomes. 
Rather, the system ﬁ  nds patterns or clusters that exist in unstructured 
data, thereby effectively  discovering   knowledge. Kelleher deﬁ  nes 
unsupervised learning as ‘a form of machine learning where the task 
is to identify irregularities, such as clusters of similar instances, in the 
data. Unlike supervised learning, there is no target attribute in an 
unsupervised learning task. ’  13   This is one of the most challenging but 
equally promising areas of machine learning. Indeed, if machines 
could truly learn by themselves without human intervention, this 
would be a major step towards the possibility of artiﬁ  cial general 
intelligence (AGI) – AI, that is, that possesses consciousness and can 
genuinely think. 
 Again, this is not dissimilar to how children learn a language, 
simply by listening to others speaking.  14   Indeed, the human mind is 
good at learning through an unsupervised interaction with the 
environment. We could also compare it to the way in which architects 
and architectural students absorb a certain design sensibility, simply 
by being immersed within an architectural environment. After all, 
architectural design is seldom taught according to any overarching 
theory or set of principles. It is as though architects and architectural 
students are expected to acquire an understanding of what constitutes 
good design almost by osmosis. 
 There are also other modalities of learning that are blends of both 
supervised and unsupervised learning. Examples would include self-
supervised learning and semi-supervised learning. Like unsupervised 
learning, self-supervised learning does not depend on labelled data, 
but learns supervisory signals, that are then used to label data 
automatically further down the pipeline. The process could be 
described as a form of ‘self-labelling’. Unlike unsupervised learning, 
however, it does not operate by identifying irregularities, such as 
clustering.  15   Semi-supervised learning, by contrast, combines a small 
amount of labelled data with a large amount of unlabelled data from 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 24
the outset. This could be compared to the situation in a school 
classroom, where the teacher offers an example, showing students 
how to solve a problem, and then leaves it up to the students to solve 
the remaining problems. Both processes effectively reduce costs, by 
reducing dependence on expensive labelled data. 
 Finally, with reinforcement learning, the system does not need to 
be given clearly labelled examples in order to learn, but depends 
entirely on feedback messages informing it whether it is correct or 
not. Its knowledge evolves based on the logic of punishment or 
reward. This is particularly effective in game playing and robotic 
control, where a game process may be repeated in simulation several 
million times at a remarkably rapid rate.  16   
 Reinforcement learning is not so dissimilar to training a dog, by 
giving it rewards when it obeys commands. Indeed, a version of 
reinforcement learning is also at work in human learning, not least in 
the training and development of an architect, in that both academia 
and practice are based on a rewards system involving scholarships, 
awards and prizes.  
   AI tribes  
 Approaches to machine learning can be divided into ﬁ  ve main 
schools of thought: symbolists, connectionists, evolutionaries, 
Bayesians and analogisers. Pedro Domingos refers to these different 
approaches as ‘tribes’, a term that is perhaps not so inappropriate, 
given the often mutually exclusive, competitive nature of research in 
this ﬁ  eld.  17   
 Evolutionaries put their trust in genetic programming that improves 
over successive generations much like natural selection itself.  18   
Bayesians believe in the principle of probabilistic inference to 
overcome the problem of noise and incomplete information.  19   
Analogisers subscribe to the logic of analogy and use that logic to 
recognise and learn from similarities.  20   However, the most signiﬁ  cant 
‘tribes’ in terms of the history of AI in general are the symbolists and 
the connectionists. 
 Symbolists believe in solving problems through inverse deduction, 
by using existing knowledge and identifying what further knowledge 
WHAT IS AI? 25
might be needed to make a deduction: ‘For symbolists, all intelligence 
can be reduced to manipulating symbols, in the same way that a 
mathematician solves equations by replacing expressions by other 
expressions. ’  21   
 Connectionists, meanwhile, believe in neural networks, and model 
them loosely on the brain. They also attempt to reverse engineer 
what the brain does, so as to align a system’s output with the desired 
response.  22   As Domingos observes, ‘For connectionists, learning is 
what the brain does, and so what we need to do is to reverse engineer 
it. The brain learns by adjusting the strengths of connections between 
neurons, and the crucial problem is ﬁ  guring out which connections 
are to blame for which errors and changing them accordingly. ’  23   Not 
surprisingly, then, it is connectionism that would seem to offer us the 
best insights into how the human brain works. 
 For someone from outside the ﬁ  eld, it might seem curious that 
there could be such a multiplicity of different approaches. And yet 
there has always been one dominant approach. For many years it 
was symbolism, but with the development of more sophisticated 
forms of neural networks and the emergence of deep learning, 
made possible by the introduction of Graphics Processing Units 
(GPUs), connectionism has now asserted itself as the dominant 
paradigm.  24    
 FIGURE  1.2 Diagram of a neural network. A neural network processes 
an image in feed forward direction with its behaviour characterised by the 
strengths of the synapses between the layers of neurons. 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 26
   Neural networks  
 Neural networks are composed of information processing units that 
are called ‘neurons’, and connections that control the ﬂ  ow of 
information between these units that are called ‘synapses’.  25   Ethem 
Alpaydin deﬁ  nes the neural network as ‘[a] model composed of a 
network of simple units called neurons and connections between 
neurons called synapses. Each synapse has a direction and a weight, 
and the weight deﬁ  nes the effect of the neuron before on the neuron 
after. ’  26   
 The neural networks used in connectionist AI should be 
distinguished from the virtual machines used in symbolic AI. They 
operate in parallel, are self-organising and can work without expert 
knowledge of task or domain. As Boden puts it, ‘Sequential 
instructions are replaced by massive parallelism, top-down control by 
bottom-up processing, and logic by probability. ’  27   They need to be 
trained by being fed a series of input–output pairs as training 
examples. The system then ‘learns’ over a period of time and tries to 
ﬁ  nd the optimal weighting for each connection, so that when fed an 
input the output matches – as far as possible – the training examples. 
Nonetheless, they are robust, good at discerning patterns – even 
when incomplete – and can deal with ‘messy’ evidence.  28   Think of 
how you can continue a tune, based only on the ﬁ  rst few notes. 
 The simplest way for a neural network to process an image is to 
operate in one direction, known as ‘feed forward’. A network consists 
of an ‘input layer’, an ‘output layer’ and – in between – multiple 
internal layers known as ‘hidden layers’. Each layer consists of 
simulated neurons. These neurons each ‘compute’ their input based 
on the ‘weight’ of the input’s connection, applying a threshold value 
to determine its ‘activation value’. In so doing, each neuron extracts 
and ﬁ  lters out certain ‘features’, before passing on its activation value 
to neurons in the next layer.  29   Each subsequent layer computes 
progressively higher level features, until a classiﬁ  cation output is 
generated based on its probability of being correct. 
 Neural networks are named after the neurons in the human brain. 
However, although certain comparisons can be drawn between 
neural networks and the brain, a clear distinction should be made 
between the ‘neurons’ in neural networks and the neurons in the 
WHAT IS AI? 27
brain. Certainly neural networks are nowhere near as sophisticated 
as the human brain. Multiple as the layers of neural networks are in 
deep learning, they are not as numerous as the countless networks 
in the human brain. Neural networks are therefore not so much 
modelled on the human brain, as  inspired   by it. As Yoshua Bengio 
comments, ‘While machine learning is trying to put knowledge into 
computers by allowing computers to learn from examples, deep 
learning is doing it in a way that is inspired by the brain. ’  30   
 Melanie Mitchell prefers to use the term ‘unit’ rather than ‘neuron’, 
since a simulated neuron bears so little resemblance to an actual 
neuron in the brain. Mitchell sums up the process as follows: 
  To process an image . . . the network performs its computation 
layer by layer, from left to right. Each hidden unit computes its 
activation value; these activation values then become the inputs 
for the output units, which then compute their own activations . . . 
The activation of an output unit can be thought of as the network’s 
conﬁ  dence that it is ‘seeing’ the corresponding digit; the digit 
category with the highest conﬁ  dence can be taken as the 
network’s answer – its classiﬁ  cation.  31     
   Backpropagation  
 Deep learning depends on ‘backpropagation’ – sometimes called 
‘backprop’. This allows a neural network to effectively operate in 
reverse in order to correct earlier prediction errors. Backpropagation 
refers to a process whereby information about the prediction error 
propagates backwards through the various layers of the neural 
network, allowing the original ‘weights’ to be recalibrated and 
updated, so that the system can ‘converge’ or edge closer to the 
correct answer, in a manner not so dissimilar to reverse engineering. 
With deep learning this process is improved by increasing the number 
of hidden layers. Indeed, there can be anything from four layers to 
over 1,000 layers – hence the term ‘deep’ in deep learning.  32   
 Each individual cycle through the full training dataset, whereby the 
weights are recalibrated, is referred to as an ‘epoch’ of training. 
Typically many epochs are required, sometimes up to several 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 28
thousand, and in principle the more epochs, the better the results, 
although there is no absolute guarantee that the results will continue 
to improve.  
   Convolutional neural networks  
 There are several different types of neural networks. With deep 
learning, convolutional neural networks (ConvNets) have become 
increasingly popular, especially for classifying images. 
 The term ‘convolution’ refers to the calculation performed by each 
layer based on the preceding layer.  33   This process vastly improves the 
process of classifying images, so much so that ConvNets have 
become all but universal. 
 ConvNets are modelled on the visual cortex of the human brain. 
Neuroscientists David Hubel and Torsten Weisel observe that the 
brain has various layers of neurons in the visual cortex that act as 
‘detectors’ operating in a hierarchy looking for increasingly complex 
features. These layers operate in both a feed-forward and a feed-
backwards way, suggesting that our perception is inﬂ  uenced strongly 
by prior knowledge and expectations.  34   
 ConvNets behave in a similar way to standard neural networks, 
but have activation maps – based on the detectors in the brain – 
operating layer by layer, detecting features such as edges, depending 
on their orientation.  35   By the time the ﬁ  nal layer is reached, the 
ConvNet has detected some relatively complex features, based on 
the dataset on which it has been trained. At this point, a classiﬁ  cation 
module, consisting of a traditional neural network, is deployed to 
evaluate the network’s conﬁ  dence – in percentage terms – that it has 
recognised the image. Mitchell offers a helpful summary of this highly 
complex process: 
  Inspired by Hubel and Wiesel’s ﬁ  ndings on the brain’s visual 
cortex, ConvNet takes an input image and transforms it – via 
convolutions – into a set of activation maps with increasingly 
complex features. The features at the highest convolutional layer 
are fed into a traditional neural network, which outputs conﬁ  dence 
percentages for the network’s known object categories. The object 
WHAT IS AI? 29
with the highest conﬁ  dence is returned as the network’s 
classiﬁ  cation of the image.  36     
   Large Language Models  
 Large Language Models (LLMs) have emerged as perhaps the most 
signiﬁ  cant development within the ﬁ  eld of AI in recent years. Although 
the ﬁ  rst version was not released until 2018, they have already had a 
huge impact. 
 Situated within the broader ﬁ  eld of Natural Language Processing 
(NLP), which aims to bridge the gap between computers and human 
language, LLMs operate primarily with text. Broadly speaking, LLMs 
are neural networks, that are trained using self-supervised learning – a 
form of unsupervised learning – which involves unlabeled data, and 
then ﬁ  ne-tuned using semi-supervised learning, where labelled data is 
used to make them especially good at certain tasks, such as dialogue. 
 FIGURE  1.3 Casey Rehm, Laure Michelon (Ushida Rehm Studio), 
Damjan Jovanovic and Lidja Kljakovic (Lifeforms.io), New Campo 
Martio Project, Ars Electronica (2020). New Campo Marzio is an 
open-ended, zero player simulation game powered by AI, that generates 
3D worlds based on Giovanni Battista Piranesi’s Campo Marzio drawing 
(1762) that are populated by autonomous agents. 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 30
 As their name implies, LLMs are large, although ‘large’ is perhaps 
something of an understatement. ‘Massive’ would be a far more 
appropriate term. LLMs are also trained on trillions of ‘tokens’, in the 
form of text data drawn from books, articles and scraped from social 
media and the internet – from sites such as GitHub and Wikipedia. To 
achieve their scale, these LLMs have beneﬁ  ted from the introduction 
of massively parallel computing, made possible by the invention of 
GPUs. This enables concurrent processing of various tasks, allowing 
LLMs to be trained on substantially more data.  37   
 LLMs are also based on around 100 billion parameters. This allows 
the models to learn and represent the intricate relationship between 
words, phrases and sentences, that have been ‘learned’ from 
historical training data but which can be adjusted by the model as it 
learns. The parameters are crucial in allowing LLMs to generate text 
with a high level of accuracy and context. Reinforcement learning can 
also be used to ﬁ  ne-tune the LLMs – to serve as a chatbot, for 
example – through human feedback.  38   Once trained, they can provide 
answers to questions and generate text almost instantaneously. 
 Furthermore, LLMs have also beneﬁ  ted from transformer models, 
a groundbreaking technology whose introduction has been compared 
to that of the internet or smartphones.  39   Transformer models are 
signiﬁ  cantly more efﬁ  cient than previous models that simply 
interpreted words sequentially. Not only do transformer models 
accelerate training times, but they also provide LLMs with more 
structured memory, enhancing their robustness. 
 Transformer models are deep learning models that use an attention 
mechanism rather than convolutional layers or recurrent layers that 
are traditionally used in encoder-decoder systems.  40   A transformer 
model has two components, an encoder and a decoder. The encoder 
processes an input sequence by breaking words down into ‘tokens’. 
The transformer is inspired by the selective focus of human beings, 
and this is where the all-important attention mechanism comes in.  41   
The transformer uses an attention mechanism to ‘pay attention to’ 
each word in a sentence, judging each word in terms of its importance, 
while simultaneously using a ‘self-attention’ mechanism to understand 
the overall meaning of the sentence. The decoder then generates an 
output, predicting either the next word in a sentence (as in OpenAI 
systems), or a word missing in a sentence (as in Google systems), 
WHAT IS AI? 31
based on what it has learnt from the encoder, while ‘paying attention 
to’ the context of previously generated words.  42   
 Importantly, transformer models ‘learn’ context and meaning by 
tracking relationships in the data, enabling them to provide ‘context-
aware’ information that is relevant and coherent.  43   This is vital 
because, as Wittgenstein and others consistently emphasise, the 
meaning of a word is largely determined by its contextual use.  44   
Consequently, LLMs rely on the principle of ‘embodied knowledge’, 
and make predictions based on an associative logic such as, ‘What 
would someone have said in this context?’ 
 In this respect, although they lack true understanding, consciousness 
and intentionality, LLMs operate in a manner not so dissimilar to how 
our human minds works. Indeed the logic of associations is essential 
for our survival. When we see a bright red ring on a cooker, for example, 
we know not to touch it, as we have learnt by association that 
something bright red is likely to be very hot.  45    
   OpenAI and GPT  
 OpenAI is a startup launched in San Francisco in 2015 by a group that 
included Elon Musk, Sam Altman, Greg Brockman, Ilya Sutskever, 
John Schulman and Wojciech Zaremba. As the name suggests, the 
original mission and rationale of OpenAI was to promote openness 
and transparency in the development of AI tools. It was intended as 
an open source non-proﬁ  t organisation and founded on the principle 
of sharing knowledge, in order to avoid the concentration of power in 
the hands of certain prominent companies, so that AI could be used 
for the beneﬁ  t of all humanity in a safe and secure way. 
 However, in 2017 it transitioned into being a for-proﬁ  t company, 
and partnered with Microsoft, thereby losing its original rationale, 
that had attracted leading ﬁ  gures, such as Musk, to invest in it. Musk 
subsequently resigned from the board, and has since sued OpenAI 
for failing to remain an open source, non-proﬁ  t organisation.  46   
 OpenAI is responsible for the Generative Pre-trained Transformer 
(GPT) series, one of the most successful series of LLMs.  47   There 
have now been several generations of GPT models, but the 
development of the earlier models is especially interesting. GPT-1 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 32
was announced in 2018, and beneﬁ  ted from the introduction of semi-
supervised learning, as opposed to the supervised learning techniques 
deployed previously. GPT-2 was announced one year later, in 2019. 
GPT-2 was trained on ten times as much data, and incorporated ten 
times as many parameters. As a result, it proved to be signiﬁ  cantly 
more powerful than GPT-1, and even showed itself to be capable of 
a limited amount of translation. GPT-3 was launched in June 2020, 
and at the time of its launch was signiﬁ  cantly larger than any other 
LLM. It had 1 .5 billion parameters, and has 17 gigabytes of training 
data.  48   To give a sense of scale, GPT-3 was ten times larger than the 
next Natural Language Processing model, Microsoft’s Turing Natural 
Language Generator, although it was not long before Microsoft 
claimed to have the largest model. With the launch of GPT-3, 
the series was beginning to show real potential. GPT-3 had an 
immediate impact and shocked many people by its capabilities.  49   
The launch of GPT-3.5 followed in March 2022, and GPT-4 in March 
2023, each a signiﬁ  cant improvement on the previous model. GPT-4 
has 45 gigabytes of training data, and is rumoured to be trained on 
1 .76 trillion parameters, an enormous increase on GPT-3, although 
OpenAI itself has not disclosed the precise ﬁ  gure.  50   There has also 
been a signiﬁ  cantly greater emphasis on enhanced safety and 
alignment, meaning that the results are likely to be substantially more 
accurate.  
   Google and LaMDA  
 In terms of LLMs the main rival to OpenAI is Google, although it is 
not clear how long this will remain the case. In fact Google has played 
a key role in the development of LLMs in that in 2017 it invented 
transformers, which made LLMs possible. In May 2021, Google 
announced its conversational Language Model for Dialogue 
Applications (LaMDA), its equivalent of OpenAI’s GPT, and, a year 
later its successor, LaMDA-2.  51   
 Google had intended to release LaMDA to the general public, but 
withdrew that proposal following the controversy surrounding its 
engineer, Blake Lemoine, who had claimed that LaMDA was sentient. 
The Lemoine controversy attracted considerable attention in the 
WHAT IS AI? 33
media, although the consensus within the scientiﬁ  c community was 
that neural networks were as yet not conscious.  52   Of course, it is all 
too easy to anthropomorphise these chatbots, and treat them as 
though they are a genuine human interlocutor. Moreover, the situation 
was made somewhat more complex by an article published by 
Google vice-president, Blaise Aguera y Arcas, in which he claimed 
that LaMDA had been making ‘strides’ towards consciousness, 
without speciﬁ  cally claiming – as Lemoine had done – that it had 
become sentient.  53   Either way, Lemoine was eventually sacked by 
Google for violating its policies.  54   The key point, however, is that by 
not releasing LaMDA to the general public Google effectively handed 
its rival, OpenAI, a huge advantage.  
   Chatbots  
 Technically, chatbots (originally called ‘chatterbots’) are not new. Early 
versions include ELIZA, launched in 1966 by Joseph Weizenbaum. 
Intelligent as they might have appeared, they were based on simple 
procedures, involving the recognition of certain words in a 
 FIGURE  1.4 Damjan Jovanovic and Lidja Kljakovic (Lifeforms.io), 
Dream Estate (2020). Dream Estate is a self-driven simulation of the 
world populated by procedurally generated, AI-controlled androids. 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 34
conversation that would act as cues and trigger pre-programmed 
stock responses, giving the appearance of an intelligent conversation. 
Yet even Wiezenbaum himself acknowledged the superﬁ  ciality of 
these systems: ‘In artiﬁ  cial intelligence, machines are made to 
behave in wondrous ways, often sufﬁ  cient to dazzle even the most 
experienced observer. But once a particular program is unmasked, 
once its inner workings are explained, its magic crumbles away; it 
stands revealed as a mere collection of procedures. ’  55   
 Over the years chatbots began to grow in sophistication, and 
became increasingly popular in many companies, especially in the 
area of customer services dealing with routine questions. Yet, 
although an improvement on the earliest models, these remained 
highly limited in their capabilities 
 All that was to change with the development of the recent 
generation of generative AI chatbots, spearheaded by ChatGPT, 
which deploy deep learning and natural language processing. These 
have been developed as a publicly accessible way of engaging with 
the logic of LLMs, ﬁ  ne-tuned so as to make them capable of following 
instructions. 
 These new chatbots operate in a conversational manner. Unlike 
traditional search engines, which are software systems that ﬁ  nd 
webpages in response to a textual web search query, these new 
chatbots generate actual responses to questions, based on 
information retrieved from the model itself. They are highly proﬁ  cient 
in generating coherent, grammatically accurate responses with 
perfect spelling. They remember a limited number of previous 
prompts in a conversation, allow for the possibility of follow-up 
questions, and are even capable of admitting their mistakes. They can 
operate in many different languages, and can generate text in a range 
of different styles and formats. They can write and debug computer 
programs, and answer mathematical problems. They can also be 
used for checking, summarising, editing and providing feedback on 
existing texts. What is more they can generate responses to even 
complex questions in a matter of a few seconds, and most standard 
versions remain free. Moreover, even their introduction has served to 
trigger an innovation race that will surely bring us even better models. 
In short, these new chatbots have had a radical impact in the world of 
generative AI.  
WHAT IS AI? 35
   The launch of ChatGPT  
 ChatGPT has proved to be a complete game changer. Ironically, 
however, the launch of ChatGPT was something of an afterthought 
on the part of OpenAI. Indeed, at the very beginning of November 
2022, there were no plans in place at all to launch it.  56   Instead, the 
entire company was focused on a more powerful model then under 
development – GPT4. However, GPT4 had its problems, and 
occasionally generated hate speech and complete nonsense. As a 
result, OpenAI kept postponing its launch. However, the company 
was also only too aware of potential competition from its rivals 
working on other AI chatbots, and was under pressure to do 
something. 
 Step in GPT3.5. An alternative strategy under discussion at the 
time was to launch GPT3.5, as an earlier, less powerful version, just 
to test it out in the market, and use any feedback to improve it. 
However, in mid-November this alternative strategy suddenly became 
a reality. OpenAI held a boardroom meeting, and Sam Altman made 
the snap decision to release the older, less powerful version: ‘The 
plan was to call it Chat with GPT 3.5 and put it out by the end of the 
month. They referred to it as a “low key research preview” . ’  57   As Mira 
Murati, OpenAI’s CTO, put it: ‘We plan to frame it as a research 
release. This reduces risk in all dimensions while allowing us to learn 
a lot. We are aiming to move quickly over the next few days to make 
it happen. ’  58   
 Reportedly, ‘It didn’t feel like a big-deal decision to anyone in the 
room. ’  59   And yet, on the contrary, it really did prove to be a ‘big-deal 
decision’ – a very ‘big-deal decision’. OpenAI added an already 
existing graphical user interface (GUI), changed the name to ChatGPT, 
and – bingo! – ChatGPT was launched only a few days later, on 30 
November 2022.  
   ChatGPT and its rivals  
 While ChatGPT stole the show as the ﬁ  rst of a new generation of 
chatbots, its launch was swiftly followed by the launch of other 
chatbots, including Google’s Bard, now renamed Gemini. Bard was 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 36
developed by both Google and its subsidiary, DeepMind, also now 
renamed Google DeepMind. 
 In an industry where there is intense rivalry and seemingly little 
long-term commitment in terms of employment, it was not long 
before OpenAI and Google had begun to spawn other rival companies. 
A leading example is Anthropic, founded in 2021 by a group of former 
OpenAI employees, who left the company, disillusioned by its 
effective acquisition by Microsoft.  60   Anthropic quickly acquired the 
ﬁ  nancial backing of Amazon and Google. While still in its relative 
infancy, Anthropic began to turn heads with the launch of its chatbot, 
Claude 3, in March 2024, reputed to outperform both OpenAI’s GPT4 
and Google’s Gemini. Another example is Perplexity, launched in 
August 2022, also by former employees of OpenAI.  61   A still further 
example is Inﬂ  ection AI, whose CEO and co-founder was Mustafa 
Suleyman, one of the co-founders of DeepMind, who has since 
moved to become CEO of Microsoft AI.  62   
 What has become clear is that alongside the mergers and strategic 
alliances, such as between Google and DeepMind and between 
Microsoft and OpenAI, there are also bifurcations, as individuals from 
one parent company leave to start another. Over time we can expect 
an increasing proliferation of rival companies. How long will we still 
be able to talk about the alliance between Google and DeepMind and 
between Microsoft and OpenAI as the main axis of competition 
within the ﬁ  eld?  
   The limitations of chatbots  
 Impressive as it might appear, however, this new generation of 
chatbots is not without its limitations. Like LLMs, they are prone to 
‘hallucinate’, and to generate false information. An estimate put the 
overall reliability of early versions of ChatGPT at only around 52 per 
cent, while it underperforms in certain speciﬁ  c areas, such as science 
and the law.  63   Chatbots also suffer from any cultural norms and 
human biases embedded in the data on which they have been trained, 
and since the online world threatens to become an echo chamber, 
these might even be ampliﬁ  ed thereby reinforcing stereotypes and 
promoting racism.  64   Likewise, this data inevitably has a date stamp 
WHAT IS AI? 37
on it based on when it was scraped from the internet and other 
sources, meaning that some of its information could soon be 
outdated. In short, despite the apparent ﬂ  uency of their answers, 
chatbots remain unreliable, and more adept at some tasks than 
others. 
 It is also important to recognise that a chatbot only  mimics   human 
conversation. As Stephen Wolfram points out, ‘It is just saying things 
that “sound right” based on what things “sounded like” in its training 
data. ’  65   However, what is remarkable – and surprising even for many 
experts in the ﬁ  eld – is quite how uncannily  similar   the outcomes are 
to what humans might have written or said. This suggests that its 
way of operating might not be so dissimilar to the way in which 
human beings compose sentences. 
 In fact, for Wolfram the fact that a chatbot can produce plausible text 
seems to infer that writing is ‘computationally easier’ than we thought.  66   
For Wolfram, this could potentially open up a new line of research into 
the human mind: ‘This suggests something that’s at least scientiﬁ  cally 
important: that human language (and the patterns of thinking behind it) 
are somehow simpler and more “law like” in their structure than we 
thought. ChatGPT implicitly discovered this. But we can potentially 
explicitly expose it, with semantic grammar, computational language 
etc. ’  67   Is human writing, then, not more straightforward and formulaic 
than previously thought, relying on stock phrases and standard modes 
of expression – drawn upon from our ‘training data’ – that ‘sound right’ 
in the circumstances? The effectiveness of Google’s autocomplete 
feature, Smart Compose, that offers suggestions on how to ﬁ  nish a 
sentence, would seem to imply this. 
 Nonetheless, in a world where many value the convenience of 
speed over accuracy, chatbots have become the public face of AI. 
Also they have the advantage of being largely correct in their grammar 
and spelling, and the quality of their information is likely to improve 
over time. Clearly, chatbots are here to stay.  
   Emergent Abilities  
 Perhaps the most curious but remarkable attribute of LLMs and 
chatbots is their capacity to develop ‘emergent abilities’, also referred 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 38
to as ‘emergent capabilities’.  68   These abilities give them the potential 
to engage in often highly convincing, human-like conversation that 
mimics human speech, generate ‘creative’ writing, translate 
languages, write code, reason by analogy, solve complex puzzles, 
and even – we could claim – to design. 
 These are totally unexpected abilities acquired by LLMs and 
chatbots in areas in which they have not been explicitly programmed. 
Indeed computationally LLMs are not so sophisticated, and are 
based on relatively limited code.  69   By contrast, these abilities arise 
implicitly as a result of their exposure to massive quantities of data 
during their training process, and are based on their capacity to detect 
and learn from complex patterns and relationships contained within 
that data. Importantly, then, it is the amount of data on which they 
have been trained – rather than the complexity of their algorithms – 
that has been the crucial factor in their success. Importantly, also, 
these abilities cannot be detected in smaller models, but appear only 
in larger models.  70   With LLMs, size matters. 
 Interestingly, the way in which LLMs have ‘picked up’ these 
abilities through exposure to these vast quantities of data is not so 
dissimilar to how human beings also pick up information, even though 
arguably human beings have a greater depth of understanding. After 
all, languages are often learnt and accents adopted, purely by 
exposure to others speaking that language in a particular accent. 
Likewise, design is rarely taught explicitly in architectural education, 
but more often ‘picked up’ implicitly through immersion in an 
architectural culture. 
 These ‘emergent abilities’ or ‘emergent capabilities’ invite 
comparison with the theory of emergence. The phenomenon of 
emergence occurs when complex properties ‘emerge’ spontaneously 
out of the relatively simple interactions of the constituent parts within 
a system, leading to novel outcomes greater than the sum of the 
parts.  71   Philip Anderson deﬁ  nes emergence as follows: ‘Emergence 
is when quantitative changes in a system result in qualitative changes 
in behavior. ’  72   Examples of emergence can be found in nature, such 
as the murmuration of starlings, or when individual ants operate 
collectively to perform a certain task, and in culture life, such as the 
formation of languages and trends. Indeed, culture itself could be 
said to be emergent. The larger the multi-agent system, it would 
WHAT IS AI? 39
seem, the greater the potential for emergent behaviours, just as the 
larger the body of training data, the greater the propensity for 
‘emergent abilities’ in a LLM. 
 Yet, although the phenomenon of emergence is easy enough to 
describe, it is less easy to actually deﬁ  ne and explain in scientiﬁ  c 
terms, and remains somewhat mysterious. Certainly, John Holland, 
one of the leading authorities on emergence, refrains from offering a 
deﬁ  nition of emergence, and constantly stresses the difﬁ  culties 
in providing a comprehensive scientiﬁ  c explanation for the 
phenomenon.  73   This is especially the case with examples of ‘strong 
emergence’ – such as social norms, cultural values and novel 
biological systems – which, unlike ‘weak emergence’, is unable to be 
modelled computationally, since strong emergence cannot be 
reduced to the sum of its parts. In fact, strong emergence is so 
unfathomable that philosopher Mark Bedau has described it as being 
uncomfortably like magic.  74   In other words, although we can observe 
these phenomena, we simply do not have a good scientiﬁ  c explanation 
as to why or how they occur. 
 Arguably emergent abilities remain the least understood and yet 
most important feature of LLMs and chatbots. Whether or not they 
can properly be understood as a form of ‘emergence’, they are equally 
mysterious. And, like emergence itself, they defy easy scientiﬁ  c 
explanation, much like the workings of deep learning in general. 
However, there is also something potentially disturbing about LLMs 
and chatbots.  75   We realize that they have now begun to understand 
different languages and write code. But what else have they learnt? 
 ‘ChatGPT, what is the answer to life, the universe and everything?’  76             
40

42
               2  
 A brief history of AI            
   W e could trace the origins of AI right back to the myths and 
legends of artiﬁ  cial life in classical antiquity and beyond. Or we 
could trace them back to the more recent religious and literary stories 
of Golem and Frankenstein.  1   Likewise, we could trace them back to 
the development of formal reasoning in philosophy. Without the ﬁ  eld 
of logic, founded by Aristotle, we would not have the modern 
computer. And without the attempts of Leibnitz to reduce human 
thought to some form of calculation, we would not have computers 
performing human-like reasoning. Or, again, we could trace them 
back to developments in mathematics. Without the algebraic 
operations formalised by George Boole, we would not have the 
Boolean logic that underpins computation.  2   
 Alternatively, if we were to limit ourselves to the domain of 
computer science, we could trace the origins of AI back to ﬁ  gures 
such as the British polymath Charles Babbage, inventor of the 
Difference Engine and Analytical Engine,  3   and to the mathematician, 
Augusta Ada King, Countess of Lovelace – otherwise known as Ada 
Lovelace – who collaborated with him, and is often regarded as one of 
the ﬁ  rst computer programmers.  4   It is generally agreed, however, that 
it was another British polymath, Alan Turing, who was the ﬁ  rst person 
to really address the potential of an artiﬁ  cial form of intelligence.  
   Alan Turing  
 In 1936, Turing wrote a paper – published in 1937 – titled ‘On 
Computable Numbers, with an Application to the 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 44
 Entscheidungsproblem  ’, in which he laid out the conceptual 
groundwork for computation, as we know it, describing what he 
initially called an ‘a-machine’ or an ‘automatic machine’.  5   This has 
subsequently become known as the Turing Machine.  6   
 Turing played a vital role in British intelligence during the Second 
World War (1939–45), using the ‘Bombe’, an electro-mechanical 
device, to decipher German messages. The challenge was to crack 
the Enigma code, and expose the secret content of coded messages 
being sent to and from the German armed forces. Once his team had 
achieved this, Turing was cautious not to offer the Germans any clue 
about the British success by intervening in any form of military 
situation based on information that could only have been gained by 
the cracking of the German code.  7   
 After the war, Turing worked for the National Physical Laboratory 
on the Automatic Computing Engine (ACE), an early form of stored-
program computer. In 1948 he took up an academic appointment at 
 FIGURE  2.1 Alan Turing, portrait photo by Elliott & Fry (1951), 
National Portrait Gallery. The heroic wartime exploits of computation 
pioneer Alan Turing remained largely unknown until after his death. 
A BRIEF HISTORY OF AI 45
Victoria University of Manchester, and designed software for the 
Manchester Mark 1, one of the ﬁ  rst stored-program computers.  8   
During this period, Turing also worked on a computer program that 
could play chess.  9   Additionally, he later became interested in biological 
morphogenesis and developed the theory of ‘reaction-diffusion’.  10   H e  
was also the ﬁ  rst to notice the connection between artiﬁ  cial life and 
AI.  11   The range of Turing’s intellectual interests was extraordinary. As 
if all this was not enough, Turing also came ﬁ  fth in the marathon in the 
1947 English national championships.  12   Turing was not only one of the 
greatest polymaths of all time, but also a true universal man. 
 In terms of his involvement with AI, the most signiﬁ  cant moment 
came in 1950, when Turing published a paper, ‘Computing Machinery 
and Intelligence’, where he speculated about the possibility of 
developing machines that could think.  13   He described a technique by 
which to judge whether the intelligence of a ‘thinking machine’ could 
match the intelligence of human beings. Initially named after a popular 
party game, the ‘Imitation Game’, it subsequently became known as 
the ‘Turing Test’.  14   The clever aspect of the Turing Test is that we do
