not have to deﬁ  ne terms such as ‘a machine’ or ‘to think’. Instead, a 
simple game is played between a judge, a human being and a 
computer program. However, both the human being and the computer 
program are behind a screen and hidden from the judge. The challenge 
is for the computer program to compete against the human being, 
taking part in a conversation and answering questions set by the 
judge in a manner that is indistinguishable from the way in which a 
human being might answer. However, all transactions are made 
through a text-only channel, so the computer does not need to 
actually speak. If the computer program manages to convince the 
judge that it is human, it passes the Turing Test. 
 As has often been observed, AI is invisible. Likewise, while Turing’s 
early work on the Enigma code was about revealing secrets, it was 
also very secretive itself. It was literally invisible, in that it was 
undertaken as part of a top-secret wartime research programme at 
Bletchley Park, England, and was classiﬁ  ed information under the 
Ofﬁ  cial Secrets Act, so that it remained a secret until declassiﬁ  ed in 
the mid-1970s.  15   As a result, the general public had no idea about the 
research that Turing had been undertaking. It was as though his work 
remained even more of an enigma than the code that he had broken. 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 46
 These days, of course, Turing is well known, following the release 
of the movie  The Imitation Game   (2014), directed by Morten Tyldum, 
with Benedict Cumberbatch passing himself off as Turing.  16   Likewise, 
Turing now appears on every £50 note, along with his famous quote, 
‘This is only a foretaste of what is to come, and only a shadow of 
what is going to be. ’  17   From once being totally invisible, Turing has 
now become the high-proﬁ  le poster boy for computation and AI, and 
an icon within the gay community. 
 And yet despite the secrecy surrounding much of his life, it could 
be argued that the tragedy of Turing is that he failed to keep one 
aspect of his life a secret – his own sexuality. As Benjamin Bratton 
astutely observes, Turing failed to pass his own ‘Turing Test’. He failed 
to convince the world that he was heterosexual in a society where 
homosexuality was deemed a crime.  18   Once his homosexuality had 
been exposed, however, Turing was prosecuted. In 1952 he was 
convicted of ‘gross indecency’ and given the choice of a prison 
sentence or chemical castration. He opted for the latter. In 1954 
Turing probably took his own life, no doubt partly as a result of this 
highly public humiliation. The irony here, of course, is that although 
Turing himself had saved the lives of millions during the war, at the 
time of his own death the general public was completely unaware of 
many of his achievements, as they remained state secrets.  19   
 In the UK, the popular television science ﬁ  ction series  Doctor 
Who    20   tells the story of a scientist with a PhD who saves the world in 
every episode. In contrast to most American superheroes, who slug 
it out with their foes in physical brawls, Doctor Who never gets into 
any physical ﬁ  ght, but vanquishes evil simply through the application 
of science and technology. Could Doctor Alan Turing have been the 
inspiration for the original Doctor Who?  21    
   The birth of AI  
 Another signiﬁ  cant development occurred in 1943, when 
mathematician Walter Pitts and neurologist Warren McCulloch 
devised an early computer using neural networks inspired by the 
human brain.  22   In so doing, they brought together the binary logic of 
Turing’s computer with the true/false structure of the propositional 
A BRIEF HISTORY OF AI 47
logic of philosopher Bertrand Russell and the on/off structure of 
neural synapses observed by neurophysiologist/cybernetician Charles 
Sherrington.  23   As Boden comments, ‘The true/false values of logic 
were mapped onto the on/off activity of brain cells, and the 0/1 of 
individual states in the Turing machine. ’  24   
 By the 1950s two overlapping branches of research into computer 
modelling had emerged, one looking at adaptive behaviour, and one 
working on logical thinking. Later these were to split into distinct 
branches; cybernetics and symbolic computing.  25   ‘Those interested 
in  life , ’ notes Boden, ‘stayed in cybernetics, and those interested in 
the  mind   turned to symbolic computing. ’  26   
 If, however, we were to isolate one particular event that was 
instrumental in not only cementing this divide, but also in deﬁ  ning the 
term ‘ AI’, it would surely be the Dartmouth Summer Workshop on 
Artiﬁ  cial Intelligence held in 1956.  27   The term was effectively coined 
in 1955 when the event was proposed by John McCarthy, Marvin 
Minsky, Nathanial Rochester and Claude Shannon, and was attended 
by many of the ﬁ  gures who would later become luminaries in the 
ﬁ  eld of AI: 
  We propose that a 2 month, 10 man study of artiﬁ  cial intelligence 
be carried out during the summer of 1956 at Dartmouth College in 
Hanover, New Hampshire. The study is to proceed on the basis of 
the conjecture that every aspect of learning or any other feature of 
intelligence can in principle be so precisely described that a 
machine can be made to simulate it. An attempt will be made to 
ﬁ  nd how to make machines use language, form abstractions and 
concepts, solve kinds of problems now reserved for humans, and 
improve themselves. We think that a signiﬁ  cant advance can be 
made in one or more of these problems if a carefully selected 
group of scientists work on it together for a summer.  28    
 The event not only helped to deﬁ  ne the ﬁ  eld, but also led to the 
adoption of the term ‘artiﬁ  cial intelligence’, even though McCarthy 
himself was not fully comfortable with it.  29   
 What followed was a period of euphoria and intense, well-funded 
research, fuelled by heady speculation. Indeed in 1967 , Minsky 
claimed that within a generation the problem of artiﬁ  cial intelligence 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 48
would be solved.  30   All of a sudden, AI came into the spotlight. With 
time, however, it became clear that these aspirations were unrealistic. 
For this and other reasons, funding dried up and the ﬁ  rst ‘ AI winter’ 
occurred.  
   AI winters  
 AI winters could be deﬁ  ned as periods in which conﬁ  dence in the 
potential of AI collapsed on the part of venture capitalists and 
government funding agencies. These crises of conﬁ  dence were 
triggered initially by reports from within the academic community, 
but ultimately owe their origins to overinﬂ  ated expectations – much 
like the dot.com boom and bust in the 1990s – and illustrated the 
volatility of public conﬁ  dence in the new technology. The ﬁ  rst AI 
winter occurred around 1974 and was prompted largely by two 
setbacks. 
 One of the setbacks was that, despite huge investment by the US 
government in developing instantaneous translation as part of its 
Cold War effort, the results did not live up to the hype. AI was quite 
capable of translating words, but only if in the correct order.  31   There 
were also other problems that led to humorous outcomes, such as 
‘out of sight, out of mind’ being translated as ‘an invisible lunatic’, or 
‘the spirit is willing but the ﬂ  esh is weak’ as ‘the vodka is good but 
the meat is rotten’.  32   As a result, the primary source of military funding 
in the United States, the Defense Advanced Research Projects 
Agency (DARPA), withdrew its funding from a major machine learning 
research project. Meanwhile in 1973, Professor James Lighthill 
issued a controversial report in the United Kingdom, where he noted 
that AI had failed to live up to its somewhat grandiose aspirations.  33   
The net result was a sudden loss of conﬁ  dence in AI, leading to a 
drastic reduction in funding for AI research – a phenomenon which 
then rippled throughout the rest of Europe. 
 The other setback was that conﬁ  dence began to fade in 
connectionist AI and the potential of neural networks, stemming 
from the early work of McCulloch and Pitts. This was partly the result 
of the publication of the book,  Perceptrons: An Introduction to 
Computational Geometry  , written Marvin Minsky and Seymour 
A BRIEF HISTORY OF AI 49
Papert, who were enthusiasts of symbolic AI.  34   The book outlined the 
limitations of the connectionist approach that relied on the use of 
the perceptron, an algorithm for supervised learning, invented by 
Frank Rosenblatt in 1958.  35   Rosenblatt had predicted that the 
perceptron would also be able to make decisions and even translate 
languages.  36   Indeed, the perceptron did show some promise, and, 
as became clear later when deep learning was developed, 
connectionist AI is quite capable of both making decisions and 
translating languages. 
 The publication of  Perceptrons   marked one of the most 
controversial episodes in the history of AI. For the most part, 
competition between the various ‘tribes’ in the AI community took 
the form of standard academic rivalry. However, some perceived 
this book as going beyond this acceptable level and delivering 
a blistering critique of connectionism. Minsky and Papert 
themselves played down their critique, claiming that it had been 
misunderstood: ‘We did not think of our work as killing Snow White; 
we saw it as a way to understand her. ’  37   Two years later Rosenblatt 
died tragically in a boating accident. The death of its most high-proﬁ  le 
exponent had a devastating effect on the proﬁ  le of connectionism. 
Without its chief spokesperson, connectionism largely disappeared 
from sight. 
 As a result, connectionism began to fall out of favour, and the rival 
approach, symbolic AI, came into the ascendancy. Indeed Geoffrey 
Hinton claims that in subsequent years he and his colleagues working 
on connectionism were forced to resort to the logic of camouﬂ  age, 
and scrub any reference to neural networks in order to get any 
academic papers accepted.  38   
 A second ‘ AI winter’ occurred in the late 1980s as the market for 
LISP machines based on the LISP programming languages collapsed, 
and workstations developed by Sun Microsystems and desktop 
computers produced by IBM and Apple began to take over the 
market.  39   A further factor was a policy change at DARPA, where it 
was decided to attach funding to speciﬁ  c projects, targeted for the 
likelihood of their success. 
 Despite the apparent crises of these ‘ AI winters’, research into AI 
actually continued to make steady progress. It is almost as though 
the initial hype about the potential successes of AI was matched only 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 50
by the hype about its failures. As Ray Kurzweil notes, ‘Many observers 
still think that the AI winter was the end of the story and that nothing 
since has come of the AI ﬁ  eld. Yet today many thousands of AI 
applications are deeply embedded in the infrastructure of every 
industry. ’  40   Rodney Brooks takes a similar view: 
  There’s this stupid myth out there that AI has failed, but AI is 
around you every second of the day. People just don’t notice it. 
You’ve got AI systems in cars, tuning the parameters of the fuel 
injection systems. When you land in an airplane, your gate gets 
chosen by an AI scheduling system. Every time you use a piece of 
Microsoft software, you’ve got an AI system trying to ﬁ  gure out 
what you are doing, like writing a letter, and it does a pretty damn 
good job. Every time you see a movie with computer generated 
characters, they’re all little AI characters behaving as a group. 
Every time you play a video game, you’re playing against an AI 
system.  41     
   The Deep Learning Revolution  
 In 2006, the surviving members of those who had taken part in the 
original workshop at Dartmouth College reconvened for the ﬁ  ftieth 
anniversary of that original event. Ironically, they had little to show for 
their efforts. AI had proved to be something of a failure. Despite the 
grandiose aspirations as to what it might be able to achieve, AI had 
achieved precious little during its ﬁ  rst ﬁ  fty years – a fact that the 
surviving members openly admitted, and all were agreed that it was 
also unlikely to achieve much in the next ﬁ  fty years.  42   
 By a curious coincidence, however, 2006 proved to be 
approximately the time when AI began to work, and the ‘deep 
learning revolution’ took off.  43   This was mainly because neural 
networks – which had been explored by Pitts and McCulloch as far 
back as 1943 – eventually proved highly effective, largely due to the 
discovery that GPUs, computer chips originally developed for the 
gaming industry, could be used to radically enhance the performance 
of computers. Since then deep learning has become the dominant 
approach, such that it is now almost synonymous with AI. 
A BRIEF HISTORY OF AI 51
 Several factors have contributed to this: 
     1   Computers have become signiﬁ  cantly more powerful and 
faster.  
    2   Major advances in algorithms have fuelled the deep learning 
breakthrough.  
    3   Cloud services have made access to signiﬁ  cant computational 
power possible.  
    4   There has been a signiﬁ  cant inﬂ  ux of capital investment from 
both the public and private sectors.  44    
    5   There has been an exponential increase in the amount of data 
generated.  45      
   AI and visibility  
 The history of AI can be read as a history of visibility and invisibility. 
Most people are largely unaware of AI, and have little understanding 
of what it does. And often even those who do understand what it 
actually does, do not understand quite how it does it – as in the case 
of deep learning. 
 Just as AI itself is invisible, so research on AI passes largely 
unnoticed by the general public. In some cases this is because there 
can be sound business reasons not to divulge information about 
research that is still ongoing. However, in most cases the ‘invisibility’ 
of research into AI is simply down to the fact that it is either not 
sufﬁ  ciently interesting or accessible to the general public to be 
covered by the press. Nor should we forget that AI itself is working 
invisibly ‘behind the scenes’, feeding us advertisements for products 
that our previous search record suggests that we might ﬁ  nd appealing. 
Advertising by stealth. AI is governed by the logic of camouﬂ  age. It 
might be there, but we cannot see it. 
 Potentially more disconcerting, however, is the opposite tendency 
of using AI for branding purposes, even when little or no AI is actually 
being used. For example, Hanson Robotics has been marketing a 
humanoid robot, Sophia, with some success. Sophia not only looks 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 52
human – much like the replicants in  Blade Runner  , also an artiﬁ  cial life 
form – but is also presented as though ‘she’ thinks like humans. In 
October 2017 , Sophia became the ﬁ  rst robot to be made a citizen of 
Saudi Arabia.  46   And in November 2017 ‘she’ became the ﬁ  rst robot to 
be named ‘Innovation Champion’ by the United Nations Development 
programme.  47   Sophia has been marketed as though it is a conscious, 
sentient being. But in fact Sophia is anything but conscious, and has 
been decried by the AI community. For Rodney Brooks, Sophia is 
‘completely bogus and a total sham’, while for Benedict Evans, 
Sophia is little more than ‘a tape recorder with a rubber head on it’.  48   
 Few would be fooled into believing that Sophia is a human being. 
But this is not the issue here. While the Turing Test is designed to test 
whether a computer can successfully masquerade as a sentient 
human, Sophia is an automaton masquerading as ‘strong AI’ – a 
sentient robot. And the purpose of this masquerade is to impress the 
general public and to promote Hanson Robotics. 
 We can ask similar questions of Ai-Da, billed as the ‘world’s ﬁ  rst 
ultra-realistic robot artist’. We obviously do not need a humanoid 
 FIGURE  2.2 Sophia, the humanoid robot produced by Hanson Robotics, 
is photographed here in the ‘AI for Good’ Global Summit in Geneva on 
7 June 2017. Photo: Fabrice Coffrini, Getty (693354896). 
A BRIEF HISTORY OF AI 53
robot to generate an ‘artwork’. Indeed, there is unlikely to be anything 
that Ai-Da has produced that could possibly match the quality of 
output generated by diffusion models. We therefore have to ask 
whether Ai-Da is really an artist, or just a con-artist? 
 This is the key question. Take the whole process by which Ai-Da 
‘produces’ paintings. Scratch beneath the surface, and an entirely 
different picture emerges. It transpires that Ai-Da is not alone in 
producing ‘artworks’. She/it has a human accomplice. As Imogen 
West-Knights observes, ‘The process by which these pieces were 
created is a little convoluted. Ai-Da did not “paint” them, per se. She 
made preparatory sketches that were then ﬂ  eshed out by being fed 
back into her own algorithm, and then a human art technician, Suzie 
Emery, whose name you do have to hunt down to ﬁ  nd, did the 
painting. Ai-Da then did some further mark-making on the paint 
surface to complete the work. The reason it’s so complicated is 
because the truth is that, so far, AI robots don’t make massively 
interesting art without signiﬁ  cant human input. ’  49   
 Gotcha! Ai-DA, it seems, has been rumbled. But this has not 
prevented her/it from achieving a considerable amount of success, 
including an invitation to mount a solo exhibition at the 2022 Venice 
Art Biennale.  50   
 Likewise – despite its name – the architectural practice AI 
SpaceFactory did not actually use AI in any of its design and fabrication 
processes at the moment of its launch, though it had plans to do so 
in the future.  51   While AI can be very good at detecting spam and 
other forms of marketing, it can also be exploited in marketing. 
Should we not be suspicious of any company with AI in its name? In 
short, never underestimate the power of AI to excite the general 
public, even when it is not there. As Maria Dantz of Spacemaker AI 
comments, ‘ AI is certainly something that opens doors, because 
people want to hear about it. ’  52   
 This is the logic of ‘inverse camouﬂ  age’. If the logic of camouﬂ  age 
is to pretend something is not there when it is – making the visible 
invisible – the logic of inverse camouﬂ  age is to pretend that something 
is there when it is not. It is a question of making the visible invisible, 
versus making the invisible visible. But could inverse camouﬂ  age not 
be a cunning marketing strategy – ‘marketing the invisible’? AI in 
some form is now being used by almost everyone. But who is to 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 54
know whether advanced AI is being used or not? Might these 
companies pass their own inverse Turing Test by persuading the 
general public that they are using advanced AI when they are not? In 
the age of AI, what is AI but the ultimate marketing opportunity? 
 Beware the AI of marketing!  53   
 The history of AI, however, has also been dominated by the 
opposite tendency – the urge to reveal what is actually there, by 
hosting a series of events showcasing the all too often hidden 
capabilities of AI. Such is the low proﬁ  le of AI that it takes a high-
proﬁ  le event to bring it to our attention. This could be a news headline 
comment by a famous ﬁ  gure, such as Stephen Hawking or Elon 
Musk. Or it could be a competition staged on television. It is these 
moments of great visibility that really capture the public imagination. 
 These high-proﬁ  le events might be entertaining, but the real 
intention behind them is to showcase AI and raise awareness of its 
commercial potential, so as to impress would-be ﬁ  nancial backers. In 
so doing the invisible logic of AI is made visible. It is simply a question 
of good marketing. Visibility is everything.  
   Deep Blue  
 The challenge of beating human beings at chess has long been seen 
as a major objective for AI. In the 1830s, Babbage mused over the 
possibility of building a chess-playing machine, as had Turing in 1946.  54   
And over the years there have been many predictions as to when a 
computer might beat the world champion.  55   In 1990, Kurzweil had 
predicted that a computer would beat the world chess champion by 
2000.  56   
 In 1997 , IBM’s supercomputer Deep Blue took on the then world 
chess champion, Garry Kasparov, in a high-proﬁ  le chess match 
reported in all the mainstream media. The match consisted of six 
games. Deep Blue won two games, Kasparov one game, and three 
were drawn. Three years before the date predicted by Kurzweil, a 
computer had beaten the world chess champion. 
 Despite its name, however, Deep Blue is not in fact an example of 
deep learning. It is a classic example of machine learning. Although it 
was given a number of handcrafted features with a speciﬁ  c set of 
A BRIEF HISTORY OF AI 55
rules and heuristics, based on information distilled from games of 
renowned chess experts, it did actually ‘learn’ to play chess by playing 
thousands of games of chess, and was able to determine many 
parameters not initially programmed. As Toby Walsh notes, ‘For 
example, it was not programmed how to weight a safe king position, 
compared to a space advantage in the center. The optimal values for 
these parameters were determined by machine learning over 
thousands of master games. ’  57   
 The success of Deep Blue shocked many people, especially within 
the chess community. Mark Wieder, a chess player who is also a 
computer programmer, commented, ‘This is a historic event. The 
greatest human player of all time lost to a machine. ’  58   Kasparov 
himself was so shocked by one of the moves played by Deep Blue 
that he reportedly thought that it had cheated.  59   In the end, however, 
Kasparov acknowledged the achievements of Deep Blue, adding that 
he had sensed a new kind of intelligence and witnessed true creativity 
in some of its moves.  60   Indeed, Deep Blue has made a huge 
 FIGURE  2.3 World chess champion Garry Kasparov holds his head in 
his hands at the start of the sixth and fi  nal match against IBM’s Deep 
Blue computer in New York on 11 May 1997. Kasparov lost the match in 
just nineteen moves. Photo supplied by Getty Images. 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 56
contribution to the world of chess, revealing new strategies and 
moves that have since been adopted by human chess players. 
 Moreover, Kasparov is in no doubt that – eventually – AI will be 
capable of outperforming human beings in every aspect of our lives: 
‘Everything we know how to do, machines will do better. ’  61   Anything 
you can do, AI can do better. 
 However, although the victory over Kasparov had a dramatic 
impact on the popular imagination and certainly gave IBM high-proﬁ  le 
publicity, Deep Blue did not contribute much to the development of 
AI. Indeed, according to Russell, the match was essentially just a 
demonstration of techniques that had been developed many years 
earlier: ‘Deep Blue is basically a demo of algorithms that were 
designed 30 years earlier and had been gradually enhanced and then 
deployed on increasingly powerful hardware, until they could beat a 
world champion. ’  62   The key advantage, then, was the increase in 
computation power. This allowed Deep Blue to compute signiﬁ  cantly 
faster and thereby analyse more possible moves in the game.  
 ‘Anything you can do, 
AI can do better.’ 
   AlphaGo  
 The next high-proﬁ  le challenge involved Go, a game that is simple 
enough in terms of its rules, but inﬁ  nitely complicated in that the 
number of potential legal board positions is greater than the number 
of atoms in the universe.  63   As a result, it is a game that has long 
been considered one of the greatest challenges for AI. The reason 
for this is that the straightforward ‘expert systems’ deployed in the 
chess match against Garry Kasparov would prove simply incapable. 
Even if all the computers in the world were deployed, they would 
not be sufﬁ  cient for the task. It therefore became imperative to 
deploy an alternative strategy, ‘learning systems’, in the form of deep 
learning. 
A BRIEF HISTORY OF AI 57
 AlphaGo is a deep learning computer program developed by 
DeepMind Technologies, a company owned by Google. The program 
uses reinforcement learning and a Monte Carlo search algorithm to 
‘teach’ itself to play Go by playing a vast number of matches against 
itself.  64   The big advantage here is that AlphaGo learns the game 
afresh, unconstrained by traditional thinking. As Demis Hassabis, 
CEO of DeepMind, has noted, ‘Deep Blue is a hand-crafted program 
where programmers distilled the information from chess 
grandmasters into speciﬁ  c rules and heuristics, whereas we’ve 
imbued AlphaGo with the ability to learn, and then it’s learnt it through 
practice and study, which is much more human-like. ’  65   
 However, this is perhaps a little disingenuous. AlphaGo cannot 
learn by deep learning alone. It should be acknowledged that AlphaGo 
also has a few handcrafted features, and – like Deep Blue – was also 
trained on a database of previous matches played by humans. As 
such, the two programmes are perhaps not quite as dissimilar as is 
often thought. As Russell comments: 
  AlphaGo, and its successor AlphaZero, created a lot of media 
attention around deep learning with stunning advances in Go and 
Chess, but they’re really a hybrid of classical search-based AI and 
a deep learning algorithm that evaluates each game position that 
the classical AI searches through. While the ability to distinguish 
between good and bad positions is central to AlphaGo, it cannot 
play world-champion-level Go just by deep learning.  66    
 It was the high-proﬁ  le match between AlphaGo and Korean 9-Dan 
professional Go player Lee Sedol, held in Seoul, South Korea, in 
March 2016, that really put AlphaGo on the map. The match consisted 
of ﬁ  ve games, with most Go experts – including Lee himself – 
predicting that the human player would win easily. However, much to 
their surprise, AlphaGo won the ﬁ  rst game and went on to win the 
match by four games to one. 
 Game 2 proved to be the turning point. After Game 1, Lee was 
surprised, but after Game 2 he was lost for words: ‘Yesterday, I was 
surprised. But today I am speechless. If you look at the way the game 
was played, I admit, it was a very clear loss on my part. From the very 
beginning of the game, there was not a moment in time when I felt 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 58
that I was leading. ’  67   The biggest talking point of the whole match, 
however, was a remarkable move played by AlphaGo in this game: 
  In Game 2, Lee exhibits a different style, attempting to play more 
cautiously. He waits for any opening that he can exploit, but 
AlphaGo continues to surprise. At move 37 , AlphaGo plays an 
unexpected move, what’s called a ‘shoulder hit’ on the upper right 
side of the board. This move in this position is unseen in professional 
games, but its cleverness is immediately apparent. [Go player] Fan 
Hui would later say, ‘I’ve never seen a human play this move. So 
beautiful. ’  68    
 Beautiful, beautiful, beautiful – it was a word that Hui kept 
repeating.  69   What this move challenges is the very nature of 
creativity. Moreover, it has also served to fundamentally change 
our understanding of the game of Go, by introducing a number of 
previously unknown moves.  70   
 FIGURE  2.4 Lee Sedol playing in the fourth match of the DeepMind 
Challenge Match against DeepMind’s artifi  cial intelligence program 
AlphaGo, on 13 March 2016 in Seoul, South Korea. Photo supplied by 
Getty. (515358446). 
A BRIEF HISTORY OF AI 59
 And Lee?  He gets up and walks out of the room  . For a moment it’s 
unclear what’s happening, but then he re-enters the game room, 
newly composed, sits down, and plays his response. What follows 
is a much closer game than Game 1, but the outcome remains the 
same. Lee Sedol resigns after 211 moves.  71   
  
 It was this move that made Lee realise that AlphaGo could indeed be 
creative. As European Go champion Fan Hui comments, ‘When 
AlphaGo chose that move, I assumed that it had made a mistake. 
I immediately looked to see Lee’s reaction. At ﬁ  rst, he seemed to 
smile – as though he too thought it had made a mistake – but as the 
minutes rolled by it was clear that he was starting to realise its 
brilliance. In fact, after the match, he said that when he saw this 
move he ﬁ  nally realised that AlphaGo was creative. ’  72   
 Hassabis goes even further: 
  Anyone can play an original move on a Go board by simply playing 
randomly. Yet a move can only be considered truly creative if it’s 
also effective. In that sense, move 37’s decisive role in game two 
represents a move of exquisite computational ingenuity that not 
only changed the game of Go forever, but also came to symbolise 
the enormous creative potential of AI.  73    
 AlphaGo deployed a long-term strategy. It made a number of moves 
that were initially dismissed by experts as ‘slack moves’ in that at ﬁ  rst 
they did not seem to make any sense. In the longer term, however, it 
became clear that these ‘slack moves’ had actually been carefully 
thought through and were setting the scene for a series of subsequent 
– and strategically devastating – moves. Indeed, such was the 
brilliance of AlphaGo’s play that Lee even began to question the 
creativity of moves made by humans that were previously thought to 
be creative: ‘ AlphaGo showed us that moves humans may have 
thought are creative, were actually conventional. ’  74   
 With that one comment, Lee forces us to reassess human 
creativity. Are we human beings quite as creative as we like to think 
we are? 
 As might be expected, DeepMind has since developed a series of 
iterations that further enhance the performance of its Go-playing 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 60
computers.  75   There followed a series of other models, AlphaGo Master 
and AlphaGo Zero, each improving on the previous best model. 
AlphaGo Master went on to win sixty straight games against 
professional Go players within a one-week period. Not to be outdone, 
AlphaGo Zero reached the level of AlphaGo Master within twenty-one 
days and was able to beat the previous best version of AlphaGo 100-0 
at the game of Go.  76   What is amazing about AlphaGo Zero is the fact 
that it taught itself to play Go without being given any prior knowledge, 
foreshadowing the emergent abilities developed by LLMs. Equally 
amazing is the speed at which it taught itself to play Go, which is 
beyond human comprehension. AlphaGo Zero trained itself in just 
three days by playing 4.9 million games of Go against itself.  77   That is a 
rate of almost twenty games of Go per second. This is truly astonishing. 
 DeepMind then developed an all-purpose model, AlphaZero, that 
could play other games, such as chess and Shogi. In December 2017 , 
AlphaZero beat the three-day version of AlphaGo Zero at Go by sixty 
games to forty. It has also beaten a top Shogi program, at Shogi, and 
Stockﬁ  sh, a top chess program, at chess.  78   More recently, AlphaStar 
has achieved grandmaster status at Starcraft 2, a video game that is 
reputed to be even more complex than Go, in that some of the 
opponent’s pieces are hidden from view.  79   Alphastar uses multi-agent 
reinforcement learning. 
 What became of Lee himself? He retired from professional Go in 
November 2019 on the basis that he would never be able to beat AI 
in the future: ‘This is an entity that cannot be defeated. ’  80   After 
conceding defeat in the match, Lee eventually decides to also 
concede defeat in his career. 
 Is this a presage of the future for the architectural profession?  
   Sputnik moment  
 A match of Go taking place in South Korea involving a professional 
player largely unknown in the West, playing a game almost unheard 
of in the West, could hardly be expected to create much of a stir, 
except perhaps within the AI community. It could be argued, however, 
that this match proved to be one of the most signiﬁ  cant events in the 
entire history of AI. 
A BRIEF HISTORY OF AI 61
 In China, AlphaGo’s triumph over Lee was an event that triggered 
a huge interest in AI. After all, the game of Go was invented in China 
more than 3,000 years ago and remains hugely popular there. The 
Chinese nation was clearly transﬁ  xed by the match. There was an 
estimated worldwide television audience of 280 million for this event, 
a signiﬁ  cant proportion of which was in China.  81   And the victory of 
AlphaGo over Lee sent a seismic shock through the whole nation.  82   
 The impact of the match on China should not be understated. It 
was a huge wake-up call for them. As Lee notes, ‘Overnight, China 
plunged into an artiﬁ  cial intelligence fever. ’  83   In what has been 
described by Kai-Fu Lee as China’s ‘Sputnik Moment’, the Chinese 
government realised after the match that it was lagging behind the 
West in a technology that held vast potential.  84   The original ‘sputnik 
moment’ was, of course, the moment when the United States 
was jolted into action in the Space Race, after being seriously 
embarrassed by the success of the Soviet Union in launching a 
satellite into space.  85   
 The Chinese government promptly decided to initiate an ambitious 
and rapid programme of investment in AI.  86   On 18 October 2017 – a 
year and a half after the match – Chinese president Xi Jinping 
announced a new plan for China to invest in AI with a view to 
overtaking the United States in AI development by 2030.  87   Kai Fu-Lee 
astutely observes, ‘If AlphaGo was China’s Sputnik moment, the 
government’s AI plan was like President John F . Kennedy’s landmark 
speech calling for America to land a man on the moon. ’  88   In fact some 
have argued that China has already surpassed the United States, if 
judged on the basis of the number of articles published on AI, and 
looks set to claim the top spot for most cited papers by 2025.  89   
 If, then, the original Sputnik moment in the United States led to 
the Space Race that was itself a manifestation of the Cold War, would 
not the second Sputnik moment in China lead to the AI race, which is 
itself a new form of Cold War? ‘No, ’ says Lee, ‘this is not a new Cold 
War. ’  90   Although AI can be used for military purposes, its true value 
lies in opening up to a new technology that will surely beneﬁ  t the 
whole of humanity. Instead of the Cold War, the comparison should 
be with the Industrial Revolution or the invention of electricity. But it 
most certainly was, as Lee so aptly observes, both a ‘game’ and a 
‘game changer’, at least as far as China was concerned.  91   
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 62
 The match also had a signiﬁ  cant impact on AI funding in South 
Korea. On 17 March – just two days after the contest – the South 
Korean government pledged 1 trillion won ($863 million) for research 
in AI over the next ﬁ  ve years. South Korean president Park Geun-Hye 
expressed her gratitude for the match: ‘ Above all, Korean society is 
ironically lucky, that thanks to the “ AlphaGo shock” , we have learned 
the importance of AI before it is too late. ’  92    
   The other Sputnik moment  
 As far as the world as a whole is concerned, however, a more 
signiﬁ  cant Sputnik moment happened with the launch of ChatGPT on 
30 November 2022. ChatGPT immediately took the world by storm, 
becoming a viral sensation, as the whole world began to realise the 
extraordinary potential of AI. By January 2023 ChatGPT had become 
the fastest growing app of all time.  93   The rest, as we know, is history. 
ChatGPT has been credited with starting the AI boom.  94   
 Indeed, the launch of ChatGPT has been explicitly described as a 
Sputnik moment. For example, at Bletchley Park – where, it could be 
argued, it all began – Michelle Donelan, UK Secretary of State for 
Science, Innovation and Technology, described its launch as ‘a Sputnik 
moment in humanity’s history. ’  95   
 But the ChatGPT Sputnik moment was a Sputnik moment with a 
difference. It had a far broader and deeper repercussions than the 
AlphaGo Sputnik moment. Not only did ChatGPT have a signiﬁ  cant 
impact internationally, presumably representing a signiﬁ  cant setback 
for China in terms of its competition with the United States, but it 
also triggered intense internal competition within both China and the 
United States. 
 In China, it led to what has been described as a ‘war of a hundred 
models’, with Tencent competing with Baidu, Alibaba and Huawei to 
develop the ﬁ  rst Chinese LLM.  96   Likewise, in the States, it fed 
existing competition between some of the leading tech companies. 
It forced Mark Zuckerberg, who had been reorienting Meta around 
the Metaverse, focusing on virtual reality (VR) and augmented reality 
(AR), to reorganise his company around a future based on AI.  97   It also 
A BRIEF HISTORY OF AI 63
provoked Elon Musk, who had previously walked away from OpenAI, 
to create his own AI company, xAI.  98   
 However, it has been its impact on Google that has proved to be 
perhaps the most signiﬁ  cant. Google was immediately put on the back 
foot, and forced to reappraise its operations. Within nine days, Google 
– a company with a reputation for extreme caution – had decided to 
change strategy, and push ahead with the launch of its own chatbot, 
Bard, powered by Google’s own LLM, PaLM 2.  99   On 6 February 2023 
– the day before Microsoft was to unveil its new AI-improved search 
engine, Bing – Google announced Bard. Unfortunately, Google made a 
mistake in one of the initial promotional videos for Bard. This was read 
by many as implying that the launch had been rushed in order to 
upstage Microsoft. Whether or not this was the case, Alphabet shares 
plummeted by $100 billion almost overnight as a result.  100   
 The launch of ChatGPT, however, has also triggered a secondary area 
of competition over search engines. Traditionally, Google has enjoyed 
the lion’s share of the market in terms of search engines, and has held a 
signiﬁ  cant advantage over its nearest rival, Microsoft’s Bing.  101   There has 
been speculation that AI – for better or worse – might eventually replace 
search engines.  102   Indeed, Larry Page, co-founder of Google, has even 
claimed that AI aspires to be the ultimate search engine: 
  Artiﬁ  cial Intelligence would be the ultimate version of Google. The 
ultimate search engine that would understand everything on the 
web. It would understand exactly what you wanted, and it would 
give you the right thing. We’re nowhere near doing that now. 
However, we can get incrementally closer to that, and that is 
basically what we work on.  103    
 However, with the effective acquisition of OpenAI, Microsoft is now 
in a position to threaten Google’s hegemony. By enhancing Bing with 
ChatGPT, Microsoft would be capable of providing actual answers, 
instead of simply searching for useful links. Meanwhile, Google’s 
Bard is designed not to be a search engine, but to operate in tandem 
with Google’s search engine, generating information and interesting 
ideas.  104   The competition is therefore clear – Microsoft’s Bing 
enhanced by ChatGPT versus Google’s search engine used in tandem 
with Bard. And Microsoft, it would seem, has stolen a march on 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 64
Google, in unveiling the world’s ﬁ  rst AI-enhanced search engine.  105   
But what of Google? As Jack Krawczyk comments, Google has its 
own ambitions in the ﬁ  eld: ‘We’ll also be thoughtfully integrating 
LLMs into Search in a deeper way – more to come. ’  106   
 ChatGPT, then, has had consequences well beyond its actual 
launch. It has provoked a wholesale reorientation in global markets, 
and has fuelled intense competition between tech companies, both 
nationally and internationally.  
   Press Pause  
 The race is on. As Sundar Pichai, the CEO of Google, notes, progress 
is happening at a lightning speed: ‘Today, the scale of the largest AI 
computations is doubling every six months, far outpacing Moore’s 
Law. ’  107   Is this necessarily good news for humanity? For some of the 
leaders in the ﬁ  eld, it is a source of growing concern. 
 In March 2023, the Future of Life Institute was so concerned about 
the speed of development of LLMs and the potential risks associated 
with them that it issued a letter: 
   We call on all AI labs to immediately pause for at least 6 
months the training of AI systems more powerful than GPT-4  . 
This pause should be public and veriﬁ  able, and include all key 
actors. If such a pause cannot be enacted quickly, governments 
should step in and institute a moratorium.  
 AI Labs and independent experts should use this pause to 
jointly develop and implement a set of shared safety protocols for 
advanced AI design and development that are rigorously audited 
and overseen by independent outside experts. These protocols 
should ensure that systems adhering to them are safe beyond a 
reasonable doubt. This does  not  mean a pause on AI development 
in general, merely a stepping back from the dangerous race to ever-
larger unpredictable black-box models with emergent capabilities.  108   
 Importantly, the petition explicitly refers to ‘emergent capabilities’ 
as being a cause of concern.  109   These are the capabilities of the ‘digital 
minds’ that ‘no one – not even their creators – can understand, predict 
A BRIEF HISTORY OF AI 65
or control’. The concern here, presumably, is that we have detected 
some obvious capabilities that these LLMs have developed, but that 
there are likely to be others that we do not know about. 
 Signatories included leading AI authorities, such as Elon Musk, 
Steve Wozniak and Yoshua Bengio, and other signiﬁ  cant ﬁ  gures, such 
as politician Andrew Yang, and academic historian/philosopher Yuval 
Noah Harari. Noble as its intentions were, however, the petition was 
doomed to failure. The companies leading the development of LLMs 
– such as OpenAI and Google – could hardly be expected to pause 
research in the face of intense rivalry. It would be somewhat like 
asking the leaders in the Olympic marathon to slow down, thereby 
allowing every other competitor to catch up. 
 There is, moreover, a problem in that it is almost impossible to enforce 
any such mandate. After all, if the international community is incapable of 
preventing Russian president Vladimir Putin from invading Ukraine, how 
could it prevent him – or anyone else for that matter – from continuing to 
develop LLMs. However, the key problem, surely, is that competition is 
inherent to capitalism, and when competition is driven primarily by 
economic factors, deeper ethical concerns are likely to be overlooked. As 
Geoffrey Hinton points out, ‘If you’re going to live in a capitalist system, 
you can’t stop Google [from] competing with Microsoft. ’  110   With Musk 
changing sides and establishing his own supercomputer, Colossus, it is 
clear that the competition has only intensiﬁ  ed. 
 ‘Is AI the new Space Race?’ 
 The history of AI has been a history of competition. AI was born 
largely of conﬂ  ict, through the work of Turing on the Nazi Enigma code 
during the Second World War, and developed through research funded 
by DARPA and other military organisations during the Cold War; the 
history of AI has also been marked by intense rivalry between different 
approaches to AI, and different software companies; and the 
marketing of AI has given rise to a series of literal competitions: Deep 
Blue versus Garry Kasparov, and AlphaGo versus Lee Sedol. Today 
there is intense national and international competition over AI. 
 Is AI the new Space Race?        
 David Alf, Meet me at the bar (2024). Open-air bar scene, generated using 
MidJourney V6.0.

68
               3  
 Image generation            
   A lthough diffusion models, such as MidJourney, DALL -E, Stable 
Diffusion and LookX, have now become synonymous with AI-
based image generation, these are extremely recent developments. 
They are the latest in a series of increasingly sophisticated techniques 
for image generation.  1   However, even image generation has a pre-
history – albeit a relatively short one. Paradoxically, the development 
of image generation techniques was dependent on advances in 
image recognition.  2   
 Interestingly, the movies predicted image recognition. Back in 
1968, for example,  2001: A Space Odyssey   (1968) depicted Hal 9000, 
the ship’s AI, lip reading and having facial recognition capabilities. 
However, it was not until the deep learning revolution that image 
recognition took off. Indeed, although facial recognition has been 
incorporated into most personal devices, as recently as 2012, image 
recognition was still undergoing early development.  
   Image Recognition  
 A crucial breakthrough in image recognition was made with the 
discovery by Fei-Fei Li, now one of the leading voices in AI, but then 
a young researcher at Princeton, that it would be possible to use 
principles adopted in the lexical database WordNet to create 
ImageNet, an image-based processing system.  3   Whereas WordNet 
was based on nouns, ImageNet exploited the system by linking 
nouns to images that contained examples of those nouns.  4   
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 70
 In fact, if we were to identify the precise moment when image 
recognition came of age, it would be the moment when – to the 
surprise of many – a team including Alex Krizhevsky, Ilya Sutskever 
and Geoffrey Hinton won the 2012 ImageNet challenge by a signiﬁ  cant 
margin, using their deep convolutional neural network system, 
AlexNet.  5   
 The result was astonishing. They had won the competition by a 
mile using what had previously been regarded as a completely 
obsolete technology. The impact of this moment cannot be overstated. 
As Li opines: 
 What was most startling about the submission was how it did 
what it did. Despite the decades of development and widespread 
interest in the modern algorithms that had won in the previous 
two years, its authors chose to bring the neural network back from 
the dead – and were absolutely  steamrolling   the competition with 
it. Second place wasn’t even close . . .  A 10-percentage-point leap? 
In a single year? And from a neural network?   . . . It was like being 
told that the land speed record had been broken by a margin of a 
hundred miles per hour in a Honda Civic.  6   
 From an architectural perspective, it was also a highly signiﬁ  cant 
moment in that it also paved the way for image generation – a 
challenge long considered the holy grail by AI researchers. For once 
an image can be recognised, the next step is to generate one.  
   DeepDream  
 Although computational neural networks are trained to discriminate 
between images, they need to have some understanding of those 
images. And this is what allows them to also generate images, when 
operating in reverse. 
 DeepDream is one of the earliest computer vision programs that 
uses ConvNets for generating images. It was developed by Alex 
Mordvintsev of Google Artists and Machine Intelligence (AMI) and 
released in 2015.  7   DeepDream reverses the ﬂ  ow of information in a 
neural network – also referred to as ‘inverting the network’ – in order 
to generate images. Instead of starting with an image and then 
IMAGE GENERATION 71
categorising it, DeepDream can be used to start with a category and 
then generate an image.  8   For example, whereas a standard neural 
network can recognise an image of a cat, and categorise it as a ‘cat’, 
DeepDream is able to start with the category ‘cat’ and generate an 
image that resembles a cat. Instead of operating ‘from image to 
media’, then, DeepDream operates ‘from media to image’. 
 However, DeepDream often produces somehat ‘trippy’ images 
that appear vaguely surrealistic with a multiplicity of objects generated 
in a variety of poses.  9   The reason behind the aggregation of poses is 
that a trained classiﬁ  cation network has learnt a many-to-one mapping 
technique, in that multiple images can be categorised, for example, 
as a ‘bird’. In this process it discards unimportant pixel information to 
make the classiﬁ  cation prediction. As a result there is insufﬁ  cient 
information for it to shift to a one-to-many mapping. The system is 
therefore incapable of generating a realistic image of a ‘bird’.  10    
   Generative adversarial networks  
 The next important step in the history of image generation, came 
with the invention of generative adversarial networks (GANs). These 
were ﬁ  rst proposed by Ian Goodfellow in 2014, but took a few years 
   FIGURE  3.1 Martin Thomas, Aurelia Aurita, DeepDream generated 
image (2015). DeepDream image generated after fi  fty iterations by a 
neural network trained to perceive dogs.         
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 72
to become popular, and have since undergone rapid development.  11   
GANs became one of the most popular early techniques for image 
generation, until the introduction of diffusion models. 
 A GAN is a technique for training a computer to perform complex 
tasks through a generative process measured against a set of training 
images. It represents a major breakthrough in the quest to synthesise 
images and overcomes the problem of objects appearing in a variety 
of poses that compromises DeepDream. It also generates images 
with signiﬁ  cantly better resolution. 
 A GAN is based on a competition between two different neural 
networks. It consists of a bottom-up generator – or ‘artist’ – that 
generates images, and a top-down discriminator – or ‘critic’ – that 
evaluates those images. In the competition, the generator attempts 
to fool the discriminator by producing images so realistic that the 
discriminator would be unable to distinguish them from a real data 
set. Mishak Navak describes the process as follows: 
 [A] Generator (an artist) generates an image. The Generator does 
not know anything about the real images and learns by interacting 
with the Discriminator. [The] discriminator (an art critic) determines 
whether an object is ‘ real ’ and ‘ fake ’ . . . The Generator keeps 
creating new images and reﬁ  ning its process until the Discriminator 
can no longer tell the difference between the generated images 
and the real training images.  12   
   FIGURE  3.2 Diagram illustrating the workings of a generative 
adversarial network (GAN). One way to think of the operation of a 
GAN would be the contest between an art forger trying to produce 
a convincing work of art, and an art expert trying to ascertain whether 
or not the work is fake.         
IMAGE GENERATION 73
 The two work in tandem and improve over time, so that the ‘artist’ 
trains the ‘critic’, and the ‘critic’ trains the ‘artist’. Once the ‘artist’ has 
been trained, the ‘critic’ can be removed. 
 The invention of GANs has since led to an extraordinary explosion of 
research and the development of many different versions, with their 
outputs becoming ever more reﬁ  ned.  13   GANs build upon the 
developments of DeepDream in interesting new ways. And although 
they are not without their problems, the standard has improved 
substantially in the relatively few years since GANs were ﬁ  rst 
developed. 
 The Progressive Growing of GANs (ProGAN), for example, 
increases the resolution of the image, layer by layer.  14   This allows the 
whole process to speed up, producing far greater realism than 
previously achieved. However, there is limited control of certain 
features in the generated image, leading to problems of ‘entanglement’ 
whereby any slight tweak or amendment to one feature has a knock-
on effect on the next. 
 A StyleGAN offers further improvements in terms of resolution 
and quality, by starting with very low resolution images and gradually 
increasing the resolution.  15   It treats an image as a collection of 
different ‘styles’, whereby each ‘style’ controls the effect at a 
particular scale, whether it be a coarse ‘style’, such as pose, hair or 
face shape, a middle ‘style’, such as eyes and other facial features, or 
a ﬁ  ne ‘style’ as in colour. This allows a StyleGAN to overcome the 
problem of ‘entanglement’ by reducing the correlation between 
different features. As it learns, the system is able to morph certain 
details, such as hairstyle or age, far more convincingly, by isolating 
speciﬁ  c features and playing them off against each other. Indeed, 
StyleGAN can generate artiﬁ  cial faces so convincing that it is often 
almost impossible to tell them apart from real faces. There is even a 
website that automatically generates highly realistic novel faces.  16   
Perhaps the greatest advantage of this technique, however, is that 
images can simply be processed, without being tagged or classiﬁ  ed, 
thereby saving considerable time. 
 A further version of GANs popular among artists and architects is 
a CycleGAN. This works with unpaired datasets, and allows for cross-
domain transfers between dataset A and dataset B.  17   The network 
has to decide how to transfer concepts between the two datasets. A 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 74
good example of CycleGAN image-to-image translation is the 
mapping of the striped patterning of a zebra onto an image of a 
horse.  18   Although often referred to as ‘style transfer’, in fact what this 
process does is to extract and transfer certain key features from one 
domain to another.  19   
 Usually with a GAN there is one generator and one discriminator, but 
with a CycleGAN there are two generators and two discriminators. The 
advantage of a CycleGAN is that it avoids the possibility of ‘mode 
collapse’ common with StyleGANs, where the generator is not 
generating anything new, but the discriminator cannot complain about 
the output as the result is ‘true’.  20   It is therefore capable of learning 
special characteristics from one dataset and ﬁ  guring out how to translate 
them onto the other dataset, without needing paired training examples.  21   
 Creative adversarial networks (CANs) are yet another version of 
GANs developed to generate art. CANs introduce a new strategy to 
AI-generated artworks. With other forms of GANs, the initial dataset 
will inevitably constrain the search space from which any output 
might be generated. In other words, although any output will generate 
an inﬁ  nite number of variations – seemingly a kind of ‘blending’ or 
   FIGURE  3.3 Daniel Bolojan, Machine Perceptions: Gaud í  + Neural 
Networks (2020). This research project uses CycleGANs to generate 
images based on two unpaired datasets of images of the interior of the 
Sagrada Familia church in Barcelona, Spain, and a video of a walk 
through a forest.         
IMAGE GENERATION 75
‘averaging’ – of data in the dataset, nothing outside that dataset can 
be generated. With CANs, however, these constraints are relaxed 
and the possibility of a variation beyond the range of the dataset 
becomes possible.  22   The resultant work therefore will conform closely 
enough to accepted canons of art, while also offering a novel variation 
that is sufﬁ  ciently intriguing to stimulate interest.  23   
 Another area of development has been natural language 
processing. Here an image is generated from a text. One of the ﬁ  rst 
explorations of this idea came with the development of yet another 
GAN, AttnGAN, which allows ‘attention-driven, multi-stage 
reﬁ  nement for ﬁ  ne-grained text to image generation’.  24   AttnGAN 
starts off with a crude low-resolution image and then gradually 
improves it to generate what appears to be a 3D interpretation of a 
2D pattern, although it remains 2D. This has signiﬁ  cant advantages 
over other GAN models and potentially opens up the domain of 
natural language processing for image generation. 
 In the end, however, architects are visual creatures, and the main 
problem with GANs, as far as architects were concerned, is that they 
simply failed to deliver. What was required was a technique that 
would be much easier to use, that could generate convincing images 
much more quickly to a higher resolution and with the capacity for 
adding rendering techniques, customised lighting and many other 
design features. This technique was soon to emerge, and it would be 
a technique that would set the architectural world ablaze.  
   Diff  usion models  
 A huge breakthrough in image generation came with the development 
of diffusion models. These are pretrained neural networks that have 
the capacity to generate scalable images within a matter of seconds, 
with a complex background, depth of ﬁ  eld effects, shading and even 
reﬂ  ections. 
 The name ‘diffusion model’ alludes to two different ‘diffusion’ 
processes that it employs to generate image.  25   The ﬁ  rst process, 
called ‘forward diffusion’, uses a Markov chain of diffusion steps, 
where each step adds Gaussian noise to input data, progressively 
corrupting the data. The second process, called ‘reverse diffusion’, 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 76
effectively reverses that process in order to recover the original data, 
thereby generating novel and diverse outcomes.  26   
 Broadly speaking, diffusion models are ‘text-to-image’ models that 
operate using a ‘prompt’ – or verbal description – written in natural 
language as their input, that they then convert into a corresponding 
image as their output. This process does not involve searching the 
internet for links between images and captions; instead, it is based 
on training data consisting of images labelled with captions.  27   A s  a  
result, the network ‘learns’ the relationship between text and images. 
A simple text prompt can then be used to generate many different 
images. It is less a question of ‘a picture paints a thousand words’ 
than ‘a prompt can generate a thousand images’. 
 Diffusion models are very easy to use. In the case of MidJourney, 
the initial step is to generate – or ‘imagine’ – four separate images 
based on a text prompt. The user can then either upscale or generate 
variations of any of these initial outcomes, so as to produce a second 
generation of images, which in turn may become the basis for 
subsequent generations. This process is called ‘iteration’, and can be 
continued for as long as the user is interested, allowing that user to 
gradually reﬁ  ne the outcomes. Iteration allows a designer to coax the 
outcomes in a particular direction, although never allowing the user 
full control of the process.  28   This could be compared, perhaps, to the 
way in which an instructor might advise a student, while leaving the 
student in charge of the overall process. Or it might be compared to 
the way in which the principal of an architectural ofﬁ  ce might instruct 
an employee on how to develop a project. 
 This process also allows for a process of differentiation, where 
one original seed can be developed in a variety of different directions. 
Think of this a little like a ‘family tree’. Effectively a designer can 
generate several branches of that same tree – like cousins – all 
bearing some familial relationship towards each other, but each 
developing in its own direction.  
   Prompt engineering  
 Careful attention needs to be paid to the precise wording of the prompt 
itself. This ﬁ  eld is referred to as ‘prompt engineering’. Text-to-image 
IMAGE GENERATION 77
prompts are similar to text-to-text prompts in that they both employ 
natural language. However, whereas text-to-text prompts consist of 
complete sentences, text-to-image prompts are divided into a series 
of short verbal descriptions, separated by commas. The order of these 
verbal descriptions is very important. The ﬁ  rst one has the greatest 
weight, although with some models it is possible to adjust the weights. 
 Clearly, ‘prompt engineering’ is becoming an increasingly 
important skill. And yet it is perhaps not as exacting a science as the 
term ‘engineering’ would seem to suggest. Diffusion models can 
appear somewhat temperamental. Change one word, and the whole 
outcome can be completely different. 
 One reason for this is that – like LLMs – diffusion models appear 
to be ‘associative’ in their logic, in the sense that any term in a prompt 
will carry its own associations. Thus, for example, the name of a 
   FIGURE  3.4 Giovanna Pillaca, Futuristic Temple in India, (2021). Image 
generated using CLIP and VQGAN, based on the prompts ‘Futuristic 
Temple in India’ and the pre-prompts ‘Ma Yansong’, ‘Morphosis 
Architects’ and ‘Wolf Prix’.         
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 78
particular architect or artist is likely to include associations with the 
environment – the ﬂ  ora, fauna, topography, weather and even the 
quality of light – of their countries. A reference to the Mexican 
architect Luis Barragan, for example, will tend to generate images 
that draw upon not only the colours, but also forms employed by him, 
and likewise the trees, landscape, quality of light and other aspects 
associated with Mexico. Equally, ‘house extension’ – a term used 
primarily in the United Kingdom – is likely to generate buildings 
looking distinctly British, accompanied by an overcast sky.  
   A brief history of diff  usion models  
 In January 2021, OpenAI launched DALL -E, a version of GPT-3 trained 
to generate images from textual descriptions. The name DALL -E is a 
combination of Salvador Dal í , the famous Spanish surrealist painter, 
and Wall-E, the Pixar character, signifying its dual ability to create 
surreal images, and generate them from text. DALL -E immediately 
created quite a stir, generating some highly novel and surprising 
outcomes, such as ‘an avocado inspired chair’. The problem with 
DALL -E, however, was that it was not fully open source, in that 
although users could interact with it, the underlying code and training 
data were not made publicly available, thereby limiting its use within 
the design community. Nonetheless, alongside the release of 
DALL -E, OpenAI introduced Contrastive Language-Image Pre-
Training (CLIP), an open source multimodal vision-and-language 
model. CLIP is capable of understanding and generating text 
descriptions of images, as well as performing tasks like image 
classiﬁ  cation based on natural language prompts. 
 Soon after, a new technique emerged combining CLIP with Vector 
Quantized GAN (VQGAN), a neural network architecture for image 
generation.  29   This integration of CLIP and VQGAN models, referred 
to as CLIP-guided VQ-VAE-2, was capable of generating images 
from text prompts effectively. For their time, the results were often 
relatively convincing and represented a signiﬁ  cant advancement in 
text-to-image models over straightforward GANs. 
 This, however, was just the beginning. There followed a ﬂ  urry of 
activity. In April 2022, Open AI announced DALL -E 2, a diffusion 
IMAGE GENERATION 79
model that was an updated and much improved version of the original 
DALL -E, although access was again limited initially to a select group 
of established AI users. In June 2022, OpenAI eventually made 
DALL -E 2 available to the public. Then, in October 2023, DALL -E 3 
was released into ChatGPT for ChatGPT Plus and ChatGPT Enterprise 
customers. 
 MidJourney followed a parallel trajectory. MidJourney V1 was 
launched initially in Beta mode in February 2022 for a very restricted 
group of users, with MidJourney V2 Beta released in April 2022. From 
July 2022, it entered ‘open Beta’, meaning that it was still in Beta 
mode, but virtually anyone could use it. There have now been a 
succession of different versions released, with MidJourney V6 Beta 
released in December 2023, representing a signiﬁ  cant improvement. 
   FIGURE  3.5 Aaron Hertzmann, Housing project by Olafur Eliasson, 
(2022). This is one of the earliest architectural images generated using 
D A L L - E 2 .          
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 80
 Meanwhile, in June 2022, Google also launched an autoregressive 
image generation model, Pathways Autogressive Text-to-Image 
(PARTI). Then, Stable Diffusion was launched in August 2022, and 
released in August 2023. And LookX, the ﬁ  rst diffusion model using 
Stable Diffusion and trained speciﬁ  cally on architectural data, was 
launched at the end of June 2023. 
Effectively, 2022 was the year in which most of these diffusion 
models – and many others besides – were launched. It is the year, 
therefore,   that marks the beginning of the ‘ AI Spring’ for image 
generation. Soon after these diffusion models began to attract a 
signiﬁ  cant following, especially within the design community, not 
least because they are so simple, quick and easy to use. They have 
now become mainstream. 
   FIGURE  3.6 Neil Leach,  Zaha Hadid Inspired Interior   (2022), generated 
using MidJourney V 5.3. This was generated using one of the earlier 
versions of MidJourney, and lacks the defi  nition of images generated by 
later versions.         
IMAGE GENERATION 81
 Although these models are associated with image generation for 
now, in the future the principle could be extended to accommodate 
anything that can be encoded in some enormous multidimensional 
space. What began with DALL -E and MidJourney has mushroomed 
into an industry. We have already seen other outcomes, such as 
videos, video games and much more, being generated through 
prompt engineering, and we can expect to see further developments 
in the future. What is beyond doubt, however, is that diffusion models 
have revolutionised AI-based image generation.  
   Diff  usion models vs GANs  
 A diffusion model operates in a very different way to a GAN. It is 
not trained on a speciﬁ  c dataset, but rather draws upon a massive,    FIGURE  3.7 Massimo Russo, Vision of industrial spaces of the future 
(2024), generated using Stable Diffusion.         

ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 82
pre-trained dataset of labelled images. The dataset is diverse and 
generic in nature, and typically unrelated to any speciﬁ  c task that the 
model will later be used for. It therefore gains a broad understanding 
of patterns and structures within data, making it more versatile. 
 A diffusion model has a number of signiﬁ  cant advantages over a 
GAN: 
     1   Diffusion models are already pre-trained, and there is no need 
to undertake the often laborious process of collecting and 
curating large amounts of data for training purposes.  
    2   The process of image generation with a diffusion model is far 
quicker than with a GAN, often lasting just a few seconds.  
    3   Generating images using a diffusion model does not require 
any advanced understanding of computational methods.  
    4   The quality and resolution of the outcomes of diffusion 
models is signiﬁ  cantly higher than those of GAN based 
models.   
 In short, diffusion models are capable of generating high-quality, 
diverse outcomes easily and effortlessly, while avoiding some of the 
challenges and limitations associated with traditional GAN-based 
models.  
   Evaluating leading diff  usion models  
 There is an enormous range of diffusion models on the market, but it 
is impossible to cover them all. For the sake of brevity, then, let us 
take a look at the models most popular with designers: MidJourney, 
DALL -E and Stable Diffusion. But for the architectural community 
especially, let us also include LookX, which has been customised for 
an architectural market. 
 According to the journal  Everypixel  , Stable Diffusion has 10 million 
users, MidJourney 15 million registered users (but only 1 .5–2.5 million 
active users), and DALL -E has 1 .5 million users. In terms of the 
number of images generated, DALL -E 2 generates 916 million, 
MidJourney 964 million, but it is estimated that there are 12.590 
IMAGE GENERATION 83
billion models generated by Stable Diffusion.  30   However, these 
ﬁ  gures are distorted by the fact that Stable Diffusion is not only free 
to use but also open source. This means that the amount of images 
produced is not limited to sites owned by Stability AI, and appears 
hugely inﬂ  ated compared to the others in the sample. LookX also 
uses Stable Diffusion. At the time of writing, LookX has only 100,000 
registered users, but this is not an insigniﬁ  cant ﬁ  gure, given that it 
was not launched until 2023, and almost all users are likely to be 
architects. 
 It is less easy to compare usefulness. In any case, with 
developments happening on a daily basis in the ﬁ  eld of AI, any 
comparison would soon appear out of date. However, it is certainly fair 
to say that diffusion models have largely eclipsed GAN models in the 
ﬁ  eld of image generation. It is also fair to say that – at the time of 
writing – commentary on the internet suggests that MidJourney is the 
platform of choice, especially among artists and architects. It produces 
‘lush vivid images that resemble paintings or photos’, while DALL -E’s 
outputs are more ‘cartoon-like’; meanwhile, although it is a little 
awkward in that it operates through the Discord social media channel, 
MidJourney is less complicated to use than Stable Diffusion, which 
has a lot of customisation options, and has a steep learning curve.  31   
 LookX has two signiﬁ  cant advantages over other generic models. 
First, it has been trained speciﬁ  cally on architectural data labelled 
   FIGURE  3.8 Tim Fu, Crumpled Paper Renderings (2023), generated 
using LookX Render Mode. Here LookX is used to render an image of 
crumpled paper into designs by ZHA and Gehry + Partners.         
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 84
using architectural terminology. This means that it will not only have a 
far better understanding of architectural semantics, but will also 
recognise the names of many architects and respond to architectural 
terms, in a way that generic diffusion models could never do. As He 
comments, ‘If you ask MidJourney to create an unusually shaped 
building, the results would be very abstract and uncontrollable, pretty 
impossible to be built in reality, because the AI has not been trained 
in architectural design language. ’  32   Secondly, in addition to its ‘Explore 
Mode’ for form exploration, it also has a ‘Render Mode’ as a ﬁ  lter for 
architectural rendering. This allows even a relatively crude sketch to 
be interpreted as a fully rendered building. Likewise, in a more 
experimental manner, it can ‘architecturalise’ ordinary images. The 
impact of Render Mode should not be underestimated. It is likely to 
both prove a game changer in the ﬁ  eld of architectural design, and 
challenge the existence of the profession of rendering.  
   Redesigning design  
 Diffusion models would appear to feeding existing trends in a culture 
that has become increasingly dominated, it would seem, by strategies 
of ‘searching’ or ‘sampling’. 
 Take photography, for example. In the days of manual photography 
we would carefully set up the camera for one ideal shot. In the age of 
digital photography, however, we take a burst of  sample   shots. We 
then review them, select our favourite one, and enhance it using 
editing techniques available on every mobile phone. It is no longer a 
question of the expert photographer setting up the ideal shot, but 
rather of the informed user taking an array of  sample   photos and then 
selecting and enhancing the best one. 
 In the age of computational design, this process of ‘sampling’ or 
‘searching’ has also become the ﬁ  rst step in the design process. 
Instead of developing a single design, the tendency has now become 
to deﬁ  ne the constraints and conduct a search, in order to generate a 
range of possible outcomes. With Grasshopper this amounts to 
writing a deﬁ  nition and adjusting sliders to generate as many options 
as needed. The assumption here is that all possible solutions already 
exist, and that it is simply a question of searching for them: 
IMAGE GENERATION 85
  If we think through the logic of the search in the context of ‘design’, 
what such an approach suggests is that if all possible solutions 
already exist, it is simply a question of deﬁ  ning a set of constraints 
and conducting a search, and then selecting one of the many 
outcomes. The potential implications of this are far reaching. Not 
only does it challenge the traditional notion . . . but it also suggests 
that if there is still any creativity in the ‘design’ process, it should 
lie, ﬁ  rstly, in deﬁ  ning the constraints that generate the range of 
possible solutions to a problem, and, secondly, in developing an 
effective method of ﬁ  ltering or evaluating them.  33    
 This has major implications for the logic of design itself. The top-
down architect form maker of the past gives way to the architect as 
the controller of processes. This shifts the emphasis from the 
‘creativity’ of the designer towards the rigour of the search itself, and 
challenges the whole notion of the ‘creative genius’. 
 As a consequence of this, we need to rethink the terminology that 
we use. If the notion of a singular design is being replaced by a range 
of potential sample options, should we not refer to them as 
‘outcomes’? And if the notion of designing is to be replaced by a 
search for possible outcomes, should we not refer to this process as 
‘searching’? Surely the designer here is merely the instigator of a 
process, and the result of that process is more of an outcome than a 
design in the traditional sense. 
 The introduction of AI has begun to fuel this trend even further, 
allowing for a more exhaustive and comprehensive search of the 
solution space. In the traditional design process we tend to start by 
thinking about a range of possible solutions. But our own human 
biases often limit that range.  34   AI, however, helps us to expand the 
range of solutions.  35   In other words, AI exposes us to solutions that 
otherwise we would not have thought about. 
 Within architectural culture this increasing emphasis on the search 
is especially evident with diffusion models. We could perhaps use the 
analogy of the GAN to explain how a diffusion model effectively 
enhances the design process by generating more options. Let us not 
forget, of course, that – from a technical perspective – a GAN and a 
diffusion model operate in entirely different ways. Nonetheless, it 
could be useful to consider how the way in which a GAN operates – 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 86
through two competing neural networks, a ‘generator’ and a 
‘discriminator’ – to explain how a diffusion model enhances the 
creative process. 
 On the one hand, we could claim that human beings might not be 
as good at ‘generating’ images and ideas, as some would like to 
think, and that these diffusion models are capable of generating a far 
greater range of possible outcomes than any one individual could 
imagine. We therefore need these diffusion models to ‘augment’ 
our imagination like some form of ‘creative’ prosthesis. While we 
write prompts, do diffusion models not prompt us to be more 
imaginative? 
 On the other hand, we could argue that we human beings are 
actually highly effective ‘discriminators’. After all, we can judge 
whether or not we like a cocktail or a perfume almost instantaneously. 
The same goes for images. Indeed the architect’s eye remains one of 
the most valuable assets in the design process, and can be surprisingly 
quick at making evaluations. As a result, it is noticeable how some 
architects and designers deploy highly sophisticated software to 
generate possible outcomes, and then simply use their ‘eye’ to 
evaluate those outcomes. 
 We could therefore see the new ‘ AI-assisted designer’ as being 
someone combining the capacity for generating a far greater range of 
options – operating like a highly effective ‘generator’ in a GAN, with 
already acute critical skills – operating like a ‘discriminator’ in a GAN. 
It soon becomes clear that the ‘ AI-assisted designer’ is a far more 
effective model than a straight designer, and is likely to produce 
better designs. 
 As a result, a new kind of designer seems to be emerging. In the 
past, the best designer was generally the most imaginative designer, 
rather than the critic, who typically would have a good critical eye, but 
a less fertile imagination. However, with these diffusion models, the 
balance shifts. What is being privileged now is less the imagination of 
the designer than the ‘eye’ of the discerning critic. We could therefore 
speculate that designers of the future are likely to increasingly play 
the role of arbiters of taste or ‘discriminators’, and that critics and 
curators themselves could even become active designers. If so, this 
suggests that in the future AI will likely have an impact not only on 
the design process, but also on our very nature of the designer. 
IMAGE GENERATION 87
 We might also speculate that in the future our AI-generated 
designs are likely to become customised to our individual aesthetic 
outlook. At present, there is no effective mechanism to incorporate 
previous design decisions into the design process. However, this is 
likely to change. We can expect that algorithms will soon be tracking 
our every design decision, and recording them.  36   Just as our mobile 
phones know what news we prefer, Spotify knows what music we 
prefer, Pinterest knows what images we prefer and Amazon knows 
what kind of books we prefer, so AI will surely know exactly what 
designs we prefer, and might even prompt us to be more adventurous. 
 It is clear that in the age of AI the entire process of design will be 
radically reconﬁ  gured. Will we still keep the terms ‘design’ and 
‘designer’? Or will we adopt other terminology to describe the new 
form of practice and new kind of practitioner emerging?  
   Text to video  
 Text-to-video models started to appear in 2022, but made an 
astonishing breakthrough with the announcement of OpenAI’s Sora 
in February 2024.  37   Sora is capable of generating videos of up to a 
minute in length, while keeping faithful to the original prompt and 
maintaining a high level of visual quality. 
 Essentially Sora uses the same logic that OpenAI had developed 
for its GPT series of LLMs. The only difference is that it uses visual 
‘patches’ instead of word ‘tokens’.  38   
 Each frame, then, is broken down into patches, in the same way 
that words are broken down into tokens in training LLMs. This gives 
it the ﬂ  exibility to be trained on a wide range of different videos, in 
terms of their resolution, orientation and aspect ratio. As Open AI 
explains, ‘ At a high level, we turn videos into patches by ﬁ  rst 
compressing videos into a lower-dimensional latent space, and 
subsequently decomposing the representation into spacetime 
patches. ’  39   
 Like LLMs, Sora displays remarkable ‘emergent abilities’, not 
surprisingly, perhaps, given that it is based on the same logic. Perhaps 
the most remarkable of these is its ‘understanding’ of 3D form. That 
it not to say that it works fully in 3D at least not as yet. However, it 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 88
can also render 3D worlds while they are in motion, and have a sense 
of how things ‘ﬁ  t together’ in 3D.
