aesthetic concerns, and rinsing it of any economic or social and 
political considerations.  55   Why is it, for example, that many architects 
have a tendency to privilege design concerns over economic factors, 
even though economic factors are the driver of any design? Indeed, 
why are there so few references to economic considerations in books 
on architecture, apart from the cost of the book on the back cover? It 
is as though architects always tend to see the world with a certain 
gaze, as though through rose-tinted, aestheticising lenses. 
 It is important to understand, then, that the gaze of the architect is 
not neutral. It has been trained, no less than a neural network has 
been trained. Whether we understand this conditioned outlook in 
Seth’s terms as a form of ‘controlled hallucination’ or in Zizek’s terms 
as being constituted through ‘fantasy’, it is clear that architects see 
the world not as it is but as they are trained to see it. But is this not 
so dissimilar to the message behind the term ‘deconstruction’ coined 
by Derrida? 
 Derrida argues that our perception of the world carries with it 
certain biases, just as the data used in AI carries with it certain 
biases.  56   Our perception of the world is therefore ‘constructed’. And 
this ‘constructed’ perception is a distorted one, as we have seen with 
the standard interpretation of a rainbow. What needs to be exposed, 
then, is how our understanding of the world has been ‘constructed’. 
AI, MEDIA ART AND NEUROSCIENCE 135
And this ‘constructed’ way of understanding the world itself needs to 
be ‘deconstructed’. The same issue applies to architecture. 
 ‘Let us never forget, ’ writes Derrida, ‘that there is an architecture of 
architecture. ’  57   Put another way, our understanding of architecture is 
itself ‘constructed’, as we have seen with the standard perception of 
‘functionalism’ as referring to buildings with ﬂ  at roofs. Our understanding 
of architecture therefore needs to be ‘deconstructed’. The irony here is 
that architects largely misinterpret Derrida. They tend to think that the 
term ‘deconstruction’ refers in some way to the construction of 
architecture, whereas in fact it is simply an architectural metaphor.  58   
 What is this misinterpretation, then, but yet another example of 
architecturalisation?  
   Can machines dream?  
 Is Watanabe correct, then, to argue that machines do not have 
dreams?  59   Arguably, we could claim that machines can now ‘dream’. 
The term ‘dream’, after all, appears throughout Anadol’s descriptions 
of his work. It also appears in the name of the technique DeepDream, 
and the Autodesk software Dreamcatcher. We can also ﬁ  nd references 
to other dream-like ways of generating images, such as ‘hallucinations’. 
Mitchell, of course, argues that AI would require consciousness in 
order to appreciate the artistic quality of anything that it generates. 
Consciousness, for Mitchell, is therefore the hallmark of creativity.  60   
But do dreams themselves necessarily depend on consciousness? 
 According to Freud, dreams have many roles. For example, 
repressed feelings can be expressed in dreams, just as they can 
erupt involuntarily in speech through the process of  parapraxis  , or can 
be expressed in sublimated form in any creative act. Equally dreams 
offer a ‘royal road’ to the unconscious, as Freud puts it, as they give 
access to parts of the mind inaccessible during conscious thought.  61   
In other words, dreams themselves do  not  necessarily have anything 
to do with consciousness and can be present in sublimated form in 
the creative act of designing. Seen from this perspective, dreams do 
not depend on consciousness. 
 Alternatively, we could argue that in terms of the discourse of 
‘extended intelligence’, the generation of AI work does indeed involve 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 136
consciousness. It is just that the human being, and not AI, possesses 
consciousness. In fact we could even argue that this is always the 
case in any computational operation, in that the computer is never 
acting on its own. Ultimately the user remains in charge.  62   Despite 
the seemingly objective nature of the computational process, the 
output must always satisfy the subjective user. And if the output 
does not satisfy the user, then the algorithms can always be tweaked 
until they produce a result that  does   satisfy the user. 
 As such, it is almost meaningless to talk about AI in isolation. For 
the moment at least, it is always a question of a form of human–AI 
collaboration: less artiﬁ  cial intelligence per se, more ‘extended 
intelligence’. And, since the user has consciousness, by deﬁ  nition 
this collaboration itself involves consciousness. Seen in this light, it is 
simply a matter of the user dreaming  through   AI, or of the user 
adopting AI as an extension of the human imagination. Moreover, as 
many have pointed out, there is only one entity superior to AI, and 
that is ‘humans plus AI’. And clearly ‘humans plus AI’ are superior to 
humans without AI. Watanabe’s question, then, should not be ‘Can 
machines dream?’ so much as ‘Can humans use machines to enhance 
their own dreams?’ 
 If we put Watanabe’s comments in their full context, however, it is 
clear that this is actually what Watanabe means. For Watanabe, the 
secret is not to read AI in isolation, but in terms of a potential AI–
human symbiosis. Watanabe refers to AI in this context as a ‘capability 
expanding AI’ or an AI that ‘can expand the imagination’. 
 For Watanabe, the working of the brain – in other words, ‘intuition’ 
– is always a ‘black box’.  63   We cannot understand how it works. 
Meanwhile neural networks are also a ‘black box’.  64   
 In the case of deep learning, for example, even computer scientists 
don’t know quite  how  it works. This symbiosis between machines 
and humans, then, amounts to a form of ‘collaboration’ between two 
black boxes. Interestingly, Watanabe sees the potential of machines 
having dreams in terms not of a competition between humans and 
machines, but of a synthesis, where humans and machines 
complement each other: 
  Will this answer always be true? Will the day come when machines 
have dreams? Preparing for that day will involve exploring the path 
AI, MEDIA ART AND NEUROSCIENCE 137
of potential cooperation between the brain and machines. This will 
require work in both areas – white boxes, in other words, the 
scientiﬁ  c approach or algorithmic design, and black boxes, towards 
collaborative methodologies, or, in other words, AI Tect. This is 
similar to how our left and right brains handle different functions 
and collaborate in delivering an outstanding performance.  65    
 And so, do robots dream? And does it even make sense to think of 
robots in isolation? Or should we only be thinking of robots in 
collaboration with human beings? Watanabe, at any rate, is interested 
in this collaboration. Working with robots can help architects to dream 
up a far greater range of options than their own imagination might 
allow. Traditionally, we have seen robots as being mechanical devices 
that can only perform mechanical tasks. But working together with 
human beings, they can open up space for the imagination. For Michael 
Hansmeyer this is the crucial step: ‘We’ve been using computers to 
increase our efﬁ  ciency and precision. Let us view the computer as our 
muse, as a partner in design, and as a tool to expand our imagination. ’  66   
 In 2019 there was an exhibition on AI held at the Barbican in 
London, titled  AI: More than Human  .   67   The premise behind the 
exhibition was that the concept of AI has to be seen within a broader 
context as a continuation of a desire to artiﬁ  cially create intelligent 
life. The exhibition was certainly convincing in making this argument. 
 The title of the exhibition, however, appears to imply that AI could 
somehow be ‘more than human’. But is it a question of comparison? 
Should we see the relationship between AI and humans as being one 
of competition? Is this title not fear mongering? Or should we see the 
role of AI as being complementary to the role of humans? Isn’t one 
of the roles of AI to free us from the drudgery of certain repetitive 
operations and allow us to expand our own all-too-human imagination? 
Is the role of AI to allow us to become more human? 
 AI, be my muse!  
   In the mirror of AI  
 Or could we even see AI as a mirror in which to understand how our 
human minds work? In this chapter we have considered how humans, 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 138
no less than machines, hallucinate; we have also discussed how 
many human operations seem to serve as parallels to operations in 
the world of AI. We are trained to see the world in a certain way, no 
less than a neural network is trained. And processes, such as 
backpropagation, have parallels in the analogue world of the brain. 
 Indeed, digital simulations can often help us to understand analogue 
behaviours. For example, it wasn’t until Craig Reynolds had produced 
a digital model for the ﬂ  ocking behaviour of birds, using ‘boids’, that 
we were able to understand the behaviour of actual birds.  68   
 ‘Is AI a mirror in which to understand 
what it means to be human?’ 
 This is not to say that AI would serve as a ‘digital model’ of human 
intelligence. After all, both are black boxes, and we have little 
understanding of how either work. Nonetheless the principles that 
govern AI might offer some clues about the principles that govern 
human intelligence. We have already discussed how AI might help us 
understand the nature of creativity? Could AI also provide us with a 
mirror in which to understand human existence in general? 
  Blade Runner   is a movie primarily about the difference between 
human beings and replicants, such that replicants become a mirror in 
which to understand what it is to be human. As the late Rutger Hauer, 
who plays the leader of the replicants, Roy Batty, comments, ‘In 
many ways,  Blade Runner   wasn’t about the replicants. It was about 
what does it mean to be human. ’  69   Likewise, Hiroshi Ishiguro remarks 
about robots, ‘The robot is a kind of mirror that reﬂ  ects humanity and 
by creating intelligent robots we can open up new opportunities to 
contemplate what it means to be human. ’  70   Could we say something 
similar about AI? 
 Is AI a mirror in which to understand what it means to be human?         

140
               6  
 AI and architecture            
   A rchitecture, as a discipline, was about a year behind Media Arts 
in embracing AI. Moreover, it was also not an architect, but a 
media artist, Reﬁ  k Anadol, who in 2019 took perhaps the ﬁ  rst steps in 
exploring the possibilities of using AI to generate an architectural 
design.  1   Following the projection of his data sculpture on to the 
fa ç ade of the WDCH, Anadol took it upon himself to upload thousands 
of images of the architectural work of Zaha Hadid Architects (ZHA) 
into his computer, and use his version of StyleGANs to hallucinate a 
design. The process produced the design on the front cover of this 
book – a design that could well have been produced by Zaha Hadid 
herself, were she still alive. 
 Make no mistake – to generate an image using a GAN is a 
laborious, time-consuming process, and the results are often glitchy 
and low resolution. Moreover, considerable technical expertise is 
required to really master the system. Indeed, initially the only way to 
generate images using GANs was by either writing your own code or 
relying on technical support from experts in computer science. It was 
in this atmosphere that architects with an advanced knowledge of 
computation, such as Daniel Bolojan, Immanuel Koh and Casey 
Rehm, began to explore this new domain. Interestingly, each had 
been working on Swarm Intelligence (SI) and agent-based behaviour 
previously, and for them it was a natural step to shift from SI to AI. 
Likewise, each was focused primarily on teaching and personal 
research. Indeed, only Bolojan had been also involved in collaborating 
with a mainstream architectural ofﬁ  ce, Coop Himmelb(l)au. Arguably 
it was his inﬂ  uence that made that ofﬁ  ce an early leader in exploring 
the potential application of AI within an architectural ofﬁ  ce.  
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 142
   Coop Himmelb(l)au  
 Wolf Prix, the Design Principal and CEO of Coop Himmelb(l)au, the 
progressive architectural design practice based in Vienna, Austria, 
was one of the ﬁ  rst to explore the potential of AI. Initially, however, 
Prix was somewhat suspicious of AI.  2   Indeed, he was sceptical of 
computation in general, and parametricism in particular, because it 
represented a closed system.  3   But he now recognises that AI can 
open up possibilities, rather than close them down. 
 Prix, of course, is no stranger to experimentation: from his early 
days of experimental projects, such as burning wings, writing 
provocative manifestos and exploring automatic drawing, Prix has 
always sought to challenge the status quo and to open up architecture 
to new possibilities.  4   Alongside Frank Gehry, Rem Koolhaas, Peter 
Eisenman, Zaha Hadid and others, he was also one of the progressive 
architects included in the exhibition  Deconstructivist Architecture  , 
held at MoMA in New York in 1988, and has remained at the forefront 
of radical experimentation in architecture.  5   We should also bear in 
mind that Prix has always been in favour of openness and freedom of 
expression. Indeed Karl Popper, author of  The Open Society, and its 
Enemies  , is one of his heroes.  6   Whatever promotes openness is to 
be celebrated, and whatever closes down opportunities is to be 
challenged. For Prix, then, we should always be open to the possibilities 
afforded by AI: ‘Don’t exclude it! You should integrate it and use it. ’  7   
 Prix tries to impress on his team how important AI is: ‘ AI is just a 
tool. But the most important co-worker in our ofﬁ  ce is AI. ’  8   For Prix, 
the real beneﬁ  t of AI is that it can make the design process more 
efﬁ  cient and leave more time to explore new languages. For Prix, this 
comes at a time when architecture is in crisis. Architecture is on 
‘shaky ground’ right now: ‘There is almost no gravity. ’  9   Architecture is 
very insecure. The old structures are dying, and ‘new structures’ are 
coming up. Although these ‘new structures’ are not yet ready, there 
is a possibility that AI might play a role in inﬂ  uencing their development. 
For Prix, students of architecture need to explore its potential. But AI 
is not useful if it limits a student’s thinking – AI should support their 
thinking. Without any content, there will be no shape. 
 At the same time, Prix remains somewhat cautious about AI: ‘Is it 
just a fashion? Will it kill us?’  10   And certainly AI has its limitations. In 
AI AND ARCHITECTURE 143
fact, Prix likes to cite the neuroscientist, Singer, who observes that AI 
is linear, whereas the human brain is multidimensional. And Prix also 
warns us against being controlled by an algorithm: ‘Be suspicious! 
Be critical!’ 18  Architects should remain in control: ‘The architects 
should be in the front seat, and AI in the back. ’  11   Above all, Prix 
warns us against the zeros and ones of computation leading to a 
‘zero-zero architecture’. And the ultimate analogy for Prix? Not 
surprisingly, perhaps, for an architect from Vienna, Prix recognises 
the risk that AI might lead to a kind of ersatz architecture, just like 
decaffeinated coffee: ‘It looks like coffee. It smells like coffee. But no 
coffee. ’  12   
 ‘Architects should be in the 
front seat and AI in the back.’ 
 Nonetheless Prix is also amazed by the potential of AI.  
    DeepHimmelb(l)au   
  DeepHimmelb(l)au   is the name of an ongoing research initiative being 
undertaken by Coop Himmelb(l)au exploring the potential application 
of AI within the ofﬁ  ce. An early outcome was an attempt to use 
CycleGANs and other forms of GANs to ‘hallucinate’ potential 
buildings.  13   As mentioned earlier, CycleGANs work with two unpaired 
datasets. In this case, dataset A is based on reference images of 
geomorphic formations, and dataset B is based on the archive of 
actual Coop Himmelb(l)au projects. The outcome is a video of a 
journey through an imaginary landscape of Coop Himmelb(l)au-like 
building forms. 
  DeepHimmelb(l)au   showed us that it is quite possible to 
‘hallucinate’ relatively convincing designs using GANs. Moreover, 
while there is considerable work that needs to go into preparing the 
datasets, and many epochs are required to generate a high-quality 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 144
   FIGURE  6.1 Coop Himmelb(l)au, Deep Himmelb(l)au (2020). Deep 
Himmeb(l)au explores the potential of teaching machines to interpret, 
propose new designs, and augment an architect’s creativity.         

AI AND ARCHITECTURE 145
ﬁ  nal product, eventually GANs are capable of generating designs 
very rapidly. Daniel Bolojan, the computational architect responsible 
for generating  DeepHimmelb(l)au   has noted, ‘ As young as it is, within 
a clearly deﬁ  ned domain,  DeepHimmelb(l)au   outperforms designers 
with regards to the speed of interpretation and representation, and 
the amount of coherent interpretations that it can generate. ’  14   
 The intention behind  DeepHimmelb(l)au   is to ﬁ  nd a way of 
‘augmenting’ design processes, and of adding a layer to already 
existing designs. As Bolojan puts it, ‘ Deep Himmelb(l)au   explores the 
idea (connected with humans) of teaching machines to be creative, to 
interpret, to propose new designs and augment design processes. ’  15   
 At the moment the images generated by  DeepHimmelb(l)au   are 
obviously just 2D. In order to develop a more convincing and holistic 
approach, the ofﬁ  ce would need to move into 3D, and that entails 
developing more data and using rendering so as to differentiate 
depth, materiality, layers and so on. In fact, the project should be 
understood as part of a larger vision for augmenting the design 
processes in the ofﬁ  ce. As Bolojan points out, ‘Those design 
processes span from physical models interpretations to real time 
analysis, real time render, domain translations 2D and 3D, design 
space explorations. ’  16   
 Initial images remained somewhat glitchy and low resolution. 
Indeed, additional techniques had to be employed to enhance the 
resolution of the images for them to be published. Nonetheless, it 
could be said perhaps that the  Deep Himmelb(l)au   project represented 
the state of the art in terms of GAN-generated image production in 
2021, the year in which Coop Himmelb(l)au was awarded a Project 
Award by DigitalFUTURES and Wolf Prix was awarded a Lifetime 
Achievement Award by ACADIA.  
   Recent work  
 Coop Himmelb(l)au has continued its work on the  DeepHimmelb(l)au   
project. A more recent version of the project explores how these 
networks can provide new solutions and augment designers’ own 
personal abilities. The challenge here is not simply to generate an 
automated system that takes over the design input of the human 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 146
operator. Rather it is to allow a form of ‘synthesis’, whereby human 
operators and AI are working in tandem. As Bolojan points out, ‘Ultimately, 
the goal is for designers and the network to learn and grow together, 
rather than simply relying on the network to automate the design process. 
This approach will allow the network to become a valuable tool for the 
designers, rather than a replacement for human creativity. ’  17   
  Coop Himmelb(l)au is developing a node-based multi-modal 
system. Although most architects have moved away from GANs and 
towards diffusion models, the practice remains committed to 
pursuing the logic of GANs for speciﬁ  c nodes within the system to 
create domain-speciﬁ  c solutions coupled with nodes for more 
domain-generic solutions. Importantly, GANs need not be based only 
on images. They can also be based on other logics, such as parameters 
of a system, a strategy that opens up a new range of far more 
   FIGURE  6.2 Coop Himmelb(l)au, DeepHimmelb(l)au (2024), generated 
using the  Deep Himmelb(l)au   diffusion node. This is generated based on 
the prompt ‘an exterior view from afar of two sheared volumes one made 
of glass and one made of concrete, by Coop Himmelblau’.         
AI AND ARCHITECTURE 147
convincing possibilities, so that the outcomes are coherent and 
integrated, and relate to one another at a formal level. 
 One of the great advantages of an ofﬁ  ce, like Coop Himmelb(l)au, 
which has been operational since 1968, is that it has a rich archive of 
many previous projects on which to draw. This is an important 
resource to draw upon: ‘Given the intricacy and semantic complexity 
of Coop Himmelb(l)au’s design style, the project has access to a large 
dataset of nearly a thousand projects from which multimodal datasets 
can be generated to inform understanding of design as a multimodal 
endeavour. ’  18   The goal, then, is not to generate outcomes based on 
straightforward images alone, but rather to focus on bringing together 
a range of different domains at once. 
 Indeed, one of the problems with discrete models is that they 
remain domain-speciﬁ  c or mono-nodal, whereas design, and especially 
the architectural design process, relies on multiple modalities. Thus, 
   FIGURE  6.3 Coop Himmelb(l)au, Deep Himmelb(l)au (2024), generated 
using the  Deep Himmelb(l)au   diffusion node. The  Deep Himmelb(l)au   
diffusion node is fi  ne-tuned with the offi  ce’s proprietary text and image 
dataset, to generate designs that embody its distinctive design language.         

ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 148
the challenge arises as to how to create systems that enable designers 
to deﬁ  ne their own workﬂ  ows and modalities but also engage with 
these systems. In this context,  DeepHimmelb(l)au   is being developed 
as a multi-nodal system, wherein designers can establish the overall 
workﬂ  ows and determine the relationships between the various 
nodes in the system, establish workﬂ  ows and decide how to connect 
different nodes within the system. Several types of nodes are being 
developed, such as domain transfers, diffusion models, representation 
learning, language models and attention models.  19   
 The ability of the system to enable various nodes to interact with 
each other is a crucial element, that draws inspiration from Jeff 
Hawkins,  A Thousand Brain Theory: A New Theory of Intelligence  , 
which posits that the brain is made up of ‘a thousand brains’ working 
in parallel, each one with its own unique set of sensory inputs, 
   FIGURE  6.4 Coop Himmelb(l)au, Deep Himmelb(l)au (2024), generated 
using the diffusion node. The model generates coherent compositions 
aligned with the practice’s architectural semantic language.         
AI AND ARCHITECTURE 149
memory and predictions, that interact with one another to reach a 
consensus.  20   It is therefore important to understand the signiﬁ  cance 
of developing processes and workﬂ  ows that might foster interaction 
between a system’s component nodes. 
 The research focuses on three main areas: gestalt, organisation 
and technical aspects.  21   As Bolojan puts it, ‘The project’s ambition is 
not just to generate aesthetically pleasing images, but rather to 
comprehend how these systems can produce holistic solutions, 
whereby there is a coordination between the various gestalt, 
organizational or technical domains, as well as between interior and 
exterior, elevations and other aspects of design. ’  22   
 In light of recent developments in the ﬁ  eld of generative AI, the 
ofﬁ  ce has also taken a keen interest in the training of deep learning 
models using reinforcement learning with human feedback (RLHF), a 
methodology which has gained widespread attention since the launch 
of both MidJourney and ChatGPT. It is also exploring the possibility 
of implementing a form of federated learning, whereby multiple 
networks are trained locally on individual designers’ computers, 
enabling a more personalised approach to network development. 
 This also helps to overcome the aesthetic biases written into these 
models, and to replace those biases with one more closely aligned 
with CoopHimmelb(l)au’s in-house aesthetic expression. As Bolojan 
notes, “While existing generative AI models such as MidJourney and 
Stable Diffusion have their own aesthetic preferences/biases, these 
aesthetic preferences may not align with Coop Himmelblau’s design 
style. Therefore, ﬁ  ne-tuning models to better capture the unique 
semantics and nuances of the ofﬁ  ce is being investigated to generate 
a model that better aligns with the ofﬁ  ce’s speciﬁ  c needs and 
objectives. ”  23   
 The system is also able to deal with multi-perspectival views of 
the same design. One of the challenges with diffusion models is that 
each outcome generated has no connection with the previous 
outcome. Thus it is impossible to generate both interior and exterior 
views of the same design, or to capture north, south, east and west 
elevations. However, the  Deep Himmelb(l)au   multi-nodal system is 
beginning to overcome this problem, and to enable the possibility of 
approximating various view points of a single design, for both exterior 
and interior, while retaining a coherent composition. The system is 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 150
   FIGURE  6.6 Coop Himmelb(l)au, Deep Himmelb(l)au (2024), generated 
using the  Deep Himmelb(l)au   diffusion node. Overcoming the aesthetic 
biases and preferences inherent in commercial models,  Deep Himmelblau   
excels in creating multiple perspectives of a single design while preserving 
a consistent composition.         
   FIGURE  6.5 Coop Himmelb(l)au, Deep Himmelb(l)au (2024), generated 
using the  Deep Himmelb(l)au   diffusion node. The offi  ce diffusion node is 
used here to generate a sinuous interior made out of concrete and 
triangular aluminum panelisation.         
AI AND ARCHITECTURE 151
also potentially able to control perspectives exclusively through 
prompts. 
 It is clear that architects would do well to move beyond the 
limitations of these popular, yet limited, pre-trained generic models, 
by developing customized systems that can tailor the outcomes to 
the interests of their particular ofﬁ  ce. As Patrik Schumacher 
comments about  Deep Himmelb(l)au  , ‘That’s the future in terms of 
owning and developing these tools, and having them discipline 
speciﬁ  c. ’  24   The only limitation, perhaps, is whether individual ofﬁ  ces 
in the future will have enough ‘superusers’ to customise these tools 
for their particular needs in the way that Coop Himmelb(l)au has.  
   Zaha Hadid Architects  
 When will the Sputnik Moment happen for architecture?’ I posed this 
question during a lecture at the Architectural Association (AA) in May 
2022, after referring to the ‘Sputnik Moment’ in 2016 when China 
woke up to the possibilities of AI. Obviously, a match of Go would 
clearly not be enough to make the architectural community wake up 
to the possibilities of AI. What architects required would be evidence 
of the capacity of AI to generate convincing images. 
 I heard a voice from the audience, ‘It’s already happened. ’ It was 
Patrik Schumacher, principal of Zaha Hadid Architects (ZHA) and a 
tutor at the Architectural Association Design Research Laboratory 
(AA DRL) in London. Unbeknownst to most people in the architectural 
community at the time, ZHA had been collaborating with Reﬁ  k 
Anadol using DALL -E 2, to which Anadol had been given early access, 
even though it had not been released as yet to the general public. Of 
course, many were aware of the potential of DALL -E 2 from some of 
the early work posted on social media, even though very few had 
been fortunate enough to experiment with it themselves. Indeed 
Twitter, in particular, was awash with extraordinary examples of 
diffusion platforms generating images far more convincing than 
anything that GANs could have produced and in a fraction of the 
time. 
 Initially Schumacher had only been interested in exploring 
the potential of AI in order to simulate how spatial organisation 
   FIGURE  6.7 ZHA/Refi  k Anadol, DALL-E 2 Studies (2022), generated 
using DALL-E 2. These images, produced in collaboration with Refi  k 
D A L L - E  2 .          
152
AI AND ARCHITECTURE 153
might inﬂ  uence social behaviour and – vice versa – how social 
behaviour might inﬂ  uence spatial organisation. For he used 
populations of AI informed ‘agents’ to model the behaviour of 
occupants in Utility AI: 
  In both arenas – and they actually feed into each other – ZHA and 
AA DRL, the use of AI is involved in the development of advanced 
simulations of occupancy and life processes in built environments 
and designs we create. We believe that on the level of complexity 
and dynamism of contemporary social institutions particularly in 
the world of corporate headquarters and campuses and maybe 
universities and research campuses . . . that this can no longer be 
handled with a ﬁ  xed schedule of accommodation but must involve 
a more complex sense of what an architectural brief is, namely 
parametrically variable event scenarios. So these will be simulated 
with agent populations.  25    
 However, a successful collaboration between Reﬁ  k Anadol and 
ZHA on a proposal to project AI-generated images onto the 
Dongdaemun Design Plaza, a ZHA-designed complex in Seoul, South 
Korea, convinced Schumacher that AI could also be used for the 
generation of ideas – or ‘ideation’. He had never been convinced by 
the quality and resolution of images generated using GANs. However, 
once ZHA had started working together with Anadol on DALL -E 2, 
Schumacher began to realize the signiﬁ  cant advances that diffusion 
models could offer in terms of the quality and resolution of the images 
generated: ‘With Reﬁ  k’s invitation we got really going with AI with 
DALL -E 2 – or we actually start before DALL -E 2 came out – and 
when DALL -E 2 came out, it shifted the possibilities. And I ﬁ  nally got 
convinced in the incredible fertility and creative potential of these 
tools, when they reach that quality where you can really use it as a 
tool of ideation, which we’ve been doing ever since. ’  26   For Schumacher, 
this might be a new technique, but it has much in common with 
previous analogue modes of operating: 
  For me it’s always been very similar to verbal-prompting teams, 
referencing prior projects and ideas and gesticulating with my 
hands. That’s the way of generating ideas, and I can do that directly 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 154
with MidJourney of DALL -E 2, or our team can do it as well on our 
behalf, and so that I think that’s quite potent.  27    
 ZHA now has a signiﬁ  cant internal research group working on AI. 
For Schumacher, DALL -E 2 can generate many possible options for a 
project, and oftentimes there are a few interesting ideas and formal 
moves that can subsequently be modelled in 3D in terms of both the 
interior and exterior, although attention still needs to be paid to ensure 
that the front, back, sides and interiors relate to one another. Moreover, 
even the early outcomes come across as relatively coherent, so that 
it is easy enough to model them. As a result, ZHA has adopted 
DALL -E 2, MidJourney and Stable Diffusion in the ofﬁ  ce for early 
ideation, not necessarily for all projects, but certainly for competitions, 
in order to explore the range of options that can be generated and to 
increase repertoire of possibilities. ZHA then selects 10–15 per cent 
of the AI-generated images, and works them up into 3D. 
 Most of these early experimentations include ‘Zaha Hadid’ in the 
prompt, together with various attributes and programmatic 
suggestions. The outcomes are incorporated into the ofﬁ  ce oeuvre, 
and form the basis of a repertoire from which ideas can be selected, 
iterated and further elaborated. On occasions, these DALL -E 2, 
MidJourney and Stable Diffusion outcomes are even shown to clients 
as early sketches in order to share ideas, largely because the light, 
shadow, geometry, coherency, sense of gravity and order are so 
potent that they become an effective way of illustrating early ideas. 
All this is very empowering for Schumacher. 
 The eventual aim of the ofﬁ  ce is to develop its own network for 
AI-based image generation which would be run on its own in-house 
server farm in its data centre. The practice has already used Low-
Rank Adaptation (LoRA), a technique for ﬁ  ne-tuning Stable Diffusion 
models, to develop both tower and masterplan models based on its 
own render databases. 
 The adoption of these diffusion models by one of the world’s 
most prominent ofﬁ  ces is a highly signiﬁ  cant step in terms of the 
introduction of AI generative techniques into mainstream architectural 
practice. However, the use of AI in the ZHA ofﬁ  ce is not limited to 
the generation of images. ZHA is also collaborating with Immanuel 
Koh, a former student at the AA DRL, in the development of effective 
AI AND ARCHITECTURE 155
   FIGURE  6.8 ZHA, Ideational Studies (2023), generated using 
MidJourney. These are ideational studies for a complex on the 
Mediterranean coast.         
techniques for turning these 2D diffusion-model-generated images 
into 3D forms. The ZHA ofﬁ  ce also uses machine learning algorithmic 
techniques and neural networks to develop more rational, module 
based conﬁ  gurations and optimisations, and to test for legibility of 
proposal and orientation issues in complex spaces. This is effectively 
an extension of the earlier research into the relationship between 
spatial organisation and social behaviours. 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 156
 However, John Frazer – one of Zaha’s own former instructors at 
the AA – is somewhat sceptical of the use of these diffusion models 
in the ZHA ofﬁ  ce:
In some of Zaha’s better examples particularly those where 
Cristiano Ceccato has played a strong role in the project, like the 
airport in Beijing, for example, where the primary inﬂ  uences are 
extremely clever analyses of pedestrian movement, trafﬁ  c 
movement, aircraft movement and so on – the environment and 
the spatial forms ﬁ  t together into an extraordinarily well conceived 
totality logically, which then generates – from that logic – exotic 
forms, which are delightful to the eye. Now I think that the 
experiment that ZHA is currently doing [with diffusion models] is 
very dangerous, because I think that they are risking trivialising 
their own work – or Zaha’s work – by suggesting that it is  only  to 
do with the forms, because those algorithms are interested in the 
visual effects, so it’s replicating and producing things that look 
Zaha-like.  28   
 By contrast, Frazer maintains, Zaha’s best projects follow the 
same inner logic: ‘It is to do with people, space, environment    FIGURE  6.9 ZHA, LORA Masterplan Model (2023), generated using 
the ZHA Masterplan Render database.         

AI AND ARCHITECTURE 157
everything – not just the visual aspect. And reducing it to [the visual 
aspect] implies that all Zaha was good at was doing curvy shapes. ’  29   
 The future, then, lies surely in the possibility of integrating both 
sets of AI-based tools used in the ZHA ofﬁ  ce – and indeed all other 
ofﬁ  ces – so that the results are both logical and visually appealing. 
Given the exponential speed of development in these ﬁ  elds, could 
we expect such seemingly ambitious developments to be upon us, 
even before most of us might expect them?  
   Foster + Partners  
 Foster + Partners has taken a very different approach to ZHA and 
Coop Himmelb(l)au. The practice is currently pursuing a range of 
applications for disruptive technologies including AI, spearheaded by    FIGURE  6.10 The Applied Research and Development (ARD) group 
Foster + Partners collaborated with Boston Dynamics to explore how the 
robotic ‘dog’, Spot, could be used in the context of construction. [Credit: 
Foster and Partners]         

ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 158
research in their Applied Research and Development (ARD) group, led 
by Martha Tsigkari. Their view is that AI is forcing them to look outside 
their comfort zone, and to rethink everything that had been taken for 
granted. At the same time AI offers obvious possibilities in terms of 
using data to augment problem-solving tasks within the ofﬁ  ce: 
  ‘ At Foster + Partners, the Applied Research and Development 
(ARD) group is rethinking this mindset and embracing machine 
learning, not to replicate or replace designers, but to enhance our 
knowledge, instincts and sensitivities, free us from routine tasks, 
and to optimise and push the boundaries of our designs. ’  30    
 Although they also use diffusion models, they are not so interested in 
deploying them to simply generate images. As Martha Tsigkari puts 
it, ‘We’re not doing it in Midjourney, because we’re not interested in 
just writing a prompt and getting an image. We’re not image makers; 
we are building makers and experience makers at the end of the 
day. ’  31   Instead, they have been trying to safeguard IP and conﬁ  dentiality, 
by building everything in their own servers, and allowing their 
architects to augment the visualisation of 3D models that they have 
already created. This is not to say that they do not use diffusion 
models to generate images on occasions, but rather they see this as 
a tiny drop in the creative process that is not so dissimilar to going 
online to search for images to put on the wall, in order to ideate at the 
beginning of the process. 
 Nor is it to say that the practice is not interested in creativity. After 
all, Foster + Partners has been coming up with highly creative 
solutions for over ﬁ  fty years now. Rather, they are not interested in 
using data from previous work to generate new work. Tsigkari again: 
‘Our reasoning is not to replace our designers or try to replicate 
designs that we’ve already made by training our datasets with 
designs that we have already delivered. We know that we can do 
better than that, and we know that we can do it ourselves. So the 
entire point of this process is: how can these models help in building 
design assistant tools for us to do things faster, better?’  32   
 The ARD group is more interested in using AI in a practical way in 
order to speed up and improve ofﬁ  ce operations, than for generating 
designs: ‘What we’re trying to do, and what we’ve always tried to do 
AI AND ARCHITECTURE 159
since 2017 , when we ﬁ  rst started looking into these things is to try to 
understand how can these processes and tools help our particular 
business cases, and how can they ﬁ  rst of all, help with automation 
and quicker results when we know certain solutions can be done 
automatically and by analytical solutions usually. And secondly, how 
we can achieve creative augmentation. ’  33   
 The ARD Group has been exploring two ways in particular, in 
which AI can be incorporated into the design process. The ﬁ  rst is 
surrogate modelling: ‘What is a surrogate model? A surrogate model 
is a machine learning model that has been trained on a lot of data to 
provide suggestions or predictions about things that we already 
know. If we are to analyse spatial and visual connectivity. We know 
we can run an analysis, and it can take quite a long of time if you do 
it for a big ﬂ  oor plate. If we train a system to predict the results, it 
takes fractions of a second to do a prediction. Is the prediction a 
hundred percent accurate? No, it’s 90–95 per cent accurate. Do we 
care? No, because this accuracy is enough to make the right decisions 
early on the design process, while allowing us to move on very 
quickly between options. ’  34   
 Tradition analytical processes for engineering simulations, such as 
structural deformation, solar radiation and pedestrian movement, 
consume valuable time and can take hours or even days to complete. 
This is a challenge that the ARD Group is taking up, by deploying 
surrogate models, which are simple analytical models, that can be 
used to mimic or model the behaviour of a complex system, 
substantially reducing time, and allowing architects to explore a 
design space more quickly and efﬁ  ciently. As Tsigkari notes, ‘To solve 
this problem, we need to provide designers with results in near real-
time. For that reason, the ARD Group investigated surrogate models, 
where a computationally “cheaper” predictive model is constructed 
based on a number of intelligently chosen simulation results. The 
designer can then have results from the surrogate model’s 
approximation of a simulation in real-time, which is good enough for 
them to make a quick decision. ’  35   These models are made accessible 
ofﬁ  ce wide through a series of customised ML plug-ins written 
in-house to help to speed up the design process. 
 The second area where AI is used explicitly in Foster + Partners is 
in ‘design assistance’, where these systems can work alongside 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 160
designers in the creative process, in areas such as Multi-Objective 
Optimisation (MOO), which effectively operates as a form of design 
prosthesis. Here the practice has been deploying the in-house-
developed, distributed computing platform, Hydra, which uses 
genetic algorithms as a form of early-stage optimisation tool used on 
a city scale urban models of up to 12 million square metres of Gross 
Floor Area (GFA). In this case, the idea is not to give a prediction, but 
rather to offer a solution space with multiple dimensions and multiple 
criteria, based on objectives that might even be completely conﬂ  ictual. 
The idea is to help their designers to make better decisions, that are 
much more educated and much faster, but leaving the creative 
decisions in their own hands. 
 This is possible due to the continued investment in the practice of 
developing the combination of massively parallel computing at the 
GPU level as well as cluster computing, technologies which have 
been powering the AI revolution. In this context, they are not only 
being used to train neural networks but also to dramatically speed up 
the performance simulations of design building. An example of this 
could be Cyclops, an in-house GPU-based ray-tracing engine powering 
both quality of view and environmental analyses. 
 Such systems produce large amounts of design data,  36   another 
cornerstone of the AI revolution. These datasets can be used to build 
predictive models in the future, which could, in turn, be consumed by 
various stakeholders and integrated into their design workﬂ  ows. This 
approach fundamentally requires a rethinking of how design data is 
distributed. This has prompted the ARD group to invest in research 
on the implementation of modern software delivery paradigms like 
DesignOps  37   and MLOps into the design process. The main goal of 
this research is to understand and leverage all these recent 
technologies to help reduce any operational inefﬁ  ciencies in the 
design workﬂ  ow, as well as to provide better quality designs through 
technological advancement. DesignOps is about implementing 
design improvements and deploying them to users (designers) as 
quickly and as frictionlessly as possible. 
 The group has also collaborated with Autodesk on focused AI-
based research projects that feed into the design process, where 
they investigated smart materials, and using GANs to calculate the 
thermal deformation of laminates, thereby speeding up the design 
AI AND ARCHITECTURE 161
process.  38   The beneﬁ  ts of such research are obvious. As Tsigkari puts 
it, ‘Imagine that we have a thermoactive, passively actuated, laminate 
material that deforms based on varied thermal conditions. As 
designers, we are interested in the start and end point of the 
deformation (how it may look at rest and how I would like it to look 
when heated) – that is what deﬁ  nes our design. In order to control 
that, we would need to know how to control the laminate layering, a 
process that requires non-linear analysis and quite a lot of time. Or, 
we could use ML to train a system to predict the laminate layering 
and give real-time feedback to the designer. ’  39   
 LLMs are also being used for knowledge dissemination, in the 
development of knowledge base systems, to help architects gain 
access to different knowledge bases created by different support teams 
in the ofﬁ  ce, addressing issues, such as ofﬁ  ce-wide design guidelines, 
without overburdening the support teams themselves. This is also 
being expanded to create a knowledge base of architectural details 
from previous projects in the ofﬁ  ce. At the same time, the practice is 
very careful to use these systems in a safe way, where it can protect its 
IP , and have fail-safes in place to guard against the probabilistic nature of 
those models. The ofﬁ  ce has also been developing AI-powered 
applications for operational data insights and business insights. 
 Finally, the ARD group has also been exploring the potential of 
using Spot, the mobile robotic ‘dog’ produced by Boston Dynamics, 
and equipping it with scanning technologies to check the fabrication 
of buildings.  40   Here the intention was to check ‘as designed’ models 
against ‘as built’ realities, and to perform semi-autonomous scanning, 
registration and reporting of progress on construction sites so as to 
gather model data for digital twins: ‘One of our main objectives for 
Spot was to investigate the viability of an automated end-to-end 
workﬂ  ow for capturing three-dimensional model data for digital twins. 
Typically, when the practice didn’t have Spot at its disposal, gathering 
data in-situ presented some challenges. Although each site is 
different, there are three main tasks in common which should be 
conducted when collecting data for digital twins: scanning, 
registration and reporting. ’  41   The potential also exists for using such 
robots to check – and thereby also to predict – post-occupancy 
activities within a building. The exercise showed how Spot – although 
not without its challenges – could help to automate these processes.  
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 162
   The AI-augmented architect  
 In this chapter we have looked at three leading architectural practices 
– Coop Himmelb(l)au, ZHA and Foster + Partners – each taking 
different approaches towards the use of AI in the ofﬁ  ce. ZHA uses 
various generic diffusion models primarily for ideation at the start 
of a project. Coop Himmelb(l)au has been investing heavily in 
developing its own customized diffusion model. Meanwhile Foster + 
Partners are more interested in using AI to speed up the design 
workﬂ  ow within the ofﬁ  ce. In other words, whereas ZHA and Coop 
Himmelb(l)au are using AI primarily – although not exclusively – to 
generate progressive designs, Foster + Partners are using AI to 
enhance productivity. 
 While these three ofﬁ  ces hardly cover the full spectrum of ofﬁ  ce 
types in a profession now rapidly coming to grasp with the challenges 
and opportunities of AI, they nonetheless offer an interesting 
representative sample of how high-proﬁ  le practices are engaging 
with AI. And even though each takes a different approach to the use 
of AI, they all see the primary role of AI as being to augmenting the 
operations of the architect. 
 ‘The architect of the future 
will be augmented.’ 
 But let us reﬂ  ect on the particular approach of Foster + Partners 
for a moment. To what extent will AI actually speed up the design 
process, and improve productivity? This, perhaps, is the crucial 
question, as far as most ofﬁ  ces are concerned. 
 ‘I think that we have an answer on whether AIs will reshape work, ’ 
quips Ethan Mollick, referring to a joint paper that he co-authored in 
the Harvard Business School.  42   Mollick had been part of a team of 
social scientists working with Boston Consulting Group, testing out 
the enhanced performance offered by ChatGPT-4 to those working in 
that group: ‘Consultants using ChatGPT-4 outperformed those who 
did not, by a lot. On every dimension. Every way we measured 
AI AND ARCHITECTURE 163
performance. Consultants using AI ﬁ  nished 12.2% more tasks on 
average, completed tasks 25.1% more quickly, and produced 40% 
higher quality results that those without. ’  43   In other words, AI was 
seen to have a signiﬁ  cant impact on increasing productivity, speeding 
up operations and improving on the quality of the output. 
 This was a generic study, working with business consultants, 
rather than architects. It was also based on ChatGPT, rather than any 
architectural design software. But do not ignore this study. Its ﬁ  ndings 
are highly signiﬁ  cant. 
 But how does this study relate to the creative industries? Is it 
relevant to architecture? In fact, part of the study did attempt to 
assess the impact of AI on creativity, and participants were tasked 
with proposing a design idea for a shoe, and seeing it all the way 
through from conceptualisation to entering the market – a challenge 
perhaps not so different to designing and fabricating a building. More 
importantly, however, the study measured improvements in the 
quality and quantity/speed of production. The primary incentive for 
the adoption of AI on the part of architectural ﬁ  rms that pride 
themselves on their designs, such as ZHA and Coop Himmelb(l)au, is 
to improve the quality of production. After all, AI is already being used 
to enhance the design process, especially in generating ideas for 
competitions, which are judged on the quality of a design. A better 
design is more likely to win. Meanwhile, the primary incentive to 
adopt AI tools for architectural practices that pride themselves on the 
efﬁ  ciency of their operations, such as Foster + Partners, is to speed 
up the design process. Arguably these two incentives to adopt AI will 
apply for  all  architectural practices to a greater or lesser extent. As 
such, even though this study did not address architectural concerns 
explicitly, its importance for architectural design cannot be overstated. 
 Over the next few years we can expect to see targeted research 
being undertaken on how AI might assist not only the architectural 
design process, but also construction itself and post construction 
operations. We can also expect to see the wholesale adoption of 
these tools by architectural practices, as resistance to them begins to 
fade, and architects begin to recognise how indispensable these 
tools have become if they hope to stay competitive in what is likely 
to become an increasingly competitive market. 
 The architect of the future will be augmented.               
164

166
               7  
 The future of the 
architectural office            
   W hat role will AI play in the future of the architectural ofﬁ  ce? 
 From the perspective of the standard architectural ofﬁ  ce, up 
until now the most common application of AI has been through AI-
based software developed by existing architectural software companies, 
such as Autodesk. In fact much of Autodesk’s software, including Fusion 
360, is already AI-enabled. The vision at Autodesk – like everywhere 
else – is not ‘ AI versus humans’ so much as ‘ AI assisting humans’. AI will 
take the form of AI assistants – at least for the foreseeable future. 
 Mike Haley offers his personal prediction of what it will be like to 
work in the ofﬁ  ce of the future. According to Haley, soon we will all 
be talking to our computers, and giving instructions based on what 
we can see on the screen:  1   
   Haley   ‘OK Autodesk, I want to design a chair. ’ 
  Autodesk   ‘Here are some popular chairs. Scroll through them 
and select some examples to give me an idea of the type of chair 
you would like. ’ 
  Haley   ‘OK, I’m making some selections now. Can you show me 
those together?’ 
  Autodesk   ‘ Alright, here are the chairs you selected. ’ 
  Haley   ‘OK. Those chairs are interesting. Could you combine all 
possible pairs between those chairs. Find out what’s in between 
them. ’ 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 168
  Autodesk   ‘ Alright. There are 15 possible pairs. I’m morphing the 
form between all pairs, generating a large range of combinations. ’ 
  Haley   ‘OK. I’d like to explore the space between these shapes. 
Could you combine, mix and mesh these designs for me. ’ 
  Autodesk   ‘Here are 5 possibilities between each of the 15 pairs. 
There are a total of 75 possible designs. ’  
 And so on. 
 This is not an unrealistic vision. After all, much of the technology 
for this scenario is already available. We already talk to ChatGPT , Siri, 
Alexa and other AI assistants. So why wouldn’t we also be talking to 
our computers? 
 There is, however, one aspect of this that is a little less convincing. 
In this simulated scenario, the computer displays a range of ‘popular 
chairs’. But could we not argue that sometime in the not too distant 
future the computer will already know our preferences, and be able 
to customise the selection? As such, the computer would already 
know what chair designs we prefer. Instead of saying, ‘Here are 
some popular chairs, ’ it would be more likely to say, ‘Here are some 
new designs that you might like. ’ Not only that, but soon AI will surely 
be able to generate new design options tailored to our known 
aesthetic preferences.  
   Superusers  
 Randy Deutsch offers a slightly different vision. He believes that the 
ofﬁ  ce of the future will be dominated by what he calls ‘Superusers’ 
– ‘high-performing, high-functioning, highly connected, and highly 
motivated’ design technology specialists who, he believes, ‘represent 
the near future of architecture’.  2   Certainly in recent years we have 
witnessed the growth of computationally advanced research units 
operating in many of our leading ofﬁ  ces – the Specialist Modelling 
Group and Applied Research and Development (ARD) Group within 
Foster + Partners, Gehry Technologies within Gehry and Partners, 
and ZHA Code within Zaha Hadid Architects, to name but a few. In 
many cases, these teams have proved indispensable in their use of 
THE FUTURE OF THE ARCHITECTURAL OFFICE 169
advanced computational skills to address design challenges in the 
increasingly complex architectural world of today. 
 But would the introduction of AI into the architectural ofﬁ  ce 
 necessarily   lead to a further expansion of this trend? The paradox of 
technology is that it might depend on increasingly complex 
computation – and certainly AI is no exception to this – but, as far as 
the average user is concerned, in general it is becoming ever easier 
to use. Take the way that we can ‘swipe’ our phones. Or take the way 
we can now use facial recognition to switch them on. In fact, facial 
recognition will soon mean that we will not need to carry around 
passports, keys, credit cards or money. In fact, in China it is already 
possible to pay for goods using facial recognition. Paradoxically 
computationally complex technology often makes life simpler rather 
than more complicated. 
 This is not to say that it is easy to engineer such seemingly 
effortless technological advances. On the contrary, it is extremely 
challenging. The logic here is not dissimilar to that of minimalist 
architecture. In order to make minimalist architecture look simple and 
effortless, considerable effort must be invested in resolving 
complexities. Details need to be controlled obsessively, and 
connections concealed.  3   Simplicity and effortlessness do not come 
easily. 
 It is likely, then, that AI will actually make life easier and simpler. 
Here a parallel can be found in the introduction of Grasshopper. 
Anyone who has spent hours or even days laboriously writing code in 
RhinoScript, MEL (Maya Embedded Language), Visual Basic, 
3dMaxScript or any other computer programming language, will 
know how much easier it is now to work with Grasshopper, the plug-
in for Rhino that bypasses code by using pictographic forms of 
information. And, arguably, working with AI will make it even easier 
still. This is in line with Haley’s view that we will simply talk to our 
computers.  
   The architect vs the Superuser  
 Here, then, we have two radically different scenarios unfolding. On 
the one hand, Haley believes that we will soon have user-friendly AI 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 170
applications for making the design process easier, to the point that 
the user might not even need to draw anything, let alone have any 
advanced understanding of the technology. On the other hand, 
Deutsch believes that the ofﬁ  ce of the future will be populated by 
computationally advanced ‘superusers’. 
 In the end, however, both situations are likely to prevail. Most users 
will ﬁ  nd AI-based tools extremely easy to use. But some will wish to 
customise them in order to exploit their full potential. In short, although 
Haley is obviously correct to assume that AI will make life easier for 
the average user, Randy Deutsch is equally correct to assume that the 
ofﬁ  ce of the future will be dominated by digital technologies and that 
superusers will play an increasingly signiﬁ  cant role.  
   Spacemaker/Forma  
 There is now a vast number of start-ups developing AI-based tools for 
architectural ofﬁ  ces. However, there are two early pioneers that still 
   FIGURE  7 .1 Lund Hagem Architects, Tjeldbergvika Development, 
Svolvaer, Norway (2019). Spacemaker AI was used to optimise sun 
conditions, sea views, wind, daylighting and noise conditions.         
THE FUTURE OF THE ARCHITECTURAL OFFICE 171
stand out in terms of their size and the investments that they have 
attracted: Spacemaker AI (Spacemaker)/Forma and XKool Technology 
(XKool)/LookX.ai (LookX). For the sake of simplicity, let’s focus solely 
on these two companies, and compare their different strategies. 
 Spacemaker AI (Spacemaker), the predecessor of what is now 
called Forma, was launched in Oslo, Norway in late 2016 by architect 
H å vard Haukeland, computer engineer Carl Christensen and ﬁ  nancial 
analyst Anders Kvale. Spacemaker soon started breaking into the 
market. In its ﬁ  rst signiﬁ  cant round of venture capital funding it picked 
up $25 million. By the end of 2017 , Spacemaker was named as one of 
the 500 most interesting startups by  Hello Tomorrow  .   4   In 2018, it was 
named the ‘hottest place to work’ by Norway’s leading business 
newspaper,  Dagens N æ ringsliv  .   5   And by 2019, Spacemaker was 
named Norwegian ‘Start Up of the Year’.  6   By 2020 Spacemaker had 
100-plus employees, with branch ofﬁ  ces in Barcelona, Stockholm and 
Cambridge, Massachusetts, close to the Harvard and MIT campuses. 
In November 2020, it was announced that Spacemaker had been 
acquired by Autodesk for $240 million.  7   In 2022, Spacemaker was 
renamed Forma.  8   
 The initial intention behind Spacemaker was to ﬁ  nd the smartest 
way to realise the potential of any building plot. Spacemaker was 
intended to be used primarily by property development professionals. 
As Steve O’Hear puts it, 
  Described as ‘the world’s ﬁ  rst’ AI-assisted design and construction 
simulation software for the property development sector, 
Spacemaker claims to enable property development professionals, 
such as real estate developers, architects and urban planners, to 
quickly generate and evaluate the optimal environmental design 
for any multi-building residential development. To achieve this, the 
Spacemaker software crunches various data, including physical 
data, regulations, environmental factors and other preferences.  9    
 The premise behind Spacemaker was that the design of buildings in 
an urban setting has become increasingly challenging for architects. 
There is simply too much data and complexity. And this is where 
Spacemaker would come in. The Spacemaker team would see its role 
as translating architecture to mathematics. To this end, Spacemaker 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 172
developed an engine that would generate, optimize and analyse 
buildings of different solutions based on input data and preferences 
deﬁ  ned by the user. 
 Once the site had been deﬁ  ned, and the various constraints and 
parameters inputted, the AI engine would then be engaged, and the 
architect would be presented with a range of optimized solutions. As 
Bilal Chowdhry explained, 
  So that means that architects are now able to explore a really wide 
variety of alternative solutions for a site, instead of only a handful 
of solutions. And at the same time the solutions that they are 
looking at are aimed at maximizing space utilization while also 
satisfying requirements and regulations in the area. This means 
that the architect can work in a completely different way, in a much 
more informed and iterative process.  10    
 What resulted would not simply be a change in the design 
process itself. it would also represent a change in the way that 
architects design, whereby the AI would become an ‘invisible 
assistant’. 
 Spacemaker would also offer a platform that used the cloud to 
allow the various stakeholders in a project to come together to study 
‘surrogate models’ of the proposed building. This would allow them 
to ‘trade off’ various performance factors in real time, so that they 
could quickly ﬁ  nd the solution that suited them best. In this sense 
Haukeland saw the platform as offering ‘customer value’ that has a 
particular appeal to developer clients. Indeed, as Haukeland observed, 
developer clients were beginning to insist that architects use AI in the 
design process, so that they could ﬁ  nd the most effective and 
efﬁ  cient solution for their client: ‘The developers really want architects 
to use Spacemaker. That technology is something they want. It is a 
requirement from their clients. ’  11   
 This seemingly throwaway comment should be taken seriously. 
This surely is the single most important factor that will lead to the AI 
revolution in the architectural profession. Clients will want architects 
to use AI in order to maximise their return on investment (RoI) and 
optimise the performance of their buildings.  12   At the same time, 
however, clients are now beginning to question whether they should 
THE FUTURE OF THE ARCHITECTURAL OFFICE 173
pay architects their full fees, now that much of the work is done 
by AI. 
 As the Susskinds have pointed out, economics are going to be the 
key driver of change.  13   It is as simple as that. Forget progressive 
aesthetics, forget experimentation. Think about cost. Moreover, the 
lasting impact of AI will be guaranteed once the majority of clients 
start to insist on their architects using AI. Thereafter we could predict 
that architects will begin to brand themselves in terms of their 
practice-based use of AI in order to attract clients, much as they now 
brand themselves as being LEED or BREEAM certiﬁ  ed in terms of 
environmental sustainability. 
 Importantly, the platform would allow users to  explore   different 
options. Hence the name of Spacemaker’s early generative platform, 
Explore. Kara Vatn described how their platform Explore operated: 
‘With  Explore  , architects and urban planners can continuously 
generate and review different site proposals, and focus in and iterate 
at both a macro and micro level. Users can make changes at any 
point in the planning process and immediately see the impact and 
alternative options for their site, all in one fast and uninterrupted 
workﬂ  ow. ’  14   
 Not only would their platform serve to increase the range of 
options, but it would also suggest options, some of which might not 
be immediately apparent. In fact, on occasion the platform would 
come up with suggestions that no architect would ever have imagined, 
but that nonetheless would offer the best solution. Haukeland cited a 
particular example of a project, affectionately known as their ‘Giraffe’ 
project, where the computer was able to ﬁ  nd the complete opposite 
of what an experienced architectural mind might have thought 
appropriate: 
  The places where the architects thought that it would be smart to 
build tall buildings, and the places where they thought it would be 
smart to build a dense wall, all the things that they intuitively 
thought would be smart – because they had hundreds of projects 
of experience – were ﬂ  ipped around. Because when you get the 
complexity of thinking of a multi-objective organizational problem 
. . . you are really not able to see the patterns that a computer can 
ﬁ  nd. So what happened was that the computer was able to ﬁ  nd a 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 174
pattern as to how to solve that site that you would never come up 
with yourself.  15    
 Again, the possibility had always existed, but – like move 37 in the 
AlphaGo match – no one had thought about it before. What this 
suggests is that from a strategic point of view, planning and the game 
of Go might have more in common than at ﬁ  rst seems apparent. 
 The platform would serve as an AI-based assistant, intended not 
to displace the architect but to serve as a prosthetic extension of the 
designers’ abilities, as Vatn elaborated: ‘ Explore   isn’t replacing 
creativity. Instead, it provides inspiration. It uses AI to deliver a vast 
number of options but it is up to the user, using their experience, to 
select those that they wish to investigate further; the user is in 
control, with Spacemaker supporting their decision-making. ’  16   
 Although Spacemaker uses AI, it also uses a range of other 
technologies. As Maria Dantz comments, AI is not necessarily the main 
driver of their processes, as least as far as their customers are concerned: 
  Our current website is very much about an AI-centred platform, but 
AI is just one part of the entire platform. A big part of what our 
users actually like are the analyses. It’s the analytics. It’s being able 
to use data in a way that they weren’t able to use and combine data 
before, in a model. And then you can basically integrate that with 
the optimization of plans and the AI part. So that is just one aspect, 
but it’s not necessarily the driver for every one of our customers.  17    
 Carl Christensen described their approach as being similar to how 
a self-driving car works. A self-driving car relies on AI, but it also relies 
on many other technologies. They were experimenting with a number 
of different technologies, including deep learning, with a view to 
incorporating more advanced AI. Although the team did not disclose 
full details of what they use, it would seem that their primary focus 
was on topological optimisation and machine learning, rather than 
deep learning: 
  It’s a real mix-up of different things, because the core idea is to 
bring everything together in one platform. So we do use a lot of 
machine learning, but we do use other algorithms and ways of 
THE FUTURE OF THE ARCHITECTURAL OFFICE 175
modelling the world as well. And we generally say that it is AI like a 
self-driving car is an AI. It’s a lot of different things coming together 
to create a result. So we do use generative design. We use 
optimization. We use simulation models of the world. We use 
machine learning models for many things, like surrogate models for 
understanding the physical environment – for example, how you 
would change a design to make it better for many factors at once.  18    
 Indeed, Christensen saw the role of AI as resting ‘on the shoulders of 
humans’ both to guide them and also to give them superpowers, so 
that humans would remain ﬁ  rmly in control: ‘To make AI successful, 
it needs to be on the shoulders of humans, giving them superpowers, 
and not telling them what to do. That would be like a self-driving car 
driving where it wants to go. ’  19   Nonetheless, as Maria Dantz explained, 
‘We are an AI platform, but we do want to empower our users. So we 
do not believe that AI is going to replace anything. But we do ﬁ  rmly 
believe that in the workplace of the future architects using AI will 
replace architects that don’t. ’  20   However, this is an extremely 
misleading claim, as it infers that one person using AI will replace one 
   FIGURE  7 .2 Schmidt Hammer Lassen Architects, Molobyen 
Development, Bod ø , Norway, 2019–20. Spacemaker AI was used to 
optimise the design, taking into account a variety of factors.         
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 176
person not using AI. Clearly, one person empowered by AI could 
achieve substantially more than one person not using AI, and it is 
therefore unlikely to be a one-for-one replacement. 
 Cedric Price once famously asked, ‘Technology is the answer, but 
what was the question?’  21   By contrast, for Haukeland, ‘Technology is 
just a lever to solve a customer problem. ’  22   Haukeland would see their 
platform as a ‘performance driven tool for the masses’.  23   This raises 
two important issues. On the one hand, it illustrates how AI can be 
seen not only as an aesthetically driven design tool, generating some 
visually striking designs, as we have seen in the previous chapter, but 
also as a performance-driven design tool, generating cost efﬁ  cient 
and performance-based solutions. On the other hand, it highlights 
how easy it is for everyone to use in an architectural ofﬁ  ce. 
 The problem with parametric tools has been that they are often 
too advanced for many architects to use: ‘It was only the parametric 
geeks in the ofﬁ  ce that were able to use them. ’  24   With AI, however, 
computation becomes so much easier to use. It is not simply about 
intelligent trade-offs. It is also a question of recognising the 
importance of the whole package in offering smart sketching tools 
and the integration of the whole design process into one consolidated 
platform. As Haukeland observed, 
  I think that you can never make AI the new way of working without 
really understanding all of the customer needs, and all of the 
problems, and once you understand that complexity, and what 
really drives the value, then AI is just empowering that . . . And 
that’s also why I personally really like Carl’s analogy of the car, 
because it’s really just about making it easier to drive.  25    
 The aim would be to provide everyone with tools that are extremely 
easy to use, and accessible to anyone, no matter how little background 
experience they have in computation. From this perspective, their 
model would be closer to Haley’s model of ‘ Autodesk, design me a 
chair’ than to Deutsch’s model of the superuser. 
 The renaming of Spacemaker as Forma was effectively a 
rebranding, updating and enhancement of Spacemaker, incorporating 
many of the original features of Spacemaker, along with some of 
Autodesk’s earlier software, such as Formit, its version of Sketchup. 
THE FUTURE OF THE ARCHITECTURAL OFFICE 177
The aim is to develop it further into ‘an all-encompassing, cradle-to-
grave platform . . . able to assist the whole of design, build and 
operation community with all the software it needs, with many AEC 
apps under one roof all working off a uniﬁ  ed database. ’  26   Eventually, it 
is hoped, rather than generating a singular outcome, it will offer the 
user hundreds of possible scenarios, ranking them in order. 
 Forma is set to become the cornerstone of a new Autodesk cloud-
based, data-driven design/build/operate platform, uniting a whole 
range of different apps into a single platform. Here the intention is to 
go beyond a design-build platform, and extend into all that happens 
after the building is completed, including ownership and maintenance 
of the building – adding the ‘O’ (operation) to ‘ AEC’ (architecture, 
engineering and construction) to produce ‘ AECO’ (architecture, 
engineering, construction and operations).  27   
 But will this be enough to keep Autodesk ahead of the market in 
terms of AI-based software for architectural ofﬁ  ces? Or will they face 
serious competition elsewhere?  
   FIGURE  7 .3 Xkool, Towers (2023), generated using LookX. In addition 
to offering a comprehensive data to fabrication platform, XKool has also 
launched its own diffusion model, LookX.         
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 178
   X K o o l   
 The big rival for Autodesk is surely XKool Technology (XKool), even more 
so, now that it has launched LookX.AI (LookX), its diffusion model, 
globally, as a separate US-registered company based in Los Angeles.  28   
 XKool was founded in 2016 by two architects, Wanyu He and 
Xiaodi Yang. XKool is a contraction of ‘ex-Koolhaas’. It refers both to 
He’s previous experience of working under Rem Koolhaas for OMA in 
Rotterdam and Hong Kong for more than seven years, and to the 
popular term ‘Xkool’, which, according to the  Urban Dictionary  , means 
to the highest level of ‘coolness’ that can be achieved.  29   
 XKool claims to be ‘the world’s ﬁ  rst innovative technology company 
that uses cutting-edge technologies such as deep learning, machine 
learning and big data to successfully apply artiﬁ  cial intelligence to 
urban planning and architectural design, is based on its own core 
algorithm technology and in architectural design’.  30   XKool has also 
been highly successful in attracting investments from 2016 onwards 
and business clients. By early 2020, XKool had secured a joint 
investment of over 100 million RMB from a group of venture capitals, 
such as Vision Night Capital, SIG and 5Y Capital.  31   XKool is a 100-plus-
person startup, with its headquarters in Shenzhen, known as the 
‘Silicon Valley of China’, and with further branches in Beijing, Shanghai 
and Chongqing, Los Angeles and Berlin. In June 2023, XKool launched 
its diffusion model, LookX.ai (LookX), as a global company registered 
in the United States.  
   Data-driven design  
 Somewhat paradoxically, it would seem that OMA – one of the least 
computational of all progressive architectural ofﬁ  ces – might have 
been in part responsible for introducing AI into architectural practice. 
While working at OMA, He encountered a highly inefﬁ  cient culture 
where architects would work late into the night – and often over 
weekends – constantly drawing and redrawing plans for a project. This 
extreme waste of resources prompted He to look for a more efﬁ  cient 
way of working. She vowed never to be in the same situation again: ‘I 
don’t want to draw those plans again and again. That’s insane!’  32   
THE FUTURE OF THE ARCHITECTURAL OFFICE 179
 There is an important distinction to be made, according to He, 
between a ‘graphic-driven mode’ of operating and a ‘data-driven 
mode’. For her, the problem with architectural culture is that we still 
operate largely in a ‘graphic-driven mode’. We draw. We sketch. But 
these are purely graphic modes of representation. They do not contain 
any information about building regulations, code, cost performance 
and so on. This means that architects are forced to continually test 
out these drawings, and then change them manually, if they do not 
conform to requirements. By way of contrast, if architects were to 
operate in a ‘data-driven mode’, then all information would already be 
embedded in the system, the design would automatically conform to 
these requirements and the system would recommend the best 
options to architects. In short, for He, architects need to shift from an 
information-free graphic-driven mode of operating to an information-
rich data-driven mode. This would make the design process far more 
efﬁ  cient. And this is where AI comes in.  33   
 AI, for He, is not a replacement of the architect. Rather, she sees 
it as a technology that can assist the architect, a prosthetic extension 
to the architect. Eventually, the idea is to hand over the routine and 
repetitive work to the computer – work that the computer is probably 
capable of doing faster and more accurately – so as to leave the 
architect more time to devote to the creative process. 
   FIGURE  7 .4 Xkool, Building Images (2019), generated using StyleGANs 
(horizontal composite image).         
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 180
 As He comments, ‘Technology will free us from repetition and 
allow us to concentrate on things with added value to society. ’  34   T h e  
role of AI here is to tirelessly adapt to the ever-changing requirements 
of the design process, and to come up with informed suggestions. 
As He notes, ‘There is no doubt that future cities will still be created 
by the human will, but in a different way, empowered by technologies. ’  35   
Eventually, perhaps, AI might be able to operate autonomously in 
almost all industries, but for now it remains merely an assistant for 
architects: ‘Current AI is narrow intelligence – it can only complete 
speciﬁ  c tasks under the guidance of humans. The role of humans in 
managing AI is ever more important. ’  36    
   A brief history of XKool  
 The story of XKool can be understood as a story of constantly evolving 
design processes. He and Yang ﬁ  rst used AI back in 2011 in an entry 
for the international competition for the Shenzhen Bay Eco-Tech City, 
which subsequently became one of the ﬁ  rst AI-assisted designs 
to be actually built. The breakthrough, however, was when they 
developed a deep-learning-based technique involving a ‘design brain’ 
and an ‘evaluation brain’ with the formation of XKool in 2016. Broadly 
put, the role of the ‘design brain’ was to generate a range of possible 
design solutions based on a massive dataset of previous designs. 
The role of the ‘evaluation brain’ was to evaluate and rank these 
designs based on a data-driven evaluation model and local compliance 
rules. In effect, the ‘design brain’ and the ‘evaluation brain’ worked 
together, with the ‘design brain’ generating possible designs, and the 
‘evaluation brain’ evaluating them. 
 This ﬁ  rst set of tools, however, had their limitations. Although 
useful in speeding up the design process, reducing costs and 
enhancing design productivity and quality, they did not provide 
sufﬁ  cient ‘references’ to be really practical. The situation was helped 
with improvements in machine learning that they subsequently 
incorporated into their working method.  37   This led to the launch of 
their ‘Rosetta Stone’ research initiative in 2017 , so called because it 
looked at the possibility of generating a range of design outcomes 
based on different styles of architecture, where these styles were 
THE FUTURE OF THE ARCHITECTURAL OFFICE 181
seen as the equivalent of different languages.  38   They then developed 
a large database of different design styles, based on material 
extracted from previous architectural designs, and are now allowing 
users and companies to customize their own style. 
 Their real advance, however, came with the success of the 
AlphaGo ZERO initiative, where, as noted earlier, the model taught 
itself to play Go without being given the rules of the game. Inspired 
by this, XKool developed a new technique using reinforcement 
learning that meant they did not need to rely on datasets of existing
