               9  
 The future of AI            
   N o account of AI would be complete without a few predictions 
about its future. AI, after all, is all about predictions. It recognises 
patterns and predicts the future. And its success lies in the accuracy 
of its predictions. 
 Equally, the history of AI can be read as a history of predictions 
about the future of AI. From Turing onwards there have been a 
number of predictions about its potential. Of course, on occasions 
these have proved to be hopelessly unrealistic.  1   And it could be 
argued that inﬂ  ated optimism about the potential of AI was in part 
responsible for the various AI winters. Nonetheless, many predictions 
about the potential of AI have proved surprisingly accurate. 
 Likewise, we human beings are also good at making predictions. 
Our brains might be quite weak at some tasks, such as logical 
argumentation, memory and making calculations, but they are 
extremely strong in the ﬁ  eld of pattern recognition and making 
predictions based on those patterns. Our brains are inherently 
predictive. In the simple act of catching a ball, for example, our brains 
are constantly updating their own calculations and predictions about 
the ﬂ  ight of the ball.  2   We make predictions to avoid both large-scale 
natural disasters, such as hurricanes, tsunamis and epidemics, and 
smaller-scale hazards – and this, it has been claimed, is one of the 
main reasons why we have a brain in the ﬁ  rst place.  3   But we also 
make predictions to proﬁ  t by gambling and investing in the stock 
market. Moreover, as we have seen, neuroscientists now claim that 
perception itself is predictive.  4   ‘Brains, ’ as Andy Clark notes, ‘are 
essentially prediction machines. ’  5   
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 218
 What predictions, then, have human beings made about the future 
of AI? Many predictions about AI could be described as mere 
extrapolations based on existing weak signals and emerging trends. 
In  Machines that Think: The Future of Artiﬁ  cial Intelligence  , for 
example, Toby Walsh offers a series of predictions about the year 
2050, many of which appear quite straightforward. Walsh predicts 
that cybercrime will become a major problem, that our health will be 
monitored by our phones, and that news will be generated by AI. He 
also predicts that we will talk to our buildings, which is hardly much 
of a prediction, in that we already do through Alexa and other devices. 
In short, perhaps the most surprising aspect about some of Walsh’s 
predictions is how unsurprising they are. 
 Some of his other predictions, however, are perhaps less obvious. 
Walsh predicts, for example, that we will be able to ‘live on’ after our 
deaths through a personalised AI chatbot: ‘It will talk like you, it will 
know the story of your past, it will comfort your family after you 
die. ’  6   In fact this is not far away. We already have avatars that can 
mimic individuals quite convincingly. Moreover, personal digital twins 
are now being produced as an AIcontrolled assistant that will know 
and understand the user intimately. This is the vision of Mustafa 
Suleyman, co-founder of DeepMind, and fomer CEO of Inﬂ  ection AI, 
   FIGURE  9.1 Behnaz Farahi, Living Breathing Wall (2013). This wall 
detects a human voice detected using a Kinect apparatus. The signal then 
passes through an Arduino control panel to activate a series of Stepper 
m o t o r s .          
THE FUTURE OF AI 219
where he was developing a personal AI assistant or – ‘personal 
intelligence’ – called ‘pi’. Suleyman describes ‘pi’ as a form of ‘chief 
of staff’ that will provide constantly, invaluable assistance: ‘(It will) 
intimately know your personal information, be completely aligned 
with your interests, and help you manage and process all the 
information you need. ’  7   It is hardly a stretch, then, to imagine that we 
will soon have convincing personal replicas that will ‘live on’ after our 
deaths. 
 Walsh’s most interesting prediction, however, relates to the future 
of driving. He predicts that we will eventually be banned from driving, 
as a result of the introduction of self-driving cars.  8   His argument is 
this: as self-driving cars become more and more reliable, we 
will drive less and less. As a consequence, our driving skills will 
diminish, so that insurance premiums will increase unless we are 
using a self-driving car.  9   Gradually, we will become resigned to not 
driving, to the point that many young people will not even bother to 
learn to drive. Finally, self-driving cars will take over, and driving itself 
will be banned. 
 Change, of course, is often incremental, just as the development 
of the self-driving car is happening gradually over time. In the case of 
a Tesla car, for example, this takes the form of regular software 
updates.  10   What is perhaps most interesting about Walsh’s prediction, 
however, is his comment about our change of attitudes: ‘We won’t 
be allowed to drive cars any more, and we will not notice or even 
care. ’  11   
 Indeed, when change is incremental, we often don’t notice it. It’s 
a bit like the story about boiling a frog. Drop a frog into a boiling pan 
of water, so they say, and it will leap straight out. But start off with 
tepid water, and gradually increase the temperature, and the frog will 
not notice it. And that, they say, is how to boil a frog. 
 Nor do we often care so much about what has gone. The human 
brain is plastic, we are told by neuroscientists. We adapt easily to the 
new. And in a culture of pervasive amnesia, as Andreas Huyssen has 
described our current epoch, we quickly forget the past.  12   How many 
of us even remember what life was really like before the internet and 
mobile phones had been invented, and when fax machines offered 
the best way to send images electronically? But there was a time, 
and it was not so long ago. 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 220
 But what if we were to push the implications of some of Walsh’s 
ideas still further? Walsh, for example, predicts that by 2050 we will 
talk to our rooms. This is hardly much of a prediction in that many of 
us already talk to our rooms through Alexa, while researchers, such 
as Behnaz Farahi have developed walls that respond to human 
speech.  13   Meanwhile we can now talk to ChatGPT and other AI 
applications, as both Haley and Kurzweil had already predicted that 
we will talk to our computers. But what if we were to think through 
the consequences of the increasing use of voice commands? Would 
we not start to privilege speech increasingly over writing, once 
speech-based informational systems have become sufﬁ  ciently 
advanced? Would podcasts not take precedent over books? Would 
pens, pencils and even keyboards go out of fashion? 
 What if we were to push the implications of this further still? What 
if Haley is correct, and we start to design using voice commands and 
hand gestures, instead of by drawing? And what if the introduction of 
diffusion models were to make drawing potentially unnecessary? 
Would drawing even go out of fashion? And what if, as a consequence, 
we were to gradually lose our capacity to draw? And if so, again, 
would we even notice or care?  
   FIGURE  9.2 Behnaz Farahi, Can the Subaltern Speak? (2020). These 
overtly political masks begin to communicate with each other, blinking 
their multiple eyelashes in rapid succession using a Markov-Chain-
generated Morse code. The work is intended to illustrate how women 
could develop their own private language to destabilize the authority of 
patriarchy.         
THE FUTURE OF AI 221
   Kurzweil’s predictions  
 If there is anyone who has made a name for himself by going out on 
a limb with often seemingly outrageous predictions, it is surely Ray 
Kurzweil. His predictions, nonetheless, about technology in general 
and AI in particular have proved to be remarkably accurate. Indeed, 
Kurzweil himself claims that 86 per cent of his predictions have been 
correct.  14   
 Although he acknowledges that many cultural events are 
impossible to predict, Kurzweil believes that ‘fundamental measures 
of information technology follow predictable and exponential 
trajectories, belying the conventional wisdom that “you can’t predict 
the future” ’.  15   Kurzweil used to subscribe to the logic of Moore’s Law 
in his earlier predictions.  16   Moore’s Law is derived from a comment 
made in 1965 by Gordon Moore, then CEO of Fairchild Semiconductor 
and later the co-founder of Intel, that the number of transistors on an 
integrated circuit board would double every year, while unit costs 
would fall correspondingly.  17   Moore subsequently revised this 
forecast to that number doubling every two years.  18   He then 
developed this observation into a theory of exponential growth, that 
Kurzweil himself applied not only to semi-conductor circuits but also 
other forms of technology.  19   The simple laptop, for example, has 
conformed broadly to these principles. 
 Exponential growth, however, cannot go on forever. As Murray 
Shanahan notes, the laws of physics dictate that every technological 
trend must meet a plateau eventually.  20   Rodney Brooks argues 
that the whole idea has been ‘oversold’.  21   Meanwhile, Kurzweil 
himself came to believe that Moore’s Law would not hold true beyond 
2020. He has therefore developed his own ‘Law of Accelerating 
Returns’: 
  An analysis of the history of technology shows that technological 
change is exponential, contrary to the common-sense ‘intuitive 
linear’ view. So we won’t experience 100 years of progress in the 
21st century – it will be more like 20,000 years of progress (at 
today’s rate). The ‘returns’, such as chip speed and cost-
effectiveness, also increase exponentially. There’s even exponential 
growth in the rate of exponential growth.  22    
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 222
 In 1990, Kurzweil published  The Age of Intelligent Machines,   a book to 
which a number of other signiﬁ  cant futurists and computer scientists 
contributed. Here Kurzweil speculates about the possibility of computers 
achieving a level of intelligence comparable to human intelligence.  23   H e  
goes on to make a series of other predictions, such as the fact that a 
computer would beat the world chess champion by 2000, as noted 
above;  24   that there would be driverless cars ‘by well into the ﬁ  rst half of 
the twenty-ﬁ  rst century’;  25   that planes would become pilotless;  26   a n d  
that laser weapons would be developed.  27   Importantly, Kurzweil also 
predicts that, as computers will begin to compete with human beings, 
paradoxically they will begin to reveal what it is to be human.  28   
 Perhaps the most stunning prediction that Kurzweil makes in this 
book, however, relates to the use of laptops with wi-ﬁ  , and the 
possibility of students having access to all the libraries in the world 
‘from their school bags’, and of sending ‘love notes’ by wi-ﬁ  : 
 Wireless networks will allow the easy sharing of courseware, 
submissions by students of papers, exams, courseware responses, 
and other creations, electronic mail and other communications 
(e.g. love notes). By being plugged into international networks of 
information, students will have immediate access to all the libraries 
of the world right from their school bags.  29   
  
 Let us not forget that this prediction was made back in 1990. 
 In  The Age of Spiritual Machines: When Computers Exceed Human 
Intelligence,   published in 1999, Kurzweil makes a series of further 
predictions for the year 2009.  30   His predictions about computers 
in particular have proved to be surprisingly accurate. ‘Portable 
computers, ’ notes Kurzweil, ‘will have become dramatically lighter 
and thinner than the notebook computers of ten years earlier. ’  31   Here 
one thinks of the MacBook Air launched in 2008, which is signiﬁ  cantly 
lighter than bulky, early laptops available at the time of his prediction. 
Indeed he predicts that we will even be able to ‘wear’ our computers.  32   
Now, of course, we have become so used to iPods, FitBit watches, 
mobile phones and Bluetooth headsets that it is difﬁ  cult to recall a 
time when such everyday items did not exist. And he predicts that 
they will have no keyboards,  33   that cables would disappear and that 
we would be using wi-ﬁ  .  34   In an age of touchscreens on mobile 
THE FUTURE OF AI 223
phones, and universal wi-ﬁ  , it is difﬁ  cult to even recall that there was 
a time when these facilities were not available. Kurzweil also 
accurately predicts the introduction of facial recognition for our 
devices  35   and the use of AR goggles.  36   Again what stands out, then, 
about Kurzweil’s predictions is how accurate they have proved to be. 
 On a few occasions, however, some of Kurzweil’s predictions have 
failed to materialise in time.  37   It is as though Kurzweil underestimates 
how long these developments would take to become commercially 
viable, although they might be technically possible in the laboratory.  38   
In 1990, for example, Kurzweil claims that, by 1999, ‘Consumers will 
be able to sit down at their home computers and design their own 
clothes to their own precise measurements and style requirements 
using friendly, computer-assisted design software. ’  39   Likewise, 
Kurzweil predicts that by 2009, translating telephones will allow 
users to speak to each other in different languages, and that most 
text will be created using speech recognition.  40   Although there are 
now apps that can do this, they were not available in 2009. 
 Again, on other occasions Kurzweil is only partially correct. For 
example, he predicts that by 2009 most long-distance travel would 
be made using ‘cybernetic chauffeurs’, a form of auto-pilot, although 
traditional transportation would be used for local travel.  41   He is careful 
to distinguish these from full self-driving cars, and, while we already 
have cruise control and automatic warnings when we stray out of our 
lane, by 2009 we did not have ‘cybernetic chauffeurs’ as such.  
   AGI and beyond  
 Artiﬁ  cial General Intelligence (AGI) – sometimes referred to as 
human-level intelligence – is a form of general-purpose AI that is not 
restricted to a limited range of abilities. OpenAI deﬁ  nes AGI as ‘highly 
autonomous systems that outperform humans at most economically 
valuable work. ’  42   
 Most working in the ﬁ  eld of AI are conﬁ  dent that AGI is achievable. 
Indeed, OpenAI, like other leading AI companies, such as DeepMind, 
is dedicated to achieving AGI. Certainly, for Kurzweil, there is no 
question that – eventually – we will achieve AGI. His latest prediction 
for the advent of AGI is the year 2029.  43   
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 224
 Some, however, are beginning to question whether AGI is so far 
away. Certainly, the recent ‘shock’ at the advances made by LLMs 
has forced many to reconsider their views. For example, Chalmers 
one of the leading philosophers of consciousness, believes that with 
the launch of GPT-3 LLMs were already showing hints of general 
intelligence, and were suggested a potential ‘mindless’ path to AGI.  44   
In fact, Musk suspects that OpenAI might have already achieved 
AGI.  45   Perhaps the most extreme position, however, is that of Arcas 
and Peter Norvig, who are adamant that AGI is already here: ‘Today’s 
most advanced AI models have many ﬂ  aws, but decades from now, 
they will be recognized as the ﬁ  rst true examples of artiﬁ  cial general 
intelligence. ’  46   However, Jacob Browning and Yann Lecun are 
convinced that AGI will never be reached through LLMs: ‘ An artiﬁ  cial 
intelligence system trained on words and sentences alone will never 
approximate human understanding. ”  47    
   FIGURE  9.3 Eduard Haiman, Endlessskyscraper (2021), generated using 
StyleGANs and other techniques.         
THE FUTURE OF AI 225
   The Singularity  
 Kurzweil’s most famous prediction, however, relates to what he calls 
the Singularity, a stage within the predicted development of AI in 
relation to human intelligence, that leads from human-level 
intelligence to superintelligence. Not so long after AGI, according to 
Kurzweil, we will reach the Singularity. His most recent prediction for 
the date of the Singularity is 2045.  48   
 The term ‘singularity’ comes from physics, and refers to a moment, 
such as the Big Bang, when mathematics begins to break down, and 
so does our comprehension.  49   John von Neumann subsequently 
relates the term to the accelerating pace of technological development, 
which Stanislaw Ulam paraphrases as the moment when ‘the ever 
accelerating progress of technology . . . gives the appearance of 
approaching some singularity in the history of the race beyond which 
human affairs, as we know them, could not continue’.  50   Vernon 
Vinge later further expanded this deﬁ  nition, coining the term the 
‘technological singularity’: 
  What are the consequences of this event? When greater-than-
human intelligence drives progress, that progress will be much 
more rapid. In fact, there seems no reason why progress itself 
would not involve the creation of still more intelligent entities – on 
a still-shorter time scale. The best analogy that I see is with the 
evolutionary past: Animals can adapt to problems and make 
inventions, but often no faster than natural selection can do its 
work – the world acts as its own simulator. We humans have the 
ability to internalize the world and conduct ‘what if’s’ in our heads; 
we can solve many problems thousands of times faster than 
natural selection. Now, by creating the means to execute those 
simulations at much higher speeds, we are entering a regime as 
radically different from our human past as we humans are from 
the lower animals. From the human point of view, this change will 
be a throwing away of all the previous rules, perhaps in a blink of 
an eye, an exponential runway beyond any hope of control.  51    
 Kurzweil builds upon these earlier descriptions and describes the 
Singularity as an explosion of intelligence, a moment of technological 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 226
change so rapid that it amounts to a ‘rupture in the fabric of human 
history’, with enormous consequences for the future of the human 
race: 
  Within a few decades, machine intelligence will surpass human 
intelligence, leading to the Singularity – technological change so 
rapid and profound it represents a rupture in the fabric of human 
history. The implications include the merger of biological and 
nonbiological intelligence, immortal software-based humans, and 
ultra-high levels of intelligence that expand outward in the universe 
at the speed of light.  52    
 From a philosophical perspective, David Chalmers agrees with Kurzweil 
that the Singularity is indeed possible.  53   Meanwhile, although some are 
ambivalent – or even negative – about the impact of these developments, 
Kurzweil himself is unequivocally positive. Machines have already 
   FIGURE  9.4 Ila Colombo, Symbiocene (2024), generated using 
MidJourney V6.         
THE FUTURE OF AI 227
improved our lives, and Kurzweil believes that they are likely to improve 
them still further: ‘What’s actually happening is [machines] are powering 
all of us. They’re making us smarter. They may not yet be inside our 
bodies, but, by the 2030s, we will connect our neocortex, the part of 
our brain where we do our thinking, to the cloud. ’  54   Kurzweil is convinced 
that machines will not only make us more intelligent, but will also bring 
other beneﬁ  ts to humanity: ‘We’re going to be funnier, we’re going to 
be better at music. We’re going to be sexier. ’  55    
   Superintelligence  
 Once the Singularity has been reached, it is a relatively short step to 
superintelligence or artiﬁ  cial superintelligence (ASI).  56   Nick Bostrom 
deﬁ  nes ASI as ‘any intellect that greatly exceeds the cognitive 
performance of humans in virtually all domains of interest’.  57   T h e  
possibilities of ASI are seemingly endless: 
  By deﬁ  nition, an ASI can perform better than us in any conceivable 
task, including intellectual skills. It could engage in scientiﬁ  c 
research and teach itself new abilities, improve its own code, 
create unlimited copies of itself, choose better ways of deploying 
its computational resources, and it could even transform the 
environment on Earth, or colonise other planets.  58    
 Closely aligned with superintelligence is the notion of ‘ultraintelligence’, 
a term that Good coined back in 1965: ‘ A machine that can far surpass 
all intellectual activities of any man however clever. ’  59   
 The question remains, of course, whether we would even be able 
to recognise the intelligence of a superintelligent or ultraintelligent 
machine, in that the intelligence that it would display would be far 
beyond our comprehension. As Elon Musk comments, ‘The biggest 
mistake that I see artiﬁ  cial intelligence researchers making is 
assuming that they’re intelligent. Yeah, they’re not, compared to AI. 
And so like, a lot of them cannot imagine something smarter than 
themselves, but AI will be vastly smarter – vastly. ’  60   
 It has also been claimed that an ultraintelligent or superintelligent 
machine would effectively be the last invention that human kind would 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 228
ever need to make, in that once we reach that point, superintelligent 
machines would be able to design other, even more intelligent machines, 
and human beings would never be able to compete: ‘Since the design of 
machines is one of these intellectual activities, an ultraintelligent machine 
could design even better machines. There would then unquestionably be 
an “intelligence explosion, ” and the intelligence of man would be left far 
behind. Thus the ﬁ  rst ultraintelligent machine is the last invention that 
man need ever make. ’  61   
 And once we reach that point, is it not ‘game over’ for human 
beings?  
   Prediction errors  
 ‘Prediction is very difﬁ  cult, ’ Niels Bohr remarks, ‘especially about the 
future. ’  62   Get it right, and everyone will tell you that it was obvious 
that it would happen. Get it wrong, and everyone will tell you that 
it was equally obvious that it would never happen. Predictions also 
tend to be strewn with errors.  63   This is why techniques such as 
backpropagation are used to correct errors and improve the accuracy 
of predictions in AI. But predictions about the future of AI itself seem 
to be particularly problematic. 
 Dates themselves are difﬁ  cult enough. Take predictions about 
AGI. Martin Ford conducted a survey, with the median date being 
2099. But in fact there have been many different surveys of timelines, 
none of which agree.  64   Although Kurzweil expects AGI to be achieved 
by 2029, Rodney Brooks thinks that we still have a long way to go, 
and does not expect that we will see AGI until 2200: ‘ Any AI program 
in the world right now is an idiot savant living in the sea of now. ’  65   
 The real problem, however, is that there is even less agreement 
about the actual deﬁ  nition of AGI. Francesca Rossi conﬂ  ates the 
Singularity with AGI.  66   Likewise, both Ford and Kurzweil conﬂ  ate 
‘human level AI’ with AGI.  67   Meanwhile, Russell considers that the 
quest for AGI is really just the quest for AI itself: ‘ AGI is actually what 
we have always called artiﬁ  cial intelligence. We are just not ﬁ  nished 
yet, and we have not created AGI yet. ’  68   In short, AGI and other 
stages in the development of AI remain difﬁ  cult to deﬁ  ne, let alone 
to predict. The more we look, the less consensus we ﬁ  nd about AGI. 
THE FUTURE OF AI 229
 In any case, how would we know precisely when any of these 
stages have been reached? And how could any such developments 
be universal? Surely different domains progress at different rates. 
What might already hold true for games of chess or Go, for example, 
is unlike to also apply to other domains. 
 Perhaps our biggest prediction error of all, however, has been to 
assume that there could be any such thing as AGI. Kevin Kelly, for 
example, believes that the entire project of AGI is misconceived.  69   H e  
argues that intelligence is not one-dimensional: ‘The most common 
misconception about artiﬁ  cial intelligence is that natural intelligence 
is a single dimension. ’  70   Rather, it is constituted by ‘many types and 
modes of cognition, each one a continuum’.  71   Intelligence, moreover, 
is effectively an eco-system, involving many different types of 
thinking, and suffused throughout the whole body. 
 Mitchell takes a similar view, noting that AGI is not a concept that 
would likely be entertained by cognitive scientists: ‘Most cognitive 
scientists would agree that intelligence is not a quantity that can be 
measured on a single scale and arbitrarily dialed up and down but 
rather a complex integration of general and specialized capabilities 
that are, for the most part, adaptive in a speciﬁ  c evolutionary niche. ’  72    
   Hinton, AI and human cognition  
 If, then, we want to measure advances in AI against human cognitive 
capacities, we need to take a less monolithic approach. We need to 
abandon the one-dimensional notion of any homogeneous stage of 
development, such as AGI or indeed the Singularity, and seek to track 
the emerging capabilities of AI not against natural intelligence, as 
such, but against a more differentiated landscape of various human 
cognitive capacities. As such, we would need to adopt a more 
nuanced approach to human cognition itself, and address intelligence 
in the broader context of other cognitive capacities, such as 
knowledge, understanding and thinking. Even so, we need to accept 
that we might not get anywhere near close enough to make 
meaningful comparisons between AI and human cognitive capacities. 
 Here, it might be helpful to sound out the opinions of Geoffrey 
Hinton, not only because he holds a pre-eminent position within the 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 230
ﬁ  eld of AI, but also because he additionally has a background in 
experimental psychology. 
 Like many others, Hinton accepts that ChatGPT most probably 
‘knows’ around 10,000 times as much as any one person. For Hinton, 
the paradox here is that ChatGPT has only around a trillion 
connections, whereas the human brain has ten times as many.  73   S o  
how has ChatGPT managed to gain this knowledge so quickly? One 
reason, Hinton postulates, is that computers can ‘learn’ far more 
quickly than human beings by sharing their knowledge instantaneously. 
As Hinton notes, 
 These digital intelligences have something that we don’t have that 
makes them much better. When one of them knows something, it 
can tell all the others. That’s what we don’t have with people. So 
imagine if you have 10,000 people, and imagine if when one 
person learns something, everybody knew it. You could learn a lot 
more stuff, right? And that’s why things like ChatGPT knows like 
10,000 times as much as any one person. It’s because when you 
train it, there’s lots of different copies looking at different bits of 
the data, and learning stuff, and they can all combine what they 
learn instantly, with a bandwidth of like trillions of bits.  74   
 As such, we should perhaps look beyond the straightforward 
exponential logic of Moore’s Law, and understand the viral spread of 
knowledge in an age of parallelism and scaling as something almost 
akin to an explosion.  75    
   Hinton, AI and understanding  
 Understanding goes beyond straightforward knowledge. If knowledge 
can be understood as the sum total of all the information that has 
been absorbed, understanding involves the ability to grasp the 
signiﬁ  cance of that knowledge. 
 Traditional approaches have viewed AI as purely mechanical, and 
lacking any real level of understanding, largely because cognition has 
to be embodied – grounded and in contact with the real, physical 
world – while AI is not embodied in any way.  76   
THE FUTURE OF AI 231
 However, some are beginning to claim that through their extensive 
training data LLMs can develop some form of ‘understanding’ that 
constitutes a kind of embodied knowledge. As Arcas puts it, ‘Large 
language models illustrate for the ﬁ  rst time the way language 
understanding and intelligence can be dissociated from all the 
embodied and emotional characteristics we share with each other 
and with many other animals . . . Knowing what we now know, it 
would be hard to claim that a biological brain can encode or manipulate 
these patterns in ways that a digital neural net inherently cannot. ’  77   
 Certainly, Hinton himself is now convinced that AI has developed 
some form of understanding. He ﬁ  rst began to realise this after PaLM 
(Google’s version of ChatGPT) explained a joke to him. If it can explain 
a joke, Hinton reasoned, it must have been capable of ‘understanding’ 
that joke.  78   ‘I’d been telling people for years that it’s gonna be a long 
time before AI can tell you why jokes are funny, ’ he says. ‘It was a 
kind of litmus test. ’  79   
 Hinton, however, goes even further. At a basic level, chatbots work 
by predicting the next word in a sentence. But let’s not think that they 
are just making statistical predictions or doing auto-complete. To 
make their prediction, they must ‘understand’ that sentence, and, 
indeed, in order for them to reply to a question, they must answer 
that question – whether or not that ‘understanding’ is quite the same 
as human understanding.  80    
   Hinton, AI and intelligence  
 We can now begin to address the question of intelligence itself, 
which is more complex than simple knowledge or understanding. 
Intelligence refers to the ability to apply that knowledge effectively. 
 Much depends, of course, on how we understand ‘intelligence’.
There are, surely, different forms of intelligence, and it would be 
wrong to limit ourselves to a deﬁ  nition tied to human intelligence. For 
Hinton, the two main types of intelligence: biological intelligence and 
mechanical intelligence. We therefore need to distinguish the 
intelligence of AI from the intelligence of humans: ‘It’s a completely 
different form of intelligence. ’  81   
 Hinton would therefore claim that AI has not only matched human 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 232
intelligence, but also surpassed it – by some considerable margin. He 
believes that AI has already developed a form of ‘intelligence’ – albeit 
not a natural form of intelligence – that is nonetheless vastly superior 
to human intelligence, ‘a new and better form of intelligence’.  82   
Moreover, the intelligence of AI will continue to grow at a remarkable 
rate. Mo Gawdat, for example, now predicts that AI will be a  billion   
times more intelligent than us by 2037 .  83   
 Clearly, human intelligence does not constitute the absolute 
pinnacle of intelligence. Arguably, there are already forms of 
intelligence that far exceed the intelligence of the human mind, of 
which we might be unaware. The real problem, however, in comparing 
AI to human intelligence, as Melanie Mitchell and David Kracauer 
point out, is that as yet we still do not fully understand human 
intelligence.  84   Until we do, we are effectively comparing one black 
box with another.  
   Hinton, AI and thinking  
 While intelligence describes our cognitive potential, ‘thinking’ goes 
further, and engages more precisely with how we use that potential. 
Now, the general assumption has always been that AI cannot think, 
and will not be able to do so for some time. But with the development 
of LLMs and the discovery of their emergent abilities, and increasing 
speculation that AI might have reached AGI, some authorities are 
beginning to question whether AI is actually starting to think. Hinton 
is one of them. 
 ‘Is AI beginning to think?’ 
 He notes: 
 I’m talking to a chatbot, and the answers it’s giving me seem a bit 
strange to me, and I suddenly realise that it thinks that I am a 
teenage girl. And I say, ‘What demographic do you think I am?’ 
THE FUTURE OF AI 233
And it said that it thinks that I am a teenage girl. So the question 
is, when I said, ‘I suddenly realised that it thinks that I am a 
teenage girl’, was that a metaphorical use of the word ‘think’? Or 
was that just the same as we use ‘think’? And I strongly believe 
that use of the word  think  , when I said ‘it thinks that I am a teenage 
girl’ was exactly the same of using the word ‘think’ as we do with 
people.  85   
 Is AI beginning to think?  
   Strong AI  
 Beyond this, Hinton believes that AI is capable of not only thinking, but 
can even performing reasoning – including complex logical reasoning.  86   
Now, since reasoning occurs in moments of consciousness, this raises 
the question as to whether computers might even be able to achieve 
consciousness. This is referred to as ‘Strong AI’. As noted earlier, most 
would argue that for the moment all we have is ‘Weak AI’, where AI 
can operate as a form of ‘zombie’, as David Chalmers puts it, but does 
not possess consciousness.  87   The related term, sentience – the 
capacity to feel sensations and emotions – refers to a minimalistic 
notion of consciousness, that would need to be accompanied by self-
awareness, sapience, intentionality and other higher level features in 
order to meet the level of consciousness itself.  88   
 Now Hinton himself believes that LLMs deﬁ  nitely have 
‘experiences’, but stop short of claiming that they are conscious – just 
yet, ‘I think that they probably don’t have much self-awareness at 
present. So in that sense, I don’t think that they are conscious, [but] I 
think that they will have self-awareness in time. ’  89   Philosopher of 
consciousness Daniel Dennett agrees with him, in that he believes 
that in principle it is possible for AI to achieve consciousness, but that 
it is much more difﬁ  cult than people in AI often think.  90   
 The problem with consciousness is that it remains one of the 
most complex and contested subjects of all. There is little agreement 
as to what constitutes consciousness, assuming, of course, that it 
actually exists. And, even if AI could possess consciousness, the 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 234
main challenge would perhaps be to ascertain whether AI might be 
conscious in the ﬁ  rst place. 
 Certainly, we would not want to fall into the trap of former Google 
engineer Blake Lemoine, who had claimed that LaMDA was sentient, 
because it  appeared   to be so.  91   Indeed, it is all too easy to 
anthropomorphize a chatbot, and perceive it as though it is a genuine 
human interlocutor. But we should be cautious not to judge it as 
conscious, because it  appears   conscious. To achieve Strong AI, a 
computer must be genuinely conscious. This would be tantamount to 
subscribing to the Turing Test, which judges whether computers 
might pass as humans, simply on the basis of outward appearances.  92   
 Perhaps the most telling critique of the Turing Test has been made 
by philosopher John Searle, using his famous ‘Chinese Room’ 
thought experiment.  93   Imagine that a computer program has been 
designed that seems as though it is capable of understanding 
Chinese. The program is able to take English text and translate it into 
Chinese text so convincingly that it passes the ‘Turing Test’, in that no 
one realises that it is just a computer program. Now imagine if Searle 
himself were to ﬁ  nd himself in a closed room equipped with an 
instruction manual in English that described the translation process. 
He would be able, surely, to process the material in a similar way. The 
only problem is that he does not understand Chinese. This is 
analogous to the question of consciousness. A computer might 
 appear   to be conscious, but it does not understand what it is doing. 
By extension, in the Chinese Room Experiment, Searle might  appear   
to be able to translate Chinese, but he does not understand Chinese. 
The Chinese Room experiment therefore challenges the logic of the 
Turing Test. For Searle, the Turing Test is inadequate, because it only 
tests whether a computer  appears   to be human. It does not test 
whether the computer is  actually   human. It could be an illusion. There 
is, in other words, a signiﬁ  cant difference between an entity  appearing   
to be something, and actually being it.  94   
 However, Dennett disagrees with Searle, and believes that 
consciousness is itself an illusion.  95   Our consciousness, for Dennett, 
is merely our representation of the world. This makes some sense, if 
we buy into the commonly accepted view that consciousness is a 
kind of ‘inner movie’ going on inside our head.  96   As such, consciousness 
would simply be a question of a representation – of the  appearance   
THE FUTURE OF AI 235
of consciousness – which echoes the model of how Plato describes 
our perception of reality in his allegory of the cave, as the mere 
appearance of reality. It would also chime with Seth’s view that our 
perception of reality is itself a controlled hallucination. 
 If so, in a neat reversal, we could use the very same Chinese 
Room experiment to challenge Searle’s own argument. In other 
words, consciousness itself could be as much of an illusion, as the 
potential illusion of AI being conscious or sentient. 
 Whether Dennett is correct or not, it would appear that the notion 
that consciousness can offer some kind of reliable access to ‘reality’ 
is clearly debatable. Until we understand precisely what consciousness 
is, there is a danger that we might be overstating its importance. 
 Yet we also need to question whether we can legitimately connect 
consciousness with intelligence, let alone with AI. Hassabis, for 
example, argues that intelligence and consciousness do not even 
depend upon each other: ‘You can have intelligence without 
consciousness, and you can have consciousness without human-level 
intelligence. ’  97   Roger Penrose concurs with this view.  98   Seth also 
agrees: ‘Consciousness and intelligence are entirely different things. 
You don’t have to be smart to suffer, but you probably have to be alive. ’  99   
 Consciousness, as Max Tegmark remarks, is probably all that 
humans beings will be able to cling on to, once computers exceed the 
intelligence of human beings: ‘We humans have built out identity of 
being  Homo sapiens  , the smartest entities around. As we prepare to 
be humbled by ever smarter machines, I suggest that we rebrand 
ourselves as  Homo sentiens  ! ’   100   Besides, if we see the world in terms 
of extended intelligence, as a coupling between human and machine, 
where human consciousness is automatically included in the loop, the 
need for a machine to also have consciousness seems less imperative. 
 Equally, we could also argue that AI does not necessarily need to 
achieve consciousness.  101   What if we were to judge AI, for example, 
not in terms of human intelligence, but on its own terms? As such, 
the question of consciousness becomes largely irrelevant. The issue 
becomes what tasks AI can perform, not whether AI can display 
human-level consciousness. As Yuval Noah Harari notes, ‘There might 
be several alternative ways leading to superintelligence, only some of 
which pass through the straits of consciousness. ’  102   This leads Harari 
to pose the important question, ‘Which of the two is really important, 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 236
intelligence or consciousness?’  103   As far as corporations and armies 
are concerned, the answer is clear: ‘[I]ntelligence is mandatory, but 
consciousness is optional. ’  104   In short, as long as a machine does its 
job well, does it really matter whether it is conscious or not? Provided 
my toaster toasts bread well, does it need to be conscious? Provided 
AI can generate good designs, does it need to be conscious? The 
answer, surely, is ‘no’.  
   Alien intelligence  
 How are we to even refer to AI once it has reached these levels 
of intelligence, so as to avoid anthropomorphising it? Should we use 
the term in inverted commas – ‘intelligence’ – just as perhaps we 
should with other equally problematic terms, such as ‘learning’, 
‘understanding’ and ‘thinking’, when referring to AI? Or should we 
adopt an entirely different terminology, so that it is clear that we are 
dealing with something totally ‘other’? 
 The term ‘alien intelligence’ offers a useful strategy for referring to 
AI, not only because it avoids the use of the somewhat condescending 
term ‘artiﬁ  cial’, but also because it helps to prevents us from 
anthropomorphising AI.  105   Others too have adopted the term. Yuval 
Harari, for example, uses ‘alien intelligence’ metaphorically to 
describe the ‘invasion’ of a highly intelligent form of AI that can make 
decisions independently and create new ideas. For Harari, such will 
be the superiority of this ‘alien intelligence’ that it will lead to the end 
of human-dominated history: 
  This is the end of history – the end of human dominated history. 
History will continue with someone else in control. In 5 years 
there will be a technology that can make decisions independently, 
and that can create new ideas independently. Maybe they’ll be 
nice. Maybe they’ll solve cancer and climate change, but we are 
not sure. I’m tending to think of it more in terms of, really, an alien 
invasion – an alien ﬂ  eet of spaceships coming from Planet Zircon, 
or whatever – with highly intelligent beings. This is what we are 
facing, except that the aliens are not coming in spaceships from 
Planet Zircon. They are coming from the laboratory. If humans are 
THE FUTURE OF AI 237
divided among themselves, and are in an arms race, then it 
becomes impossible to contain this alien intelligence.  106    
 The important issue, however, is not whether AI can match 
human-level intelligence, but whether humans can ever hope to 
compete with the ‘alien intelligence’ of AI. Indeed, it could be argued 
that the whole notion of judging AI from a human perspective – and 
viewing it as inferior, because it does not possess consciousness and 
so on – is misconceived, and that we should adopt the opposite 
stance, judging human intelligence from an AI perspective. If so, 
would we be forced to recognize the inferiority of human intelligence? 
 ‘Are we are no longer the most 
intelligent entities in the Universe?’ 
 As such, should we not challenge the anthropocentric approach 
that has dominated the discourse of AI up until now, and argue 
   FIGURE  9.5 Ila Colombo, Bio Architecture (2023), generated using 
LookX. LookX is used to generate bio-architectural designs out of 
jewellery designs.         
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 238
instead for a ‘Second Copernican Revolution’.  107   If Nicolaus Copernicus 
had argued that the Earth is not the centre of the Universe, but rather 
the Earth rotates around the Sun, should we not now also adopt a 
similar position towards the centre of intelligence in the Universe? 
 Are human beings no longer the most intelligent entities in the 
Universe?  
   The future of intelligence  
 How will AI-generated architecture be perceived in the future? 
 Back in 2002, Bill Gates predicted that the ﬁ  rst decade of the third 
millennium would be known as ‘The Digital Decade’ in that, by the 
time that it would come to an end, ‘the impact of the digital realm 
would be so far-reaching that there would scarcely be any facet of 
human existence that would remain untouched by it’. 83  His prediction 
has proved largely accurate. 
 Similarly, back in 2010 it was also predicted that by 2020 we would 
stop using the term ‘digital’ in architectural design circles.  108   This 
would not be because we would no longer be using digital design 
tools, but for precisely the opposite reason – almost everyone would 
be using digital tools. For this very reason, the term itself would 
disappear. The digital would be everywhere and nowhere.  109   W e  
would therefore stop referring to ‘digital design’. This prediction has 
also proved largely accurate. In many aspects, ‘digital design’ has 
become simply ‘design’. 
 This is echoed in the way that we refer to drawings. Initially, when 
most drawings were drawn by hand, they were referred to as 
‘drawings’, and CAD drawings were called ‘digital drawings’. Once 
most drawings became CAD drawings, the term ‘drawing’ came to 
refer to CAD drawings, while the term ‘hand drawings’ came to refer 
to drawings drawn by hand. In other words, now that the tipping 
point has been reached when more drawings are produced 
computationally than drawn by hand, CAD drawings have become 
simply ‘drawings’. 
 A similar analogy could be made in relation to cars. In the very 
early days, when there were relatively few cars on the road, they 
were referred to as ‘horseless carriages’, and carriages drawn by 
THE FUTURE OF AI 239
horses were still referred to as ‘carriages’. However, once ‘horseless 
carriages’ came to outnumber ‘carriages’, then the situation changed, 
and ‘horseless carriages’ became known as ‘cars’, and carriages 
drawn by horses became known as a ‘horse-drawn carriages’. By 
extension, we could predict that once self-driving cars outnumber 
cars driven by humans, self-driving cars will become known simply as 
‘cars’ and what we now call ‘cars’ will become known as ‘human-
driven cars’. 
 And what about AI? Are we now in ‘The AI Decade’? And what 
might we predict about the way we will refer to AI by the end of this 
decade? At present, we distinguish between AI and human 
intelligence, and refer to human intelligence simply as ‘intelligence’. 
With time, however, as AI becomes increasingly prevalent and more 
powerful than human intelligence, we are likely to reach another 
tipping point. Could we predict that AI will simply be known 
as ‘intelligence’, and what we now call ‘intelligence’ will become 
known as ‘human intelligence’? And might we even begin to view 
‘human intelligence’ with a degree of sentimentality, just as we now 
view horse-drawn carriages, handwritten letters and hand-drawn 
drawings? 
 ‘The quest for AI is the quest 
for intelligence itself.’ 
 The irony about the term ‘artiﬁ  cial intelligence’ is that when John 
McCarthy originally coined it back in 1955, he and others did not really 
like it, but, as McCarthy points out, ‘I had to call it something, so I 
called it “ Artiﬁ  cial Intelligence” . ’  110   His goal, however, was to achieve 
 genuine   – rather than artiﬁ  cial – intelligence. This is still the case with 
many contemporary AI researchers. Besides, as Walsh observes, 
‘Putting the adjective  artiﬁ  cial   in front of anything never sounds very 
good. ’  111   Stibel also claims that the word ‘artiﬁ  cial’ is misleading, 
causing people to associate AI with something fake or ‘less than’ 
intelligent. Instead, Stibel notes, what is needed is an artiﬁ  cial brain 
to create real intelligence, ‘much in the way that an artiﬁ  cial heart is 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 240
used to create a real heartbeat’.  112   Perhaps, then, a better term would 
have been ‘synthetic intelligence’. 
 Interestingly, many of the central ﬁ  gures in AI have been more 
concerned about understanding how the brain works. Turing, as we 
have already seen, had confessed that he was more interested in 
how the brain works than in the practical applications of computing.  113   
Likewise, Geoffrey Hinton, now a luminary within the world of AI, 
initially studied physiology for a particular reason: ‘I wanted to know 
how the brain worked. ’  114   Demis Hassabis, who holds a PhD in 
neuroscience and is CEO of DeepMind, does not refer to AI but simply 
to ‘ intelligent   systems ’ : ‘Our ambition in DeepMind is to build  intelligent   
systems to help ﬁ  nd solutions that can learn to solve any complex 
problem . . . Put another way, we want to use it to solve everything 
else. ’  115   
 The quest for AI is the quest for intelligence itself.  
   Architectural intelligence  
 The applications of AI and data-driven informational systems have 
now become so widespread throughout the domain of architecture 
that we should perhaps refer to them collectively as contributing to 
a form of ‘architectural intelligence’, an intelligent approach to 
architectural design that is fast becoming the dominant approach.  116   
In the future architecture will be intelligent. 
 Makoto sei Watanabe is probably the ﬁ  rst to use the term 
‘architectural intelligence’ to refer to a new type of architectural 
designer, the ‘ AI Tect’, exploring the potential of AI in architecture.  117   
Wanyu He has also coined a similar term, the ‘ AI-chitect’, to refer 
to a very similar kind of designer.  118   Let us not forget, of course, 
that Molly Wright Steenson also used the term in a narrower 
sense, to refer to four pioneers in the ﬁ  eld of computational 
architecture.  119   
 ‘In the future architecture 
will be intelligent.’ 
THE FUTURE OF AI 241
 In this context, however, ‘architectural intelligence’ is not limited 
to the application of AI. It could be used to refer to a new approach to 
design that embraces  all  forms of intelligence in architecture, such as 
‘structural intelligence’, ‘environmental intelligence’, ‘constructional 
intelligence’, ‘programmatic intelligence’ and so on. It is a new 
approach that has already been deﬁ  ned: ‘ A new movement is 
emerging. It is a movement that operates at the interface between 
advanced digital technologies and the built environment . . . We are 
calling this new movement “ architectural intelligence  ”.’   120   
 Nor is architectural intelligence limited to the digital. We can 
ﬁ  nd examples of ‘biological intelligence’ throughout nature. Indeed 
nature itself offers countless examples that can inspire a biological 
approach towards design. This informs the work of architects such 
as Achim Menges, Neri Oxman and Claudia Pasquero, who draw 
upon biomimicry and biomimetic principles. So, too, we can ﬁ  nd 
examples of ‘material intelligence’ throughout the natural kingdom. 
This could even be described as a form of ‘material computation’. The 
form of a sand dune, for example, is ‘computed’ through the force of 
wind and gravity on the particles of sand. Likewise, the form of a 
soap bubble is ‘computed’ through a combination of forces, such 
as internal and external pressure, surface tension and so on. This, 
in turn, informs the work of architects such as Antoni Gaud í  and 
Frei Otto, who draw upon material behaviours and morphogenetic 
principles.  121   
 Buildings, of course, are material, whereas computation is 
immaterial. However, we can now use intelligent computational 
techniques, such as AI, to inform the intelligent design of a material 
building and make our designs ever more materially intelligent. 
Architectural intelligence therefore stands not only for the intelligent 
use of materials and the use of ‘intelligent materials’ in the 
construction of a building, but also for the use of intelligent 
computational techniques to design the material form of that building. 
 How might we refer to ‘architectural intelligence’ in the future, 
once the various forms of intelligence – structural intelligence, 
environmental intelligence, material intelligence, programmatic 
intelligence and so on – have become increasingly prevalent in 
architectural circles? Could we predict, perhaps, that once every 
aspect of design has become intelligent, the term ‘intelligence’ would 
ARCHITECTURE IN THE AGE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE 242
cancel itself out? Would references to ‘intelligence’ disappear? If so, 
all that we would be left with would simply be ‘architecture’.  
   AI and the future of architecture  
 We have already seen some opposition to the introduction of AI into 
architecture, just as there was opposition to the introduction of 
computation before the turn of the millennium. Within academia 
especially, we could imagine that AI might also be banned from some 
architectural studios, just as computers were once banned in certain 
schools of architecture, and ChatGPT has been banned – but often 
reinstated – in certain schools and even countries.  122   Indeed, the key 
question is not whether academia should  adopt   AI, as clearly it has 
little choice, but how much it needs to  adapt to   AI. Even more than 
the profession, it is clear that academia will be utterly disrupted by AI. 
Will we need so many professors in the future? Does architectural 
education need to be so long, once so many tasks will be fulﬁ  lled 
by AI? 
 On the whole, however, opposition to AI is likely to fade away over 
time, as happened with the initial opposition to computation itself. 
Nonetheless, some might deliberately opt not to use AI, even though 
it will obviously prove more convenient, just as some hipsters today 
deliberately choose to ride bicycles instead of driving cars, as a 
lifestyle choice. Clearly, AI will not be for everyone. 
 It is the example of the self-driving car, however, that might shed 
some light on the full implications of introducing AI into architecture. 
Christensen, as we have noted, compares the introduction of AI into 
the architectural ofﬁ  ce with the development of the self-driving car. 
There is, however, a problem with this analogy. 
 AI, of course, will make life easier, just like self-driving cars. With 
self-driving cars, however, the driver eventually becomes redundant. 
If we adopt the same model for architecture, would this not mean 
that eventually the architect would also become redundant? Rather 
than striving for the equivalent of the self-driving car in architecture, 
should we perhaps keep to the model that Wolf Prix suggests, with 
the architects in the front seats, and AI in the back seats? 
THE FUTURE OF AI 243
 Or is it already too late to prevent these developments? Might the 
architect also disappear, just as the human driver will disappear? And 
might we not notice or even care? 
 Let us ﬁ  nish, then, with a series of predictions about the future 
impact of AI on architecture, based on comments made in 
this volume. These are predictions as to what will be technically 
possible – although not perhaps implemented universally – by the 
end of the decade: 
     1   AI will become an indispensable assistant.  
    2   Design will be driven by data.  
    3   AI will automate the design process.  
    4   AI will optimise designs.  
    5   Buildings will become intelligent.  
    6   Cities will become intelligent.  
    7   Clients will expect their architects to use AI.  
    8   Economics will be the main driver of change.  
    9   The architectural profession will be disrupted.  
    1 0   AI will be able to design a building completely autonomously.   
 Welcome to architecture in the age of AI!

    2 0   Mode collapse occurs when the generator learns to generate only 
one plausible output that the discriminator thinks is ‘real’, and it will 
not generate a wide variety of output images. As a result, it 
captures only one mode of visual information in the training data. 
The discriminator will eventually learn to always reject this output, 
but then the generator will slightly modify its output to beat the 
discriminator, causing the network to become trapped and never 
learn anything new.   
    2 1   Instead CycleGAN will learn translations from dataset A to dataset 
B, but it will have to prove that it is able to reconstruct the 
translation from dataset B to dataset A.

conservative approaches to creativity, such as that of Martin 
Heidegger, need to be reconsidered. For example, Heidegger’s 
notion that the work of art is linked to the Greek term ‘ aletheia  ’, 
which means ‘not forgetting’. In other words, in producing the work 
of art the ‘artist’ merely uncovers – or ‘remembers’ – an already 
existing ‘truth’. On this, see Leach,  Rethinking Architecture  , pp.

    5 8   ‘Now the concept of deconstruction itself resembles an architectural 
metaphor . . . It is not simply the technique of an architect who 
knows how to deconstruct what has been constructed, but a 
probing which touches upon the technique itself, upon the authority 
of the architectural metaphor and thereby constitutes its own 
architectural rhetoric. Deconstruction is not simply – as its name 
seems to indicate – the technique of a reversed construction when 
it is able to conceive for itself the idea of construction. One could 
say that there is nothing more architectural than deconstruction but 
also nothing less architectural. Architectural thinking can only be 
deconstructive in the following sense: as an attempt to visualize that 
which establishes the authority of the architectural concatenation in 
philosophy. ’ Jacques Derrida, ‘ Architecture Where the Desire May 
Live’, in Leach,  Rethinking Architecture  , pp. 302–3.

   Chapter 6  
     1   In 2019, there was so little awareness of the potential contribution 
of AI to architecture that a bid to curate the British Pavilion at the 
NOTES 279
2020 Venice Biennale on the theme of Architecture and AI failed to 
be even shortlisted. This is all the more remarkable, as it highlighted 
the undeniably important role of British ﬁ  gures, such as Alan Turing, 
Ada Lovelace and Charles Babbage, in promoting computation in the 
ﬁ  rst place. It also involved a ‘dream team’ including myself, ZHA, 
Jane Burry, Reﬁ  k Anadol, Jane Burry and others, and had the 
ﬁ  nancial support of Google.   
    2   ‘ Artiﬁ  cial intelligence is up to you to seek a part in it. I am happy to 
not be alive when that happens, but I am worried about my kids and 
questioning having a hand in it. ’ Interview with Wolf Prix, ‘Forward 
Slash’,  Wash   1,   https://washmagazine.com/Interview-Wolf-D-Prix  .   
    3   ‘ All computer programs are closed systems. In closed systems there 
is no trial and error. There is only one way: the “PARAPATRIK” way. 
I call him [Patrik Schumacher] PARAPATRIK. I really appreciate his 
work, but his use of rules reminds me of a closed system. There is 
no variety. ’ Interview with Wolf Prix, ‘Forward Slash’.   
    4   Indeed many of Prix’s early experimental drawings sought to draw 
upon the unconscious as a mechanism to unleash repressed ideas. 
It is perhaps with the unleashing of the ‘unconscious’ – much like 
Reﬁ  k Anadol’s WDCH projection – that we can glimpse the potential 
of AI to open up architecture to new forms of expression.      Peter  
 N o e v e r    ,    Architecture in Transition: Between Deconstruction and New 
Modernism   ,   Munich  :  Prestel ,  1991  .

with three versions of a decree issued in Egypt in 196  BC . One

Egyptian Demotic script and one in ancient Greek script. By 
comparing these three scripts, researchers were able to decode 
ancient Egyptian hieroglyphic script for the ﬁ  rst time.      Richard

    4 5   Some glimpse of the potential of XKool’s radical data-driven vision 
for architecture can already be found in the maritime world – driven 
initially by the technologically advanced, cut-throat world of 
America’s Cup yachting. In 2021, Emirates Team New Zealand used 
reinforcement learning to speed up the testing process of various 
designs, giving it a competitive advantage that allowed it to 
eventually win the competition. This technological know-how has 
now begun to spill into the domestic world of yachting, and even 
into commercial tankers through AI-driven marine engineering 
companies such as BAR Technologies, who market themselves in 
‘bringing racing pedigree to commercial marine’. What is remarkable 
about the design work of BAR Technologies is that they no longer 
employ any drawing whatsoever in the design process,   https://
www.bartechnologies.uk/  .

    5 5   Lessons can also be learnt from IKEA. After all, IKEA has 
succeeded, where other commercial design initiatives have failed.

failed to win over customers. By comparison, IKEA reduces costs 
by ﬂ  at-packing its products, making them easy to assemble, and 
leaving fabrication to the customer. For most customers, the fact 
that most IKEA products look stylish might well be only a 
secondary concern.

    5 0   The term ‘homeostasis’ was coined by the American physiologist, 
Walter Bradford Cannon (Cannon, 1929), in reference to living 
systems. Signiﬁ  cantly, Cannon chose the ancient Greek term 
‘homeo’ (meaning ‘similar’) over the alternative ancient Greek term 
‘homo’ (meaning ‘the same’). By this Cannon sought to distinguish 
human operations with their considerable variables from mechanical 
operations, such as in the case of the thermostat, that operate 
within a ﬁ  xed system.

when feedback is quick, the rate is increased. It turns out that the 
brain employs a similar “algorithm” based on a feedback 
NOTES 293
mechanism, involving “long term potentiation” and “long term

 Leach    and    Xu   Weiguo    (eds),   Design Intelligence: Advanced 
Computational Techniques for Architecture   ,   Beijing  :  CABP  ,  2010 , p. 
 8   . This is certainly now true in terms of drawings.   
    1 0 9   This argument comes from Jean Baudrillard, who contends that 
once everything becomes aesthetic, the word ‘art’ becomes 
meaningless: ‘When everything becomes aesthetic, nothing is 
either beautiful or ugly any longer, and art itself disappears. ’ Jean 
Baudrillard, ‘Transpolitics, Transsexuality, Transaesthetics’, trans. 
Michael Valentin, in      William   Stearns    and    William   Chaloupka    (eds), 
  The Disappearance of Art and Politics   ,   London  :  Macmillan ,  1992 , p. 
 10   . But the argument could also be applied to other ﬁ  elds: ‘When 
everything becomes historical, nothing is historical any more. 
When everything becomes political, nothing is political any more. 
And likewise when everything becomes aesthetic, nothing is 
aesthetic any more. We live in a transhistorical, transpolitical and 
transaesthetic world. As each category grows swollen and 
distended, a condition of obesity exists, and the contents are 
obliterated. Everything has been xeroxised into inﬁ  nity. In this 
ecstasy of replication, this society of saturation, events have been 
NOTES 301
lost in the void of information, deprived of all meaning. ’      Neil   Leach   , 
  Millennium Culture   ,   London  :  Ellipsis ,  1999 , p.  24   .   
    1 1 0   McCarthy adds, ‘I had a vague feeling that I’d heard the phrase 
before, but in all these years I have never been able to track it 
down. ’ N. J. Nielson, transcript of ‘Oral History of John McCarthy’, 
interview by Nils Nilsson on 12 September 2007 at and for the 
Computer History Museum, Mountain View, CA,   http://www. 
nasonline.org/publications/biographical-memoirs/memoir-pdfs/ 
mccarthy-john.pdf  ; reprinted in      N. J.   Nielson   ,   John McCarthy: A 
Biographical Memoir   ,   Washington ,  DC  :  National Academy of 
Sciences ,  2012   .
